HEADLINE: Couple Who Stirred Issue of Same-Sex Marriage Still Hopeful

BYLINE: By CAREY GOLDBERG 

DATELINE: HONOLULU, July 26

 BODY:
Having fallen in love at first sight at a disco dance lesson and spent 19 inseparable years from that very moment, Joseph Melillo and Patrick Lagon tend to believe in kismet.
But the surprising tale of how their simple desire to wed turned into a national election-year obsession -- and brought their mellow, orchid-strewn state willy-nilly to the verge of a social revolution -- would test anyone's belief in coincidence. 
 Mr. Melillo said that when he and Mr. Lagon decided to wed in 1990, they thought they might be able to walk in, apply for a marriage license, and sign it.
"We were just two guys in love who wanted to get married," Mr. Melillo recalled, speaking over the articulate squawks of a cockatoo named Peewee in the office of the silk-screening company he and Mr. Lagon own. "It's unfortunate it's all gotten so political."
It has indeed become political: the House of Representatives overwhelmingly approved the pre-emptive Defense of Marriage Act defining marriage as a union between a man and a woman, on July 12. It has also become legal: the next round in a Hawaii trial court comes on Sept. 1, and is expected to leave backers of same-sex marriage here just one step short of victory.
But though the would-be spouses, their lawyer and even state officials are lightly amazed at the unexpected political snowball they started, they can also see a certain logic as to why it began here.
Hawaii, with its potpourri of cultures, is also known for its tolerance, and although the Legislature has rejected proposals to create a "domestic partnership" setup in lieu of same-sex marriage, most of the sulfuric, Sodom-and-Gomorrah warnings have come from the mainland.
"It's the aloha spirit -- there's such tolerance here," Mr. Melillo said.
The state is politically liberal as well, ruled by Democrats in a virtual one-party system, and was one of the first states to legalize abortion and pass the Equal Rights Amendment.
Despite all that, it has been no easy road for the would-be spouses and their lawyer, Daniel Foley, who took the case for Mr. Melillo, Mr. Lagon and two lesbian couples more out of sympathy than any hope of winning.
Until the state Supreme Court ruled partly in their favor in May 1993, they had little attention and support from gay groups, which were busy fighting other battles and divided on the issue of gay marriage. Some gay-rights advocates still argue that the campaign to legalize such unions diverts attention from more important issues, like AIDS.
Nor did civil rights groups, like the American Civil Liberties Union, support them before the victory. The couples did much of the fund-raising for their lawyers' fees themselves, at dinners and barbecues.
That changed after the 1993 decision, in which the Hawaii Supreme Court found that the ban on same-sex marriage violated the due process clause in the state constitution and sent the case back to the trial court for the state to justify itself.
Now the Lambda Legal Defense and Education Fund, the premier gay legal organization, is helping with the legal work, and the Hawaii Equal Rights Marriage Project, which raises money for the case, has backers around the country.
A pivotal factor in the 1993 decision, Mr. Foley believes, was a change in the Hawaiian Supreme Court. When he began the case in 1990, he said, "The court was five men approaching 70; from that time through '93 you had a changing of the guard and you had a baby boomer court with one woman, and that was a major difference."
Although controversy has swirled around the case, the lives of Mr. Melillo and Mr. Lagon show little sign of the broad impact their engagement has had. Unlike Ninia Baehr and Genora Dancel, a photogenic lesbian couple suing with them, they are invited to do virtually no public speaking or fund-raiser schmoozing these days, Mr. Melillo said.
Rather, their days are deeply domestic, filled with concerns about whether their T-shirt silk-screening company will survive the current downturn in the Hawaiian economy, getting rid of the termites eating their suburban house not far from Pearl Harbor and getting their photocopying machine fixed. Relaxed, warm and a touch campy, they are kept busier than they would like already, with their silk-screening company and other creative and commercial endeavors, which range from catering -- Mr. Melillo is a professional chef -- to art -- Mr. Lagon is a talented stylist in ink and watercolor -- to designing and sewing costumes for Waikiki Beach hotel shows, arranging flowers and fashioning leis from the bounty of the blooming trees gracing their house.
But every once in a while, the case kicks up again.
That is expected to happen in September, when, after many months of delay, a trial court judge is scheduled to hear a State Deputy Attorney General defend the state law. The Attorney General's office must prove there is a "compelling state interest" for same-sex marriage to remain banned.
"That burden is very hard to meet," said Mr. Foley, a civil rights specialist who helped Micronesia fashion its Constitution. "That's why everyone is predicting I'll win."
Even if he does win, Mr. Foley said, the judge will almost surely stay the decision pending appeal, because if same-sex couples started marrying and then the decision was overturned, the legal ramifications would be complex. Mr. Foley expects the circuit court decision by November, the state appeal to the State Supreme Court in December.
Concern that a decision to legalize same-sex marriages would result in gay couples from the mainland traveling to Hawaii to wed has led conservatives in at least 19 states to seek new state laws denying recognition to such unions. Without specific laws, states would be obliged under the United States Constitution to recognize same-sex marriages performed in another state.
Similar worries led to the Defense of Marriage Act, which awaits action in the Senate and which President Clinton has said he would sign. The act does not outlaw same-sex unions, but by withholding Federal tax, pension, health and other benefits would deny gay couples many of the civil advantages of marriage. It would also allow all states to ignore same-sex marriages performed elsewhere.
The hubbub around the Defense of Marriage Bill, and similar storms over the issue in the Hawaii Legislature for the last two years, puzzles Mr. Melillo: "They have nothing better to do than decide if two guys or two girls can get married?" he asked.
Hawaii's Attorney General, Margery Bronster, has found herself under pressure from conservative groups to use attacks against homosexuality in the state's defense. Her office has said it would not take any "homophobic" tacks; its main argument, Ms. Bronster said, concerns children as the compelling state interest. "All things being equal, the best surroundings for a child is within a family with a father and a mother," she said.
"It's not saying you can only get married if you have children," she added, "but it's promoting a family where you'd have, in the best circumstances, a mother, a father and children, and that's reflected in our law in many ways."
So far. Mr. Melillo, 48, said he and Mr. Lagon, 39, are confident of winning eventually and even dream of having one of the Supreme Court justices marry them.
"I'm in the background planning the wedding," said Mr. Lagon, who was born in Hawaii to a Filipino family. Mr. Melillo is originally from Summit, N.J.; both came from strong Catholic families, and say their relatives support them fully.
After years of waiting, they plan to marry the very day it becomes legal, even if that means complications planning the large reception they want. And if and when they are truly married, Mr. Melillo said, they could get serious about adopting children.
"I'm the one who keeps saying: 'When? When can we? Will we?' " he said. "We're getting older and let's have someone in our lives besides us and our dogs."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Joseph Melillo, left, and Patrick Lagon say they never intended to create a political storm when, several years ago, they decided to try to marry. But their request has brought a national debate over same-sex marriage. (Jim Wilson/The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Miami Fetes 100 Years of Hyperbole

BYLINE: By The New York Times 

DATELINE: MIAMI, July 26

 BODY:
Henry Flagler had no intention of building anything here, where the Miami River pours into Biscayne Bay. But the 19th-century railroad tycoon, responsible for much of South Florida's early development, changed his plans after meeting Julia Tuttle, a local landowner bearing an orange blossom and big dreams for a new American city.
Less than one year after Mrs. Tuttle's public relations gambit, according to Miami's official history, the city of Miami was founded on a steamy July day in 1896. This Sunday, Miami celebrates its founding with a centennial birthday festival along the bay. 
 Mrs. Tuttle's orange blossom, as it turned out, was an effective way to grab Mr. Flagler's attention in 1895. That season's citrus crop, which was primarily harvested in North Florida then, had been wiped out by unseasonably cold weather. Mrs. Tuttle, who owned 640 acres on the bay, delivered a simple, symbolic message with that blossom: Miami was warm, and its sunshine was reliable.
She promised the industrialist that he could have half of her property if he agreed to build a hotel on the site, as well as a railroad terminus. Mr. Flagler, recognizing that the weather and the location of Mrs. Tuttle's land made it a fine candidate for development and agriculture, immediately set to work.
"Miami will eventually be one of the greatest and most important cities, financially, commercially and residential, as well as the most important deep-water port in all the southland," Mrs. Tuttle said in 1896.
Arva Moore Parks, a Miami native who wrote the city's official history, "Miami: The Magic City," is quick to remind the uninitiated that Miami was the first major United States city founded by a woman.
Mrs. Parks, 57, is co-chairwoman of Miami Centennial '96, the committee planning the city's birthday celebration in Biscayne Park, less than a mile from where Mr. Flagler's Royal Palm Hotel once stood; the site is now home to a parking lot. Mrs. Parks likes to say that Mrs. Tuttle was a true visionary. But she is quick to add that hyperbole has always played a role in Miami's history.
"I don't think we've stopped since Julia Tuttle," Mrs. Parks said. "We have a history of hype. One thing you have to realize is, up until 20 years ago, our main economy was tourism. We had to advertise to bring people down. We were anxious to grow."
That eagerness spawned countless slogans and advertising campaigns, designed to attract people to Miami. An abundance of sun and surf images color the city's public relations gestures and have succeeded in bringing people tired of cold winters to a city that calls itself the Capital of the Americas, a title that can be claimed now, as opposed to just 10 years ago, without hyperbole, Mrs. Parks said.
"We've really become the brain trust for Latin America," she said. "The lawyers, accountants, many professionals from Latin America, are trained here. Parents send their children here for their education. It's bigger now, more cosmopolitan. We were more talk than reality 10 years ago."
In fact, Mrs. Tuttle's grandest predictions have already come to pass, with Miami International Airport outranking all other United States airports in terms of international cargo traffic and ranking second in the nation in international passenger loads. The Port of Miami handles more passengers per year than any other port in the nation, largely on the strength of cruise lines that operate here.

HEADLINE: Cuba Strong Despite U.S. Law, Castro Says

BYLINE: Reuters 

DATELINE: HAVANA, July 27

 BODY:
President Fidel Castro says that Cuba is recovering from economic recession and that this is the country's answer to a United States law aimed at curbing foreign investment on the island.
In his annual speech on the anniversary of a guerrilla attack he led on an army barracks in 1953, Mr. Castro rebuked President Clinton on Friday night for having signed into law measures aimed at tightening the American economic embargo against Cuba. Mr. Clinton's action on March 12 was the result of "political opportunism" and "constituted a lack of morality, a lack of ethics," Mr. Castro said. 
 In a speech in Holguin, 435 miles east of Havana, Mr. Castro cited figures released this week by the Government that said the country's gross domestic product had grown 9.6 percent in the first six months of the year.
He said this growth, achieved mainly through advances in sugar production, tourism and nickel mining, constituted an "Olympic medal" for the Cuban people and was a fitting response to the law, known as Helms-Burton, that Mr. Clinton signed in March.
Senator Jesse Helms and Representative Dan Burton were the main Republican sponsors of the law, which threatens sanctions against foreign investors in Cuba who are judged to be "trafficking" in property expropriated from United States citizens or from Cubans who later became United States citizens.

HEADLINE: In Canadians' Retort on Trade, Politics of the Absurd

BYLINE: By CLYDE H. FARNSWORTH 

DATELINE: TORONTO, July 27

 BODY:
They say they are speaking for 3 million Canadians whose Loyalist ancestors fled a revolutionary United States more than 200 years ago and who now want their family property back.
John Godfrey, a Liberal member of Parliament from Toronto, lays claim to an ancestral home in Carter's Grove, Va. Peter Milliken, a Liberal member from Kingston, Ont., has forebears who owned land in New York State's Mohawk Valley. 
 Standing proudly at Old Fort York, whose guns defended Toronto in the War of 1812 but were stilled by American amphibious forces who burned the fort in 1813, they say they want to get even with the United States for what it is doing to Canadians who do business with Cuba.
The two lawmakers announced on Wednesday that in the fall they will introduce a bill to do the same thing to recover property confiscated from Loyalists to the British crown as the Helms-Burton Law, passed this year, does to enable Americans to recover assets seized after the Cuban revolution of 1959.
"Our guns are old but they are big," said Mr. Godfrey. (In August 1814, in retaliation for the devastation at Fort York, the British burned Washington.)
Mr. Milliken added, "The idea is to point out that if you're going to use domestic law to interfere with business relations with foreign states, other people can do the same thing."
He said one constituent told him he had deeds for all of downtown Philadelphia. "If the bill passes," Mr. Milliken said with a grin, "he'll be a millionaire."
Grinning is what the two lawmakers did a lot of. This is not Government-sponsored legislation. It may elicit the sympathy of Prime Minister Jean Chretien, their party leader, who has bitterly assailed Helms-Burton -- as have European Union and Mexican leaders. But it will not have Mr. Chretien's support because of his reluctance to precipitate a real crisis with Canada's leading trade partner.
The bill will go in as one of the thousands of private member's bills that are introduced annually but rarely pass into law. Its sponsors' intention, they said, is to parody Helms-Burton with mirror-image provisions and to highlight what the Canadians insist is the absurdity of the American law.
Thus, under the bill, Canadians would be able to sue the current American owners of their Loyalist ancestors' property in Canadian courts. And corporate officers of American companies who engage in "trafficking" in confiscated Loyalist properties, as well as their spouses or minor children, would be barred from entering Canada.
But not everyone is laughing up here at the lawmakers' initiative.
Michael Bliss, a professor of history at the University of Toronto, said: "The whole point is kind of stupid because the United Empire Loyalists were compensated for their confiscated property. The loyalists actually did very well. I see all this as high silliness and grandstanding. Helms-Burton is a serious issue and should be treated seriously."
In 1802, after much debate, the United States and Britain signed a treaty settling all Loyalist claims. The United States paid a lump sum of 600,000 pounds, or $3 million. That was a lot of money in those days, nearly half the $7.9 million in the Federal budget.
Marc Thiessen, spokesman for Senator Jesse Helms, Republican of North Carolina, said he wanted to thank the two Canadian lawmakers for accepting the principle that someone whose property has been expropriated is entitled to compensation.
He added, "We encourage Godfrey and Milliken, if they feel inadequately compensated, to take it up with John Major" -- the Prime Minister of Britain.

HEADLINE: Safety Plan for Puerto Rican Projects

BYLINE: By ADAM CLYMER 

DATELINE: LUQUILLO, P.R.

 BODY:
The change that everyone talks about is safety. "You don't have to be afraid of a stray bullet," Luz D. Quinones, a resident of the Yuquiyu housing project, said recently.
Two and a half years ago, residents were afraid. 
 Five young people were gunned down in a drug-related massacre on the basketball court at the entrance Yuquiyu shares with the Cemi housing project.
A few days later the police and the National Guard moved in to patrol the two public housing complexes as part of Puerto Rico's anticrime "intervention program" in the projects.
The program has drawn criticism as a threat to civil liberties from a coalition of organizations that include the Human Rights Commission of the Puerto Rican Bar Association, which says that bringing the National Guard into housing projects is a dangerous precedent.
There is also skepticism among critics about whether crime has actually been curbed or just moved elsewhere. But politicians in cities that include Washington and Chicago have called for similar measures, and here in Luquillo, most of the 683 tenants in the two projects say they are pleased with the results.
So, surveys and interviews show, are those in the other public housing projects on the island where intervention programs have been established. The stationing of police officers and guardsmen at the projects is followed by the installation of walls, fences and permanent security guards to keep strangers out.
In the approximately three years the program has been in operation, it has reached about two-fifths of the public housing units in Puerto Rico.
The overwhelming tenants' reaction is relief. Before the stepped-up security, according to a recent poll of 1,097 residents of 14 of the 79 projects where the program is in effect, only 13 percent of residents considered themselves safe.
The poll, conducted by Prof. Elena Martinez of the University of Puerto Rico, found that 68 percent of the residents said security was better since the program started, and 71 percent said the general quality of life had improved.
Seventy-five percent said they encountered security personnel every day. There is no set period for replacing the police officers and guardsmen with project security personnel.
The survey found, in fact, that residents' attention had turned from security to need for jobs and sports facilities. To follow up the increased security, Government officials have moved to rejuvenate the projects with social services and community contacts. Many dangerous projects did without such contacts, ranging from visits by health service personnel to deliveries of fast food.
Gov. Pedro J. Rossello calls the intervention program "a process of re-empowering the residents." He said in a recent interview that project residents had higher self-esteem as a result of the program, quoting a letter from a resident of one project, who wrote, "Thanks, Governor, for letting everybody know we're people."
Carlos Vivoni, the Puerto Rican Secretary of Housing, said his department and 15 others worked together in the follow-up program on rehabilitation in what they call Quality of Life Councils, combining efforts to combat drug addiction, high school dropouts and unemployment.
Mr. Vivoni estimated that the crime rate in all the affected projects had dropped by 80 percent. At El Cemi and Yuquiyu, he said, 91 school dropouts had returned to classes, 24 drug addicts were under treatment, 30 children were participating in sports programs and 37 tenants had new jobs.
Across the island, a large share of the jobs has come from construction at the housing projects. About 100 tenants from all the projects have been hired to build the new fences and gates, said Carlos Pesquera, the Secretary of Transportation and Public Works.
Up beyond the Cemi basketball court sits one symbol of success, a new pizza stand managed by a resident. For three months Elena Martinez, a former factory worker who is no relation to Professor Martinez, has sold pizza slices for $1 and soft drinks for 50 cents, on a franchise from a local chain.
If business keeps up, she said, she hopes to get a cart that will enable her to move the business around the two projects.
Governor Rossello, who seems comfortably ahead in a race for re-election this fall, contended the heightened security measures were a critical part of a program that generally emphasizes self-reliance rather than dependence on government.
The housing department now contracts with private industry to manage the projects and is hoping to sell many public housing units to the residents as condominiums. The government plans to subsidize mortgages in this effort.
But the fences and gates at the public housing projects are central to safety, the Governor said. Luxury apartment buldings and wealthy communities have guards and often walls, he said, and the poor need the protection even more.
"You cannot allow only those communities that have the resources to protect themselves," Governor Rossello said.
Out on the court at Cemi, young men were playing basketball. "Now," said Rosael Ramos, a 41-year-old single mother, "you can let the children out of the apartment."

HEADLINE: THE CLINTON RECORD;
Interview With Clinton: Political Landscape

 BODY:
Following are excerpts from an interview with President Clinton conducted on July 8 with reporters and editors of The New York Times:
Q. If you look across the four years of your Administration, your first two years were your most activist ones, probably the ones where you had the most accomplishments. And yet, that was when your popularity was lowest and the public voted in the Republicans in Congress. The last two years, the Republicans have been in control of Congress, and yet your popularity has soared. What do you make of this strange situation? 

 A. Well, I think I would make maybe three points about that. First of all, in the first two years, I had to do some things that were controversial, particularly with specific interest groups, but the benefits were not apparent by Election Day '94.
I mean, remember what a hard fight we had to pass the deficit reduction plan in '93, with the various component parts of it, which included doubling the earned-income tax credit for families. It included a dramatic departure in the student loan program, as a real improvement in the student loan program, but the interest groups didn't like that. And the whole deficit reduction plan was roundly condemned by the Republicans, and they all voted against it.
But the full economic benefits of it, even though we had a good year in '93 and a good year in '94, were not manifest in the lives of ordinary people, didn't really begin until starting about a year ago, we began to see some sense that, well, hey, maybe this thing is really getting better. . . .
So I would say a lot of the positive things that were done -- I could make the same points in education, in the environment, in welfare, where we'd already done a lot of the waivers to give states the power to move people from welfare to work, right across the -- were not particularly manifest. Point 1.
Point 2, the last image people had of the President and the Congress was the failure of health care. And I think it sort of overshadowed and enshrouded the campaign -- I mean, the campaign of '94.
Then the third point I would make is that in '95 and '96, I have not been as active in Congress, although to be sure that some important legislation passed -- the most important things, I would say, are the telecommunications bill, the anti-terrorism bill, the lobby reform bill. . . .
But I have been -- I think the reason is that people for the first time had a clear sense of what the choices were between the Congressional Republican vision of how America should go into the 21st century and my vision. And, I think, in a funny way the contrast between the two approaches made it easier for people to kind of focus on what they thought and what they believed and how the American people identified with the differences in our approach on the environment, for example, or on what should happen in the Medicare program, or what our education policies ought to be, or just right across the board.
So I think that those are the three reasons. I also would say that you shouldn't judge -- and I say this to myself as well as you -- one of the things that I have learned in the last two years is that the President can do an awful lot of things by executive action, so that the sum total of the Presidency's impact on the American people at home is not just what happens in the Congress, it also involves what can be done by way of executive action and by way of creating partnerships with people throughout the country.
I don't know how many people -- I'll just give you just a couple of examples. We started this national movement to hook up all the schools to the Internet by the year 2000. And it's sort of taken on a life of its own, it's going like -- I tried to highlight communities that were doing curfews and school uniforms, and all we did was to put out documents from the Education Department or the Justice Department showing how to do this legally and not have any problems with it. And every day, another community calls us and does this. . . .
 
ON CHANGE
 
 Q. So you would reject the idea that the public somehow is allergic to change and that when you tried to bring about change, they turned on you, and when the Republicans tried to bring about change, they turned on them?
 
 A. No. No, I disagree. I would disagree with that. I also agree, however, that change is hard. The public is -- change is difficult, but it depends on what kind of change it was. The public -- you just have to say that it's going to be hard, and you have to put the choices out. But I believe -- I had a hard fight for Nafta, for example, and the public was more or less evenly divided on it. But after we did it, the public was more in favor of it than opposed to it. . . .
I think that -- on the other hand, I think that if you look at the health care issue, I think that I overestimated the extent to which a person elected with a minority of the votes in an environment that was complex, to say the least, could achieve a sweeping overhaul of the health care system when no previous President had been able to do it for decades and decades, and against the enormous amount of organizational effort and funding that was spent to convince the American people of something that was not accurate -- namely, that we wanted to have the Government take over the health care system or that we wanted to regulate it with a cumbersome bureaucracy -- neither of which was true, but I think they believed that. And I think people said, hey, that's not the kind of change we want.
 
 Q. You said that the ability of affecting major change with a minority of the votes was one of the problems. Isn't that not, in fact, going to be a problem for the foreseeable future in a three-way race?
 
 A. It's different now. For one thing, I'm a known quantity, Senator Dole's a known quantity; our two approaches are clearly known. And if third- or fourth- or fifth-party candidates get enough votes in the aggregate to pull the victor below a majority, it will still become apparent whether the policies, the approaches we want to take command the support of the majority of the America people or not. . . .
 
ON A SECOND TERM
 
 Q. Why don't we go from there to a second term? In terms of what you want to do specifically in a second term, you tend to focus on the unfinished agenda: Kennedy-Kassebaum, the balanced budget, the welfare bill. Beyond this unfinished agenda, what would you like to accomplish in the second term?
 
 A. Let me take just a minute or two to sort of say where I think the second term is in history. When I ran for President four years ago, I gave a series of speeches at Georgetown in 1991 which basically described where I thought our country was and where I thought it ought to go, moving to the 21st century. So I always viewed this project, if you will, as more than a four-year project.
My whole idea was trying to get our nation to buy into a vision of America at the dawn of the 21st century that -- basically a vision that said, No. 1, we will be the world's strongest force for peace and freedom and prosperity; No. 2, we will be a country, with all of our diversity and differences, that will still have the American dream alive for everybody who is willing to work for it; and No. 3, we will manage all the differences among Americans in a way that brings us together as a community instead of divides us, the way so many countries are being divided by race and ethnicity and religion today. That was my vision. . . .
And I felt -- my little three-word prescription for what the strategy to implement the vision was: create more opportunity, demand more responsibility and build a greater sense of community. And that's essentially what I have tried to do.
Now, if you look at where we are and where we ought to go . . . let's take the economy for one. I think what we have done as Americans, not just the President, and not just the President and the Federal Reserve Chairman, but the whole -- or the President and the Congress, or the economic planners -- but as Americans, what we have done in the last four years is put our economic house in order, restore growth and create opportunity. We have succeeded in reducing the deficit, expanding trade, targeting investment in critical areas, and we have over 10 million new jobs.
Now, what's missing in this picture? I think you have to ask yourself what's missing in this picture in the second term. What's missing in this picture is that not every American has the capacity to take advantage of the opportunities. Of these 10 million new jobs, I should point out, it is no longer true that most of them are part-time, lower-wage jobs. Two thirds of these jobs almost are in higher-wage job categories. But there is still a lot of stagnation and insecurity in the picture. So what's missing in this picture? How are we going to give all Americans who are responsible the opportunity to succeed? And I would argue that, therefore, this naturally leads us to the next set of initiatives. And I think that in my second term I would focus much, much more on educational opportunity and performance even than I did in the first term, although, as you know, we spent a lot of time in '93 and '94 passing the education programs for those two years.
And let me just give you some examples. I believe that we should make two years of education beyond high school the norm for everyone. That's why I proposed -- make the tax-credit proposal equal to almost every state's community college tuition -- I did at Princeton. I believe we should allow people of any age a deduction for the cost of college tuition, whether in four-year colleges, graduate schools or whatever. I think that's important. I believe we should pass what I have called the G.I. Bill for America's workers. . . .
I think we ought to have job skill grants to people that basically break down all these Government programs, 70 or more of them, just collapse them, and give people a grant and let them then purchase their job placement services and their job training, if they need it.
I believe we should pass the package of pension reforms that I have presented to the Congress so that someone in a small business or a self-employed person who may move from job to job can carry a pension around with him or her for a lifetime. And if you're unemployed for a while and you can't pay into it, you don't lose it. And if you change jobs from one small firm to another, you don't have to wait as you do now eight or nine months trying to transfer your 401(k) and do all that. I think we need to create a seamless web of opportunity for pension security there. Part of that, also, is allowing more people access to I.R.A.'s, which you can withdraw from for education, health care or other emergency purposes. But I think that's important.
The next thing I think it's important to do, if you assume Kennedy-Kassebaum passes -- let's assume that for the moment -- then I think we need to look at what we can do to try to find ways for people to hold on to health insurance if they have sustained periods of unemployment, if they outrun their ability to maintain their old health insurance policy because their employer is not obligated to pay anymore. In other words, Kennedy-Kassebaum will give you the right to keep it, but it wouldn't give you the co-pay from your employer for a long time. . . .
 
ON HIS ACHIEVEMENTS AND DISAPPOINTMENTS
 
 Q. You've answered a lot of your achievements. Are there any more you want to throw in, and what would you say your errors have been?
 
 A. You know, I don't want to spend a lot of time talking about that. I'm sure you've been briefed about them. I think if -- let me just say, I made a couple notes here. . . . I think that compared to the vision I laid out to the American people and the hopes that I had when I came in office, three and a half years into this great adventure, I think we have done what I thought we should do with good results with regard to the economy, fighting crime, welfare, education, the environment, trade, foreign policy and reinventing Government. We've actually reduced the Government more than I thought we could in the first three and a half years. And I want to reconfigure it more. . . .
I am disappointed that we did not do more in health care. We didn't provide the full middle-class tax cut that I mentioned -- I acknowledged that. And I think generally in the deficit-reduction effort, our country needs some more investment in some critical areas. I already said -- research, technology, education and the environment, I would like to do more. But I think that given the fact that we always live in an imperfect world, I think we made the right decision because getting the interest rates down and getting the economy going again was a precondition of having a lot of other good things happen.
 
ON WHITEWATER
 
 Q. You have had a stormy Administration. There continues be be this thing in the polls in which people say when asked the question, do you think that the President and his wife were fully forthcoming on all these issues that just keep coming up -- and I understand it must drive you crazy -- the answer is no. And the question, I guess, is why? And what can you do about that?
 
 A. Well, I don't know. I think that all I can do is to answer the questions that are asked and provide all the evidence that I've asked. I can say this. We've given more information on more people and more subjects I think than anybody ever has. And if you look at the D'Amato hearings, which I think is one of the reasons we haven't done more in this Congress is that there was more time spent in the hearings of D'Amato than on Medicare, Medicaid, welfare, the economy, education and all other subjects put together. By far. Look at what the evidence pointed out. The evidence adduced by them failed to have one single, solitary shred of evidence showing any wrongdoing by me. In fact, the evidence that they tried to adduce about my governorship showed high ethical conduct, even in dealing with people who had strongly supported me. And there was no evidence of wrongdoing by the First Lady. . . .
 
 Q. They still don't believe that you're telling the full truth.
 
 A. There's nothing I can do about that except to tell the truth. I think it has something to do with the way the issues have been presented to them. But if you start from Day 1 and you persist until the end in setting up an issue in which you say, we're going to ask a bunch of questions and once they're disproved, we won't bother to tell you that there was no evidence of them, we'll just ask a bunch of new questions and once those questions are answered, we won't bother to tell you those answers to those questions, we'll ask a bunch of new questions.
I mean, how many people in the United States do you think know what the Resolution Trust Corporation report said? Not very many. So all I can tell you is there's nothing I can do about that.
All I can tell you is I'm going to keep -- I'll cooperate with the people I'm supposed to cooperate with, I'll answer the questions I'm supposed to answer, but I believe in the end the American people will get it right. They normally do. I trust them. As the campaign goes along, if they have questions of me on this, I think that they'll ask them. But I also think people are -- a lot of people know that a whole lot of this has just been pure politics.
Now, I don't believe that about the F.B.I. files. That's a legitimate issue. And I say we are determined to cooperate, and I want to know what the truth was there myself. That's in a separate category. That's different. . . .
But the other business, I believe the American people know that there was a lot of politics in that and that they're interested in things which affect their lives and their future, and I'm fighting for them.
 
 Q. Have you personally tried to get to the bottom of the F.B.I. files things? Have you called in your top aides and said, "What do you know about this?"
 
 A. Let me tell you something. I have done everything that I thought I should do, consistent with also honoring the fact and the appearance that an investigation would go forward unhampered.
Ask Mr. Panetta and others. The last time we tried to do something like this, then what we did became the issue, you know. And so we have pledged to fully cooperate with them. We have encouraged them to get here in a hurry; let's get on the ground. What we have said is we'll put somebody with you and we'll do what we're supposed to do, but we want you here. . . . If somebody is here from the outside from the beginning, then we will be able to prove whatever needs to be proved.
Apart from that, I think the American people know there's lots of politics in the rest of this, and I think there's a discount factor there. But the most important thing to me is I just realized that the more time I spent on it and the more time I spent talking about it, the less time I would spend being President. And that I thought it was wrong, but I wasn't going to let it rob the American people of my Presidency. I was going to show up for work every day, put it out of my mind and go to work, except when I was required to actually respond to something. And that's what I've done. And as a result, I think we've been able to get quite a lot done here in the last three and a half years.
 
 Q. You're not just talking about the files, you're talking about the whole----
 
 A. The Whitewater process. . . . When you get elected President, you get four years, and every day those four years come closer to being elapsed. Every minute the President needs to be focused on -- I think literally every day, I think about what was the vision I had for this country when I came here: Is it still valid? Is our strategy working? What do we still need to do? Where have we failed? What do we need to do? What are we going to do in the next four years? Do I think I did a good job in the first four years? Yes, but that's what they hired me to do. And how are we going to build on it? And I know that the more I think about that and the less I think about anything else, the more likely I am to leave a legacy for the American people that is positive and good.
And so, what I try to do literally is just not even to think about the other things unless there is a specific issue where I can be helpful. If I can answer a question, if I can give information, if I can fulfill some specific responsibility, I try to do it. Otherwise, I try to just keep my eye on the ball. . . .
George just handed me a note. This is what it is. He just said you ought to point out that even though I said the F.B.I. was different, that does not mean that I think there's any evidence that anybody I know in a senior position did anything wrong.
 
ON FOREIGN POLICY
 
 Q. There's a lot that's happened.
 
 A. There's a lot that's happened, but the most important thing is we have to make foreign policy make sense to most ordinary Americans in a way that is not just episodic. You know, it kills us to see people dying in Bosnia at night, but why are we doing it in terms of the long term? But sometimes you just do things because they're right. There's something to be said for that -- Haiti or Bosnia or whatever, helping in Northern Ireland, Middle East. But also, how does it fit into this larger sense?
I think that's one point that is worth making because my view of this is that we had basically, you know, three big objectives. One was to finish the unfinished business of leaving the cold war behind. That's our relationship with Russia, our relationship with China, the North Korean nuclear problem and building a united Europe.
The second issue was dealing with the new security threats, ethnic hatreds and other kinds of internal bloodbaths. That's the Middle East, Bosnia, Northern Ireland, Haiti. Terrorism, which you know there's no point in my elaborating that. You know what we've done there. Drug-running, organized crime and these emerging environmental and health-related transnational threats.
And the third issue is building a new structure of opportunity and peace through trade investment and commerce. And that's Nafta, GATT, 200 trade agreements, Ron Brown's 10 emerging nations initiatives, what I did with APEC leaders and what we did with the summit of the Americas.
See, I think that the commerce thing, I think it's been wrongly positioned as are you choosing money over values. It seems to me that if America is in the center of these emerging networks, it dramatically increases our leverage to work with people for peace, for human rights and for stability in the world.
So when I think of the framework like that, that's how -- I'm trying to get to the point where I can explain to the American people, you know, how come we ought to pay our U.N. dues, why should we engage in peacekeeping missions, even though only 28 percent of the people thought I was right in Haiti and 38 percent in Bosnia or whatever the numbers were, why it really makes sense over the long run. Why did you help Mexico when people said 81 to 15 on the day you did it you were wrong? Why did you do it?
I think that it really is something that I think I need to take on even more is trying to figure out a way to make the American people believe not just episodically but instinctively that there's no longer an easy dividing line between foreign policy and domestic policy. That the world we're living in doesn't permit us that luxury anymore. And that we've got to break down that wall in our minds. . . .
I really try to think every day also about how all this looks to someone living in a medium-sized town in Ohio or California or Arkansas or anyplace else. You know, what it's like for them and how this can connect to their lives. That's what I -- if I have brought anything to the Presidency I think that is special, I think I do have a feel for how all these issues fit together and why. You know, fighting for the V-chip or regulating cigarette advertisements or trying to help communities adopt stiffer standards on truancy and curfews and all that, that's part of a security environment that's a piece of what we're trying to do with anti-terrorism and nonproliferation.
I try to see these connections, and I think that, slowly, I'm being able to communicate that to the American people in a way that resonates with them.
I hope that's true, and I believe it's true.

HEADLINE: BOMB AT THE OLYMPICS;
Heart Ailment Kills War Survivor in Altanta

BYLINE: By The New York Times 

DATELINE: ISTANBUL, July 27

 BODY:
Melih Uzunyol, who died in the Atlanta bombing today, was the chief cameraman of the state-owned Turkish Television. He had survived coverage of wars in Azerbaijan, Bosnia and the Persian Gulf but finally fell victim not to bullets, but to a weak heart, colleagues said today.
"As far as we know, he had a heart attack while running to cover the explosion and died on the way to hospital," said Orhan Tanakina, head of the Turkish Television Cameraman's Association. He had a history of heart problems. 
 He is survived by two children and his wife, Funda, an economics correspondent for the state-owned Anatolian News Agency.

LENGTH: 4475 words

HEADLINE: Despite His Reversals, Clinton Stays Centered

SERIES: THE CLINTON RECORD: The Overview -- First of seven articles.

BYLINE: By ALISON MITCHELL 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON

 BODY:
His hands in restless motion, Bill Clinton sat in the Oval Office earlier this month assessing the Presidency he calls "this great adventure."
At times pensive, other times fiery, his finger stabbing the air as he disputed a point, he confidently made his case for a second term. "As Americans, what we have done in the last four years is put our economic house in order, restore growth and create opportunity," Mr. Clinton said. "We have succeeded in reducing the deficit, expanding trade, targeting investment in critical areas, and we have over 10 million new jobs." 
 "Now, what's missing in this picture?" he asked during an interview earlier this month, adding, "What's missing in this picture is that not every American has the capacity to take advantage of the opportunities."
Mr. Clinton ticked off a raft of ideas to help. Tax credits for community college tuition. Guarantees that pensions can be taken from job to job. Vouchers for job training. Ways to give more people access to health insurance.
What was striking about the proposals was their modesty. They sounded so humble from a President who had once fought for a $30 billion jobs program and who had made such a bold stand to give all Americans "health care that can never be taken away, health care that is always there."
What a contrast from the grand visions of 1993, when Mr. Clinton swept into Washington for his inauguration from Thomas Jefferson's Monticello, repeating the third President's maxim that "to preserve the very foundations of our nation we would need dramatic change from time to time."
"Well, my fellow Americans," the 46-year-old President had said then, "this is our time. Let's embrace it."
But America, as it watched the early Clinton Presidency, did not embrace it. The very people whom Mr. Clinton had said he would serve -- "the hard-working Americans who make up our forgotten middle class" -- deserted his Democrats in droves or stayed home in the 1994 midterm elections, giving the Republicans control of Congress for the first time in half a century.
That election sent shudders through the White House and virtually split Mr. Clinton's Presidency into periods so distinct that his own advisers acknowledge that they could almost be viewed as two separate terms.
The first two years were marked by Government activism, culminating in Mr. Clinton's disastrous effort to carry out the largest expansion of social welfare since the New Deal: his overhaul of the health care system. That grand failure swamped Mr. Clinton's other domestic achievements and led to the victory of Republicans intent on contracting the Government that Democrats considered their legacy. It also effectively ended the bid by his wife, Hillary, to carve out an overt policy role as First Lady.
But over the next two years, the ever-resilient Mr. Clinton rose again. After the midterm Congressional elections largely relieved him of the burden of delivering on his promises, Mr. Clinton benefited from merely standing in counterpoint to the Republicans, while co-opting some of their more appealing imagery.
With the help of Dick Morris, the adviser who brought Mr. Clinton back from defeat as Arkansas Governor more than a decade earlier, the President expropriated the language of values from the Republicans. At the same time, he blocked their most ambitious plans to reshape the Government.
Mr. Clinton has so successfully repositioned himself toward the center that the public perception of him, in the latest New York Times poll, has shifted from liberal to moderate. Yet as the election approaches, some voters still wonder which Bill Clinton would prevail in a second term. Most of those polled say they do not know enough about the President's stands to predict what he would do if re-elected.
The New York Times Poll was conducted by telephone from June 20 to 23 with 1,121 adults throughout the United States. In theory, in 19 of 20 cases, results based on such samples would differ by no more than three percentage points in either direction if all American adults had been surveyed.
Republicans like to say that, unhampered by the strictures of another re-election campaign, Mr. Clinton would re-emerge as the liberal he seemed in his first two years. The President's defenders say he is the same mainstream, centrist New Democrat the country elected four years ago.
Whichever direction he would take, Mr. Clinton these days seems more comfortable in office and more disciplined in his speech and public appearances. Only rarely does he slip back into the improvisational excesses and indecision of his first chaotic year. The former Vietnam War protester whose interest in foreign affairs had been only fleeting, his course unsteady, now revels in his role as Commander in Chief.
He is far more popular than he was at his nadir, with an approval rating of 53 percent -- among his best ever -- in the Times poll, taken June 20 to 23. And he is widely credited with having made real efforts to improve the economy, cut the deficit, preserve Medicare, enact universal health care, fight crime and protect the environment.
Still, reservations persist about Mr. Clinton's character. A majority of those polled say they believe that Mr. Clinton and his wife are hiding something in the controversies known as Whitewater.
Pondering the loss that led to his political revival, Mr. Clinton said the benefits of programs like his 1993 deficit-reduction plan and the anti-crime bill "were not manifest in the lives of ordinary people" by the midterm election. The failure of his health care initiative, he added, "overshadowed and enshrouded the campaign."

The Change President
Great Ambitions Quickly Deflated
Mr. Clinton acknowledged that he had overestimated the extent of change that a President elected without a majority could undertake. But he also conceded that he had gained from the comparison with the Republican-controlled Congress. "People for the first time had a clear sense of what the choices were between the Congressional Republican vision of how America should go into the 21st century and my vision," he said.
In fact, the Times poll indicates that a majority of Americans believes that Mr. Clinton has spent the past two years standing up to the Republicans rather than proposing new ideas. Yet 54 percent of those polled said that if the Republicans continued to control Congress, it would be better to re-elect Mr. Clinton, who promised in 1992 to end gridlock in Washington, than to elect a Republican.
As he faces the November test, Mr. Clinton now seems aware not just of the possibilities of his office but of its limits. With Democrats unlikely to win back control of both houses of Congress, he said, "One of the things I have learned in the last two years is that the President can do an awful lot of things by executive action, so that the sum total of the Presidency's impact on the American people at home is not just what happens in the Congress."
Bill Clinton's election ended a drought that had shut Democrats out of the White House for 12 years. The party's ambitions were enormous, and Mr. Clinton stoked the mood of expectation.
As a candidate, Mr. Clinton had made such a detailed list of promises that they filled a 200-page book, "Putting People First."
And he had not only campaigned on change, he personified it. He was the youngest man since John F. Kennedy to be elected President. He was the first member of the post-World War II generation to win the nation's highest office.
But he and Hillary Rodham Clinton carried into the White House the polarization that has always come with their generation: controversy over whether someone who had avoided service in Vietnam could be Commander in Chief and ambivalence about the changing role of women, caused by the Clintons' "buy one, get one free" refrain.
Looking back, many of Mr. Clinton's aides say that the President overreached.
"There was a misreading of how much change people wanted," said Harold M. Ickes, the deputy chief of staff, adding, "There was this rhetoric of change, change, change; this was the 'Change President.' We're going through volatile scary times and on the one hand people want change. On the other hand they are nervous and afraid of change."
That mixed message could have been read in the election results. While nearly two-thirds of the nation had voted against Mr. Bush in a three-way Presidential race, Mr. Clinton had garnered only 43 percent of the popular vote, a figure he now concedes may have doomed his health care plan.
"I think that I overestimated the extent to which a person elected with a minority of the votes in an environment that was complex, to say the least, could achieve a sweeping overhaul of the health care system," he said in the interview.
Nor had Mr. Clinton provided any coattails for his party. Democrats retained control of Congress, but they picked up no seats in the Senate and lost 10 seats in the House in 1992.
"What that would have suggested," said Michael R. Beschloss, a historian of the Presidency, "was that he was to some degree a provisional President, someone Americans elected but that they had doubts about."
Newt Gingrich, the Republican Speaker of the House, said flatly, "What happened in '93, I believe, is they thought they won the election when in fact the Republicans lost the election."
Twenty-four years earlier, another President, Richard M. Nixon, won a similar minority victory and hewed carefully to the political center for his first years in office. Mr. Clinton, though, embarked on an enormously ambitious, activist agenda, and signaled -- intentionally or not -- that he was more left-leaning than the centrist "New Democrat" he had run as.
Some of his early problems stemmed from the speed with which he backed away from some campaign stands, making him appear to have no core principles. Mr. Clinton, for example, did not even wait until he was in office to say that he would maintain the Bush Administration's policy of forcibly returning Haitian refugees, even though he had condemned it during the campaign.
Mr. Clinton also quickly became overexposed in the press, and his tendency to muse aloud on any issue sent conflicting messages. He nominated several flawed appointees for high-level jobs then dropped them as they became too controversial, adding to the impression of temporizing. No one reined in the President's propensity to stretch meetings into rambling policy seminars.
"If everything is important, then nothing's important," Dee Dee Myers, Mr. Clinton's former press secretary, said recently. "The White House lost sight of what the priorities were."
Far from everything Mr. Clinton did in his first two years in office could be characterized as liberal orthodoxy. He pleased Wall Street by pushing a package of spending cuts and tax increases to help reduce the Federal budget deficit. He bucked organized labor to push through the North American Free Trade Agreement, an ambitious accord initiated by his Republican predecessor, George Bush. And on crime, another issue historically dominated by Republicans, he put through a measure that banned 19 kinds of assault weapons and promised to put 100,000 new police officers on the streets.
Still, in fits and starts, the Administration communicated a leftward drift.
In his first week in office, Mr. Clinton sought to fulfill his pledge to remove the ban on homosexuals in the military, putting himself on the cultural left and at odds with the military. After a politically costly public battle, he eventually had to settle on a compromise that left the prohibition in place but said that members of the armed services should not be asked about their sexual orientation. This "don't ask, don't tell" measure satisfied no one and especially alienated more conservative voters in Mr. Clinton's native South.
On economic policy, Mr. Clinton quickly confronted the fact that the huge deficit he inherited and his desire to lower it precluded him from fulfilling campaign promises to offer a tax cut to the middle class and increase spending on education, job training, public works and health care.
He failed to persuade Congress to pass a $30 billion economic stimulus package to counter some of the budget cuts. With Republicans voting no as a block, he barely won approval for his deficit reduction plan. That plan included a tax increase that, while largely aimed at the wealthy, also hit many high-income Social Security beneficiaries and raised the gas tax by 4.3 cents a gallon.
On social policy, Mr. Clinton put aside his signature campaign pledge to "end welfare as we know it" and instead made the health care overhaul his domestic policy flagship, placing Mrs. Clinton in charge.
Even many Administration officials now admit that Mrs. Clinton had a tin ear for the politics of Washington, alienating crucial allies. The measure produced seemed the very symbol of too much government: a mammoth 240,000-word bill.
As time went on, more Americans feared that rather than make health care more accessible and affordable, the Government would limit their choices. "We lost because we didn't reassure people with insurance that they wouldn't have less than they have now," one Administration official said. Today, the number of Americans without health insurance has risen to 40 million, from 37 million when Mr. Clinton took office.
In this chaotic period, the ethical morass known as Whitewater bubbled up again. No longer just an inquiry focused solely on whether Mr. Clinton had given favorable treatment as Governor to his partners in the Whitewater development project, the affair spread into related investigations.
Several associates have since been convicted of fraud, and others, including the Clintons, are staggering under legal bills. The affair has also cost the Clintons some of the friends who came from Arkansas determined to set Washington on fire.
Webster L. Hubbell, a former law partner of Mrs. Clinton, resigned as No. 3 at the Justice Department and is in prison for bilking his law firm and former clients. Vincent W. Foster Jr., another former law partner, committed suicide, and Senate hearings looked into whether the White House had obstructed the investigation into his death.
The First Lady was subpoenaed by a grand jury earlier this year after her legal billing records, long sought by the independent counsel, showed up mysteriously in a White House family room. And Bruce R. Lindsey, a close adviser, was named an unindicted co-conspirator of bankers charged with illegally contributing to Mr. Clinton's 1990 campaign for Governor in exchange for patronage jobs.
Despite voters' doubts about whether he has been completely honest about Whitewater, Mr. Clinton said he would simply keep cooperating with the inquiries and have faith that Americans "will get it right."
"I just realized that the more time I spent on it and the more time I spent talking about it, the less time I would spend being President," he said, adding, "I wasn't going to let it rob the American people of my Presidency."
As he went into the Congressional elections, Mr. Clinton in fact had many achievements: a family-leave law, a waiting period for the purchase of handguns and initiatives like his national service program. As part of his deficit reduction plan, he had also put in place a major expansion of the earned-income tax credit, which provides tax relief for the working poor and which he viewed as a down payment on his pledge of a middle class tax cut. And he had won passage of the anti-crime legislation, which he now holds up on the stump as a major achievement.
But he had little to show the anxious middle class: not a health care overhaul, nor tax reductions nor a crackdown on welfare.
"The mandate he ran on was not necessarily the one he executed," said Stanley B. Greenberg, Mr. Clinton's pollster through 1994. "He ran as a cultural conservative and an economic populist. He was the first Democrat to talk about welfare reform and the death penalty." But, Mr. Greenberg added, "the cultural conservative side of Bill Clinton disappeared in the first two years."

Loss and Recovery
Republicans Meet The Comeback Kid
Three days after the Democratic rout of 1994, President Clinton groped to explain what went wrong. "Most everybody is for change in general and then against it in particular," he said ruefully in a speech at Georgetown University.
No longer quoting Jefferson, Mr. Clinton turned instead to Machiavelli. "It must be considered that there is nothing more difficult to carry out, nor more doubtful of success, nor dangerous to handle than to initiate a new order of things."
Yet looking back, one Clinton strategist calls the loss of Congress the key to the President's recovery. "The '94 election was a total blessing in disguise," he said. "If the President wins re-election" -- a virtual certainty, the official quickly added -- "it's in no small part due to our losing the Congress."
Mr. Clinton, his aides say, was shocked into disciplining himself. He began to delegate more. He learned to shorten and focus his speeches. He changed his schedule so he would have long periods to think.
While his White House staff remained largely in place, Mr. Clinton dismissed virtually his entire political team of consultants and pollsters. First surreptitiously, then openly, he turned to Dick Morris, the big-thinking consultant who had been the mastermind of his first political resurrection in Arkansas.
In many ways, the parallel with the 1980 loss is striking. As a very young first-term Governor, Mr. Clinton had pushed through an activist agenda far ahead of public opinion and found himself pushed out of office. Mr. Morris counseled him to moderate his course, and he won the governorship back.
Under the guidance of his long-ago counselor, who had drifted over the years to Republican candidates like Jesse Helms, Mr. Clinton began to move to recapture the center ground by co-opting much of the Republicans' spirit, if not their actual proposals.
After submitting a budget in early 1995 that envisioned $200 billion in deficits for years to come, Mr. Clinton, over the protests of most of his aides, embraced just a few months later the Republican call for a balanced budget. In months of negotiations, he agreed with the Republicans that the budget could be balanced over seven years, despite the cuts in domestic spending required.
Mr. Clinton staked out his own ground on social values, an arena the Democrats had long ceded to the Republicans. Early this year he started giving speeches on youth, schools and crime. Across the summer he has taken stands on school prayer, television violence and affirmative action, usually setting out a middle ground. And he has spoken ever more frequently of reshaping the welfare system.
What is instructive about many of Mr. Clinton's speeches is what they allow him not to do. By saying in a major address that the Constitution permitted personal religious expression in school, Mr. Clinton was able to defuse the push for a constitutional amendment on school prayer. By saying "mend it but don't end it," he had an answer to those opposing affirmative action.
By calling for a balanced budget, Mr. Clinton was able to turn the debate into a referendum on how to do it. That allowed him to stand firm against some of the most ambitious (Mr. Clinton would say extreme) aspects of the Republican budget plan: most notably, their attempts to restructure Medicaid and Medicare.
The President who had seemed so conciliatory, even malleable, to a Democratic Congress in the first two years that he never issued a veto, issued 15 in the past two years, forcing Republicans to rewrite some measures and stymieing others. Only once was he overridden.
At the other end of Pennsylvania Avenue, Mr. Gingrich offers a harsh assessment of Mr. Clinton's tactics in seeking to recover from his 1994 loss. He accuses him of deceit in practicing "one of the most interesting and potentially dangerous experiments ever undertaken by an American President" in order to recover from the '94 loss.
"They decided, I think beginning sometime in late August, early September of last year, that they could create a conscious strategy of falsehood," Mr. Gingrich said, speaking of his White House adversaries, "and drive it hard enough that they could pretend to be us while avoiding being us and thereby keep their allies on the left through the avoidance while gaining the moderate voters in the middle by the pretense."
White House officials and Congressional Democrats contend that the Republicans themselves saved Mr. Clinton from the dangers of a split with his liberal allies. They say the Republicans misplayed their hand by proposing changes and savings in Medicare that frightened the public, by forcing Government shutdowns to try to compel passage of their budget plans and by walking out of budget talks this past January.
Mr. Clinton's aides had been deeply divided over whether and when to make a budget deal. Mr. Morris desperately wanted the agreement, to deprive the Republicans of the issue in the coming campaign. But advisers with stronger Democratic ties feared that a bad deal would cause a rupture with the party, leaving Mr. Clinton isolated. In the end, the President never had to make the choice.

The Foreign Arena
Novice Happily Learns The World's a Stage
As his ability to carry out domestic policy waned, Mr. Clinton overcame early stumbles in the foreign arena and reveled in showcasing his Presidency on the world stage.
The onetime Vietnam war protester had proved singularly inattentive to foreign policy initially and had seemed uncomfortable with his role as Commander in Chief. He lurched from crisis to crisis, from the slaughter of American soldiers in Somalia to the humiliating moment in Haiti when the transport ship Harlan County, sent to make way for the return of President Jean-Bertrand Aristide, turned around when faced with armed thugs on the docks of Port-au-Prince.
But his successful use of diplomacy, coupled with the threat of force, in Haiti in the autumn of 1994 let him restore Mr. Aristide to power and bolstered his confidence.
Still, it was almost another year before Mr. Clinton took decisive action in Bosnia, pushed by the new French President Jacques Chirac and by the Bosnian Serb offensive against Muslims in the so-called safe havens, which made a mockery of the United Nations peacekeeping mission.
When Mr. Clinton finally launched a new course in the summer of 1995, with air strikes against Bosnian Serbs, it produced the peace agreement that he announced at the White House. He then won tentative Congressional support to send 20,000 American troops to Bosnia -- a risky election-year deployment.
Mr. Clinton's critics say he has yet to articulate a compelling foreign policy for the post-cold-war era and too often makes decisions based on domestic politics. But he has learned to project himself and his country on the world stage: visiting troops in Bosnia, making a triumphant tour of Northern Ireland, signing a security accord in Japan or arranging a Middle East summit meeting.
As he concluded his interview, he had some last words about foreign policy. "There's a lot that's happened," he said, "but the most important thing is we have to make foreign policy make sense to most ordinary Americans."
He went on, "I really try to think every day also about how all this looks to someone living in a medium-sized town in Ohio or California or Arkansas or anyplace else."
"If I have brought anything to the Presidency I think that is special," he added, "I think I do have a feel for how all these issues fit together and why. You know, fighting for the V-chip or regulating cigarette advertisements or trying to help communities adopt stiffer standards on truancy and curfews and all that, that's part of a security environment that's a piece of what we're trying to do with anti-terrorism and nonproliferation."

Second Term?
A Lasting Enthusiasm For Governing
As he heads into the final months before the election, Mr. Clinton again faces the kind of three-way race that is likely to keep the victor from claiming a majority mandate.
Yet he insists that this election will not have the ambiguities of 1992.
"It's different now," he said. "For one thing, I'm a known quantity, Senator Dole's a known quantity; our two approaches are clearly known. And if third- or fourth- or fifth-party candidates get enough votes in the aggregate to pull the victor below a majority, it will still become apparent whether the policies, the approaches we want to take command the support of the majority."
Yet 52 percent of those questioned in the Times poll said they did not know enough to say what Mr. Clinton might do if he is re-elected. Even though Democrats in Congress have produced a platform of modest steps much like Mr. Clinton's, some Democrats worry that the President is not laying the groundwork for a successful second term.
Mr. Clinton likes to say that Republicans think "we're always better off without government unless we need it for national defense and one or two other things that we can't get any other way." His own view, he says, is "that we have to reform the government." He adds: "But I don't believe any country ever became great by deciding to give educational opportunity to just a few, or deciding to pollute the environment to advance the economy, or deciding we don't have common responsibilities to make safe streets for our children."
Mr. Clinton's support for erasing the deficit gives him little room for new spending, however. And even the most hopeful Democrats believe that they are unlikely to win both the House and Senate.
Asked if Mr. Clinton could feel content governing under such circumstances, Mr. Ickes said, "He loves governing. He loves government. He loves policy. Will he be content? No question about it. Will he be fulfilled? That remains to be seen."
Most of Mr. Clinton's aides believe that if he is re-elected, he will still be dogged by legal issues from the Whitewater inquiries. But the President presses on. "When you get elected President, you get four years," he said, "and every day those four years come closer to being elapsed. Every minute the President needs to be focused on -- I think literally every day, I think about what was the vision I had for this country when I came here: Is it still valid? Is our strategy working? What do we still need to do?"

Chronology: "Despite His Reversals, Clinton Keeps His Focus on Middle Ground"
 
--- 1993 ---
 
INAGUARATION
Jan. 20 -- William Jefferson Clinton is inaugurated the 42d president of the United States.

'NANNY GATE'
Jan. 22 -- Mr. Clinton withdraws Zoe Baird's nomination as Attorney General after learning she hired an illegal alien as domestic help. His second choice, Judge Kimba M. Wood, withdraws for similar reasons, and he appoints Janet Reno.

'DON'T ASK, DON'T TELL'
Jan. 29 -- Fulfilling a campaign promise, the President announces that homosexuals will no longer be discharged from the military. A storm of protest leads Mr. Clinton to announce a new policy, known as 'don't ask, don't tell,' in July.

WACO
April 19 -- After a 51-day standoff that began with an abortive raid on Branch Davidians in Waco, Tex., on Feb. 28, Federal agents assault their compound, starting a fire that kills 80.

BOSNIA
May 7 -- Secretary of State Warren Cristopher returns empty-handed after a trip to persuade the European allies to go along with President Clinton's proposal to lift the arms embargo to help the Bosnian Muslims and use limited NATO airstrikes against the Serbs.

'TRAVELGATE'
May 19 -- The White House fires the seven-person staff of the travel office, alleging financial mismanagement, and places a campaign aide in charge.
 
SUICIDE
July 20 -- Vincent Foster Jr., Mr. Clinton's deputy legal counsel and Hillary Rodham Clinton's former law partner in Little Rock, is found dead from a self-inflicted gunshot wound. His death becomes a subject of the Whitewater investigations.

DEFICIT REDUCTION
Aug. 5-6 -- Without the support of a single Republican, Congress passes Mr. Clinton's $500-billion deficit reduction plan, which cuts spending and raises income taxes on the wealthy.
 
MIDEAST PEACE
Sept. 13 -- Israel and the Palestine Liberation Organization sign a peace accord on the White House lawn.

HEALTH CARE REFORM
Sept. 22 -- After months of closed-door meetings between Hillary Rodham Clinton and hundreds of consultants, the President unveils a plan for universal health care -- a centerpiece of his domestic agenda.

SOMALIA
Oct. 3 -- Additional troops are sent to Somalia after 18 Americans die in a firefight between warlords.

HAITI REBUFF
Oct. 12 -- In a symbol of American military impotence, Mr. Clinton withdraws the USS Harlan County after Army-backed demonstrators in Haiti prevent the landing of 200 non-combat troops hoping to pave the way for the return of the Rev. Aristide.
 
NAFTA
Nov. 17 & 20 -- Congress passes legislation to implement the North American Free Trade Agreement.

--- 1994 ---
 
WHITEWATER
Jan. 20 -- Independent counsel is named to investigate the personal and political finances of the Clintons in the 1980s, including their role in the Whitewater Development Corporation.

SHAKE-UP
June 27 -- As part of an extensive White House shakeup, Mr. Clinton removes Thomas F. McLarty, his friend from childhood, as chief of staff, and replaces him with Leon E. Panetta, the budget director and former congressman.

HEALTH REFORM DEAD
Aug. 26 -- The White House and Congressional leaders all but concede that health care reform is dead for the year.

CRIME BILL
Sept. 13 -- Mr. Clinton signs a $30.2 billion anticrime package providing for 100,000 new police officers, expanding the death penalty and banning the sale of certain assault weapons.
 
TROOPS IN HAITI
Sept. 19 -- United States troops enter Haiti peacefully to enforce the return of the exiled President Aristide.

JAPAN
Oct. 1 -- Japan and the United States reach agreements designed to open domestic Japanese markets to foreign competition, averting the imposition of trade sanctions against Japan.

NORTH KOREA
Oct. 21 -- United States and North Korea sign agreement requiring that Pongyang dismantle its nuclear development program.

G.O.P. SWEEP
Nov. 8 -- In midterm Congressional elections, Republicans take control of the House and Senate for the first time in 40 years.

GATT
Dec. 8 -- Mr. Clinton signs a sweeping global tariff-cutting trade pact approved by Congress.

--- 1995 ---
 
PESO RESCUE
Jan. 31 -- Mr. Clinton sidesteps Congress and uses his own emergency authority to lend up to $20 billion to Mexico to keep it from defaulting on Government-issued bonds.

PLAYING DEFENSE
April 18 -- In his first prime-time news conference in more than eight months, Mr. Clinton declares he is not irrelevant in the face of the new Republican majority in Congress.

BUDGET BATTLE, PART 1
June 29 -- Congress passes a compromise seven-year budget plan designed to cut spending by nearly $900 billion, cut taxes by $245 billion and hold down increases in Medicare and other social programs.

SAFE HAVENS FALL
Aug. 11 -- President Clinton begins a new peace initiative in Bosnia, after Serbs overtake Muslim 'safe havens' and continue to shell Sarajevo.

BUDGET BATTLE, PART 2
Oct. 19 -- Repudiating his own earlier budget, Mr. Clinton says that the Republican plan to wipe out the deficit by 2002 is an attainable goal but should scale back proposed cuts in education, health and environment programs.

GOVERNMENT SHUTDOWN, PART 1
Nov. 14 -- The Federal Government suspends non-essential services in a shutdown that lasts six days.

BOSNIAN PEACE ACCORD
Nov. 21 -- The presidents of three rival Balkan states initial an agreement that ends nearly four years of war in Bosnia.

WELFARE VETO
Dec. 6 -- Mr. Clinton vetoes the Republicans' balanced-budget bill, which contains a sweeping overhaul of the welfare system. A month later, he vetoes a second welfare bill.
 
GOVERNMENT SHUTDOWN, PART 2
Dec. 16 -- After weeks of fruitless negotiations on a balanced budget, the Government shuts down for the second time.
 
--- 1996 ---
 
BUDGET STANDOFF
Jan. 24 -- Congressional Republicans abandon the effort to get a seven-year balanced budget, with the two sides split over core differences on health and welfare programs.
 
TAIWAN
March 10 -- To warn China against harassing Taiwan, the United States sends two aircraft carriers to the area.
 
DOLE RESIGNS
June 11 -- Following a Republican primary season in which Bob Dole emerges on top after unexpected challenges, the majority leader resigns from the Senate to campaign full time against Mr. Clinton.
 
CHINA TRADE
June 18 -- Under threat of sanctions, China begins enforcing the accord on intellectual property rights for foreign software, music and videos that it signed more than a year earlier.
 
MODEST PROPOSALS
Spring/Summer -- President Clinton spends much of his time making a series of speeches focusing on modest and low-cost solutions to social problems, such as curfews for teen-agers, uniforms in schools, fighting truancy, violence on television and a national registry for sex offenders. (pgs. 28 & 29)
