HEADLINE: The Path of Capitalism

 BODY:
To the Editor:
In "Beneficial Capitalism, R.I.P." (Viewpoint, June 16) Alexei Bayer lamented that the capitalist ideology was dead in the United States and presented his version of the rise and fall of the concept. 
 In his view, it arose in this country in response to "the Soviet threat in the postwar decades," and because this country "had to demonstrate that capitalism provides a good life for workers." Further, he said, the capitalist ideology reached its apogee with President Lyndon B. Johnson's declaration of the war on poverty, and began to stagnate after the fall of Communism in the Soviet Union.
Capitalism, however, is a system based on private property rights and free-market principles. It is marked by a lack of government intervention in the economy.
So how can the article praise a wide distribution of the economic pie as a high point of our capitalist ideology? Apparently, the writer's term refers to our mixed economy, which combines elements of socialism and capitalism, and it is the social element that he sees as disappearing.
 
LANA CONOLLEY
Manhattan, June 17

SECTION: Section 3; Page 32; Column 3; Money and Business/Financial Desk 

LENGTH: 184 words

HEADLINE: The Path of Capitalism

 BODY:
To the Editor:
"Beneficial Capitalism, R.I.P." sustained a balanced tone in delineating serious and controversial subject matter. 
 Global capitalism, in my opinion, has gone over the edge in its self-indulgent exploitation of its increasingly unopposed power to do as it pleases, now that the foil of Communism has been vanquished.
The article distinguished between capitalism and free-market thinking, while it would seem that there are more parallels than differences. Nevertheless, it appears that Americans once incorporated certain character traits into their social thinking -- a sense of responsibility toward humanity, if you will -- that have been consciously excised from at least the corporate psyche today.
Thankfully, in a democracy, for all its limitations, the propensity for excess by a dominant group will arouse opposition in another group, which at least has the theoretical ability to throw out the entrenched. This system, of course, is subject to manipulation, but it is the best we can come up with in an imperfect world.
 
JOHN MANNIX
Floral Park, L.I., June 17

HEADLINE: From Bears to Bulls and Back Again

BYLINE: By REED ABELSON 

 BODY:
WHEN David Shulman, the chief equity strategist for Salomon Brothers, announced last December that he had finally turned bullish on the stock market, it seemed as if the last bear on Wall Street had gone into hibernation. Suddenly, though, the bears are foraging everywhere.
Elaine Garzarelli, the strategist known as a market guru for having predicted the October 1987 crash, abruptly metamorphosed from rip-snorting bull to bear last week, sending stocks into a temporary tailspin and generating plenty of publicity. It was a stunning about-face for someone who had predicted just days before, in this newspaper, that the Dow Jones industrial average would hit 6,400. 
 She was not the first to predict a stock slump, though. Charles I. Clough Jr. of Merrill Lynch and Byron R. Wien of Morgan Stanley began warning investors about a possible market decline several months ago. Lesser-known names from lesser-known firms and writers of market newsletters have been quick to point out after the recent stock dip that they, too, saw it coming and expect more of the same. Even Mr. Shulman is sounding like a repentant bull, recently predicting a 10 percent correction before the stock market rallies.
Certainly, the majority of market seers at major brokerage firms -- Goldman, Sachs; Donaldson, Lufkin & Jenrette; Lehman Brothers; CS First Boston -- remain safely bullish. They make their money, after all, selling stocks to customers, not sending investors into cash.
But with no single voice reverberating like that of Henry Kaufman, who made a series of prescient interest-rate forecasts more than a decade ago, there is a rising cacophony of conflicting opinions.
Deciding how much faith to place in various market mavens is no simple matter. A good way to start is by examining how long they have held their positions, what school of thought they adhere to and what outside forces might be driving them.
Many prognosticators -- whether they rely on interest-rate predictions, market psychology or charts of stocks or the heavens -- struggle to make themselves heard above today's din. The most shrill voices often belong to people not backed by a major institution. Some attract attention by staking out extreme positions and by then being the quickest and the loudest to say they were right. And the prospect of a bear market is particularly enticing to them, because it offers a once-in-a-market-cycle opportunity to seal their fortune as market gurus.
"Every time the market goes down, some of the strategists are going to turn bearish, to enhance their name and to help people out," said Richard Strong, chairman of the Strong Funds in Milwaukee.
A well-timed market call could mean huge rewards as a highly paid investment strategist, a sought-after portfolio manager or a popular newsletter writer.
"If the market goes down, for the next three years you'll be as rich as Crassus," said Eugene Lerner, a former finance professor who now heads Disciplined Investment Advisors in Evanston, Ill. "The next time around, everybody will listen to you."
Perhaps no one knows the rewards of being anointed as a market guru better than Ms. Garzarelli does. She made her reputation by appearing on television and predicting a market drop a week before the 1987 crash. Several months earlier, she had told investors to put all their financial assets in cash only to reverse herself two weeks later in an interview in Financial World magazine, in which she proclaimed that the market would "probably head higher for the next several months."
Despite her penchant for dramatic turnabouts, no star shone brighter after the '87 crash. Ms. Garzarelli attracted $650 million into her own mutual fund and an annual salary estimated at more than $1 million from Lehman Brothers as the top technical analyst.
But she was slow to send investors back into stocks, causing them to miss out on sharp gains immediately after the crash. After turning in a mediocre performance as a portfolio manager, Ms. Garzarelli lost her job at Lehman Brothers in late 1994 in what was described as a cost-cutting move by the firm. She quickly resurrected herself as a sought-after prognosticator while running her own research and money management firm and writing a newsletter.
As a quantitative analyst, Ms. Garzarelli uses a combination of economic indicators -- earnings, industrial production, interest rates, cash-flow levels and the like -- to predict the direction of the market. After expressing unbridled optimism based on her indicators a week ago, she roiled the market on Tuesday by telling institutional clients that the market could decline by 15 to 25 percent from its recent highs.
News of her opinion was broadcast on CNBC and carried by several news services. The Dow industrial average, which was in the process of gaining 40 points, reversed course to lose 44 points for the session. Since then, though, it has gained more than 100 points.
"I think she is going to be the new Joe Granville," Mr. Lerner said, referring to one newsletter writer who has been persistently wrong in predicting a crash in the stock market.
With seemingly little new information about interest rates or inflation, which Ms. Garzarelli had described as the keys to her bullish forecast, her new opinion left some market observers shaking their heads. "What people don't understand is Elaine's model is not strictly scientific," said Alfred Goldman, the chief investment strategist for A. G. Edwards in St. Louis. He speculated that her change of heart was tied to her belief that corporate earnings would soon become disappointing.
Ms. Garzarelli, who left a telephone message with a brief explanation last week, said her sudden shift was simply a result of updating her model, a task she performs every two weeks. By using new numbers on corporate cash flows, she said, her indicators turned from neutral to bearish.
MANY of the bears now raising their heads are technical analysts who study charts and predict trends in the market by looking at recent patterns of stock movements and volumes. While that style of investing has been out of favor for several years, technicians are becoming more visible after the latest turmoil in the market.
"We go through cycles," explained Stan Weinstein, the editor and publisher of The Professional Tape Reader, a newsletter in Hollywood, Fla. "When things start to turn negative, that's when technicians become prominent."
In part, that is because technical analysts study what has just occurred -- the number of stocks that hit new highs, for example, or the number of stocks that advanced and the number that declined. When Mr. Weinstein told his several thousand subscribers on June 22 that the market in over-the-counter stocks had probably reached "the top of this speculative cycle," the Nasdaq composite index of small stocks had just dropped 6 percent from its June high.
Mr. Weinstein is among the most vocal bears, though his track record leaves something to be desired. According to Hulbert Financial Digest, which tracks newsletters, his market-timing services have significantly underperformed the broad market. Mr. Weinstein said Hulbert's methodology was unfair, but he declined to provide specific information.
In a statement to subscribers a week ago, Mr. Weinstein expanded his forecast beyond the small stocks to include the broad market. The bear market has already begun and "is likely to last well into 1997," he said, with "the potential to go significantly lower."
Another loud pessimist is Doug Fabian, the president of Fabian Investment Resources in Huntington Beach, Calif. In telephone calls to reporters and a statement to the press, he recently trumpeted his sell signal issued after the market closed on July 15, the day the Dow lost 161 points. Mr. Fabian uses a system developed by his father to time the market by issuing buy or sell signals based on the direction of the moving average of the Dow and a composite of mutual funds. "We are trend followers," he said.
"I am not a guru with a crystal ball," he added. "It's technical analysis, and it works."
The system is the top-ranked timing service followed by Hulbert and has returned an annual average of 15.6 percent over the last 16 years through June 30, beating the broad market by a hair and showing substantially less volatility.
Mr. Fabian estimates that his 40,000 subscribers pulled as much as $1 billion from stock funds over three days beginning on July 15. Mutual fund redemptions were unusually heavy during that time, but there is no way to prove or disprove whether his contention is correct.
Two of the five funds highlighted in Mr. Fabian's newsletter had split reactions. The Founders Growth fund attributed $100 million in transfers to Mr. Fabian's call, but a spokeswoman for the Berger 100 fund described it as "a nonevent."
Not that Mr. Fabian is willing to stake his credibility entirely on a coming bear market.
"The reason we haven't declared the bull market is dead is if the market breaks out on the upside," he said. In other words, if the market goes up, he could be bullish.
AND not all technicians are bearish, of course. A year ago, when being bullish was certainly nothing out of the ordinary, Ralph Acampora, the director of technical analysis for Prudential Securities, turned himself into a super bull, predicting that the Dow would climb to 7,000 in a few years. He reiterated his forecast in March, calling for the Dow to reach 6,000 by the end of this year.
Some strategists at major brokerage firms who employ fundamental analysis, which emphasizes corporate earnings and interest rates, have also turned bearish in the last several months. Their shift is particularly noteworthy because some of these firms have large retail bases of customers and their brokers make their livings selling stocks to clients.
These strategists can be reluctant to sound anything less than ebullient about the market. And they are sure to pause for more than a minute before issuing a bearish statement if they have been outspokenly bullish. "If Ralph Acampora shifted from the Dow at 7,000 to slightly neutral, his phone would melt in its cradle," said Robert Stovall, the president of Stovall/21st Advisors, referring to how anxious customers and brokers might react to the news.
Despite internal conflicts, "mostly the pressure is to be right," Mr. Shulman of Salomon said. He conceded, though, that there was a "natural bias" to be bullish at brokerage firms because investors make more money by buying stocks than by sitting on the sidelines. Mr. Shulman described the blunt forecast by Mr. Wien, the Morgan Stanley strategist, who in April predicted a 1,000-point decline in the Dow, as "a gutsy call."
Along with Mr. Wien, Wall Street's most recent bull-turned-bear is Mr. Clough, who recommended that investors reduce their stock allocations in February despite any grief he might take from Merrill Lynch's legions of brokers.
As members of the fundamentalist camp, both men look at many of the same indicators on the overall state of the economy. Yet they reached their latest stances from very different vantage points. Mr. Wien says he thinks that a strengthening economy will cause interest rates to rise and that a market decline will follow. Mr. Clough says he thinks that a faltering economy may lead to a recession and to weaker corporate profits and may cause -- you guessed it -- a market decline.
Mr. Wien and Mr. Clough are definitely not market timers, those all-or-nothing souls who recommend that investors switch from all stocks to all cash and back again as frequently as their crystal balls dictate. Their message is more moderate. Mr. Clough, for example, is advising investors to limit the portion of their portfolios in stocks to 40 percent.
Exactly what constitutes a bear is a matter of debate. Few of the strategists like the label, and definitions of a bear market, typically said to be a downturn of 20 percent or more, vary. Mr. Clough, for one, prefers to be known as bullish on bonds rather than bearish on stocks.
Scarce as they were for a while, some of the bears never really died. The market turbulence just flushed them out.
These seemingly perennial bears, strategists whose advice led investors to miss out on the tremendous gains in the stock market over the last few years, are in great demand by news organizations whenever the market turns south. Though they bristle at their label, the group includes Michael Metz, the chief investment strategist for Oppenheimer & Company, who has been talking of a stock decline for much of the last two and a half years; Steven Leuthold, who said he was a bull in the 80's but has been bearish for the last two years, and Jim Rogers, the charming, bow-tied private investor who has disliked the domestic stock market for many years.
"I don't like the term," Mr. Metz said when asked whether he was a bear. But he said he thought cash would outperform stocks until sometime in 1997. People think of him as a perennial bear, he said, because "I'm the only one who really speaks out."
ANOTHER bear is Mr. Granville, the newsletter writer who last week said that his much-heralded crash was just around the corner. But even he objected to being called a perennial bear, saying he had only been bearish since 1992.
Jeremy J. Siegel, a finance professor at the Wharton School, dismisses many of the long-term bears. "If you make enough predictions, long enough, you'll be right," he said. While some strategists, he said, might have a hot streak for a while, just as mutual fund managers do, knowing when that streak will cool off is extraordinarily difficult.
Mr. Goldman of A. G. Edwards concurred, adding that he included himself in that admonition. "No one should take any one guru that seriously," he said. "My basic approach to the market is that none of us mortals can predict the future. The best I can do is to understand the trends in place in terms of the economy and corporate earnings."
So what is an investor to make of those who are growling the loudest now? "While the bears may be right," Mr. Shulman said, "it's too early to declare a victory." And if the bears are wrong, most will probably go about their business, simply having missed out on an opportunity to be the new guru of the 90's.

HEADLINE: What's Doing in San Diego

BYLINE: By JAMES STERNGOLD; JAMES STERNGOLD is a business reporter for The Times based in Los Angeles.

 BODY:
This is going to be a weighty summer for San Diego, which will play host to the Republican convention from Aug. 12 to 15. But for all the seriousness that will surround the convention, a visitor might get a better sense of what San Diego is really all about, and why it can be such a delightful place to visit, by attending another seminal summer event -- the Annual World Body Surfing Championships. This hotly fought contest will be held from Aug. 16 to 18 off the Oceanside Pier.
What this event underscores is the fact that San Diego -- though overshadowed in the past by Los Angeles and such popular attractions to the north as Disneyland -- is beautifully situated, and that the city has grown economically, politically and socially in large measure because of the draw of its wonderful coastline and superb weather. It is no accident that several of top golf-club companies have headquarters here. The area is also home to some of the country's largest Navy and Marine bases and is a thriving center for technology companies. 
 But for a visitor, this is definitely an area for shorts, T-shirts, flip-flops and trendy sunglasses. And a sweatshirt -- the warm, dry days generally give way to cool evenings. Often, outsiders do not appreciate another draw: the surprising diversity of the landscape. There are vineyards, mountains (the famous Palomar Observatory is in San Diego County) and desert, with many parks making a variety of terrain accessible.

 
Events
 The biggest event of the summer, of course, is the Republican National Convention. It is likely to tie the city in knots, with 30,000 visitors expected, and casual visitors are encouraged to avoid the city that week. Many hotels have been booked for months. The delegates and politicians will gather in the handsome San Diego Convention Center, on the waterfront downtown.
Taking advantage of the spotlight the convention will cast on the city, San Diego and the surrounding communities have planned a range of other events. There are actually two body-surfing competitions. The first, the 18th annual body surfing contest at Carlsbad State Beach at Ponto, goes from Aug. 9 to 11. More than 300 surfers from around the state are expected. Competition begins at 7 A.M. and runs a little past noon. Entry is free. Call (619) 434-2989. The 20th annual World Body Surfing Competition, Aug. 16 to 18, is at Oceanside Pier, at the north end of the county. As many as 400 surfers from around the world are expected. Heats begin at 7 A.M. and run until about 1 P.M. Call (619) 966-4535.
The city's long maritime tradition is saluted at the Maritime Museum, 1492 North Harbor Drive, (619) 234-9153, which will have special sailings of its centerpiece, an 1863 bark called the Star of India, the oldest iron-hulled sailing ship still afloat, on Aug. 10, 11 and 18. Although no visitors will be permitted on board while it is sailing, the ship should be quite a sight in the city's harbor. It will depart from the Embarcadero in downtown, a good viewing point. The museum also displays the Berkeley, a ferry that used to run between San Francisco and Oakland, and the Medea, an old Scottish steam yacht in beautiful condition. Through the end of September, the museum will feature an exhibition on the Monitor, the Civil War ironclad ship. Admission is $5. Open daily 9 A.M. to 9 P.M. in summer; closes at 8 the rest of the year.
The Miramar Air Show take place Aug. 17 and 18. at, 9:30 A.M. to 4 P.M., plus 6 to 10 P.M. on Aug 17. It will include exhibits on the ground at the Miramar Naval Air Station, off I-15 north of San Diego, as well as performances by the Blue Angels, wing walking, parachutists, simulated air combat and more. Admission is free for those who bring a blanket or beach chair. Grandstand seats are $6 ($3 for those aged 3 to 10), with special seating, including food and drink as high as $50 ($30 for children). Call (619) 537-6289.
One of the more pleasant seaside spots in the county is the town of La Jolla, just north of San Diego. From Aug. 16 to 25 the town will hold its Summerfest, a series of chamber music concerts in Sherwood Auditorium at the Museum of Contemporary Art, San Diego, at 700 Prospect Street. Tickets are $20 to $32.50. Call (619) 459-3728.
The La Jolla Playhouse (619) 550-1010, is featuring "The Green Bird," a comedy by Carlo Gozzi, to Aug. 25. It opened in New York earlier this year, is directed by Julie Taymor and has music by Elliot Goldenthal. Tickets are $19 to $36.
 
Sightseeing
 San Diego is justifiably proud of its parks and other outdoor attractions, which get crowded at peak times, particularly weekends.
The 1,200-acre Balboa Park, in the heart of San Diego, contains most of the city's museums and the justifiably renowned San Diego Zoo. Thezoo, (619) 234-3153, which is celebrating its 80th anniversary, has the usual array of bears, elephants, gorillas, tigers and birds but its 100 acres of wooded, hilly grounds interspersed with well-tended gardens give it a special beauty. Another thing that sets it apart is the care with which the various animal habitats have been designed. Entry 9 A.M. to 9 P.M. until Labor Day. After that it closes at 6 P.M., with free admission from 4 to 6 P.M. Admission is $15, $6 ages 3 to 11.
The zoo also operates the San Diego Wild Animal Park, (619) 234-6541, 30 miles north of downtown in Escondido. The animals roam freely over the 2,150-acre park, while visitors travel on monorails and observe from a distance. Entry 9 A.M. to 6 P.M. Monday to Wednesday, 9 A.M. to 8 P.M. other days. Admission is $18.95, $11.95 aged 3 to 11.
A prime spot to begin any exploration of the Balboa Park museums is the Spreckels Organ Pavilion, (619) 226-0819, an outdoor theater where a booming pipe organ is played in free concerts Sunday at 2 P.M. all year, plus Monday evening in the summer.
Within walking distance of the organ pavilion are the Museum of Man (619) 239-2001, showing an exhibition of Pueblo, Navajo and Hispanic textiles to Feb. 23; the Museum of Art, (619) 232-7931; the Aerospace Historical Center, (619) 234-8291, which features a reproduction of the "Spirit of St. Louis," which was built nearby, and the child-friendly Reuben H. Fleet Space Theater and Science Center, (619) 238-1233, with hands-on exhibits and an Imax theater.
The Palomar Observatory, (619) 742-2119, with its 200-inch reflecting telescope, is open to the public. It is in rugged hills in the eastern part of San Diego County, less than two hours from downtown. Visitors cannot look through the telescope, but they can see it and view photos taken through it. Admission is free. Enter off Route 76. Open 9 A.M. to 4 P.M.
About seven miles north of the observatory is is Palomar Mountain State Park, (619) 742-3462, which charges $5 a car, and has large picnic grounds, hiking trails and a small stocked fishing pond.
The campus of the University of California at San Diego is a rolling stretch of land near the ocean in La Jolla and is handsomely landscaped, with plenty of outdoor art and fine architecture. There are guided walking tours on the second, fourth and fifth Sundays of each month. Information is available at the booths at the entry gate near the intersection of Gilman Drive and La Jolla Village Drive, (619) 534-4414.
One of the great pleasures of a visit is the beaches -- particularly the stretch of ocean from Mission Bay, north of downtown, heading north through La Jolla to Del Mar and Cardiff-by-the-Sea. A particularly picturesque beach is the Torrey Pines Beach in the Torrey Pines State Reserve, (619) 755-2063, just north of La Jolla. The 1,700-acre park includes dramatic cliffs and, of course, gnarled Torrey pines. The beach entrance is between Callan and North Torrey Pines Park Road. Parking is $4 a car and the beach is open 8 A.M. to sunset.
 
Where to Stay
 The modern, well-situated Wyndham Emerald Plaza, 400 West Broadway, (800) 626-3988, fax (619) 239-3274, with 436 rooms, has a special discount rate of $109 a night (the standard rate is $238), including breakfast, for visitors with a coupon from a booklet the city is making available until the end of the year; call (619) 236-1212 for the coupons, which also cover restaurants and tourist attractions.
Farther north are several hotels on particularly nice stretches of coast. In La Jolla, the Sea Lodge, 8110 Camino del Oro, (800) 237-5211, fax (619) 456-9346, with 128 rooms, is a Soanish-style hotel in a quiet spot, and rates for a double room run from $165 to $255.
The homey Del Mar Inn, 720 Camino Del Mar, (800) 451-4515, fax (619) 792-8196, in the seaside village of Del Mar, has 80 rooms with doubles at $130 a night.
Budget: At the Ramada Inn Salona Beach, 8717 South Highway 101, (800) 232-2407, fax (619) 792-2370, rates are reasonable at a good location. Double rooms start at $110.
Luxury: The best-known lodging in the area is the Hotel del Coronado, 1500 Orange Avenue, Coronado, (800) 468-3533, fax (619) 522-8238, a wonderful, gigantic clapboard structure overlooking Coronado Beach. It is white with a red shingle roof, and Old Glory flutters atop its big dome. The hotel, built in 1888, has a storied past (the movie "Some Like it Hot" was filmed there in 1958). There are 399 rooms in the old building and 293 more in a more recently built section. Double rooms run from $179 to $399 a night.
The Sheraton Grande Torrey Pines, 10950 North Torrey Pines Road, (619) 558-1500, fax (619) 597-6962, near the Torrey Pines State Reserve and in front of a well-regarded public golf course, has doubles with modern decor from $220 a night, several restaurants, a pool and a health club.
 
Where to Eat
 Two lively Italian restaurants in the rejuvenated Gaslamp Quarter downtown are Fio's, 801 Fifth Avenue, (619) 234-3467, which attracts a fashionable crowd and where lunches run from about $7 to $15 a person and dinners from $10 to $25, and the smaller Panevino, 722 Fifth Avenue, (619) 595-7959, where lunches run are $9 to $15 and dinners $12 to $30 per person.
For Mexican food, one of the more relaxed restaurants is up the coast in Cardiff-by-the-Sea, the North County Taco Auctioneers, 1951 San Elijo Avenue; (619) 942-8226. It is a modest place with outdoor tables and is known for its taco tray (a three-foot-long board with dishes of ingredients and freshly made tortillas for making your own, $13.95 for two people at lunch, $1 more at dinner). Lunch runs from $6 to $10 and dinners from $8 to $15.
For brunch overlooking the ocean, try the Atoll restaurant at the Catamaran resort, 3999 Mission Boulevard in Mission Beach; (619) 488-1081. The casual restaurant is popular with families. The buffet emphasizes seafood. Breakfast and lunch run from $7 to $20 a person.
Another well-known seaside restaurant is Qwiig's Bar and Grill, at 5083 Santa Monica Avenue in Ocean Beach; (619) 222-1101. Most tables command a fine view. Meals run from $11 to as much as $40 a person at lunch and dinner.
A favorite hangout in La Jolla is the homey Harry's Coffee Shop, 7545 Girard Avenue; (619) 454-7381. Breakfast is served all day. Aside from standard coffee shop fare, other offerings include Mexican dishes and such specials as salmon Florentine. Closes at 2:30 P.M. Meals are $3 to $10 a person.
 
Ultimately, though, the F.D.A. could not have taken the offensive without the mistakes made by the tobacco industry. Cigarette companies had left behind a paper trail stating their goals of developing, through clever advertising campaigns, children as customers. Leaked industry documents also show that some companies privately recognized cigarettes as little more than nicotine-delivery devices. What the tobacco companies aggressively dispute, and what will undoubtedly be argued in court, is whether, as the F.D.A. charges, the industry manipulated the nicotine levels in cigarettes to create addiction.
In interviews, Kessler tends to answer questions about the F.D.A.'s tobacco campaign by reading directly from tobacco industry documents; it is his way of avoiding a discussion of his own opinions, certainly, and also his way of letting the enemy make his case. And so it is tonight: Kessler roots around his living room in search of a document. "Here," he says, thumbing his way through some oft-visited pages in cigarette company reports from the 1970's that were recently obtained by the F.D.A. " 'The adolescent seeks to display his new urge for independence with a symbol, and cigarettes are such a symbol,' " he reads. " 'The fragile, developing self-image of the young person needs all of the support and enhancement it can get. Smoking may appear to enhance that self-image in a variety of ways. This self-image enhancement effect has traditionally been a strong promotional theme for cigarette brands.' "
He takes a breath and puts the document down silently, as if that's all that needs to be said.
So doesn't that make him angry?
"There's a time and place for anger," he says, "but not in this job."
What the conversation does produce are strong hints about where Kessler hopes to carry his anti-cigarette campaign. So far, some of the F.D.A.'s ideas have won wide attention: the proposals to ban cigarette vending machines, to forbid sporting events from naming themselves after tobacco companies, to halt distribution of Joe Camel-style paraphernalia and to force cigarette companies to abandon their visually seductive magazine ads, restricting them to text-only ads in magazines with significant teen-age readership. But it is not enough, Kessler tells me, to separate advertisers from their target audience. Teen-agers must be actively convinced that smoking is, in his words, "uncool."
There is a provision, mostly unnoticed, in the proposed F.D.A. regulations that demands $150 million each year from tobacco companies to finance anti-smoking ads. It is this sort of campaign, Kessler says, that will help the Federal Government reach its goal of cutting under-age smoking in half over the next seven years.
"You want to talk about getting angry -- ask a 16- or 17-year-old how they feel when they see an 11- or 12-year-old smoke a cigarette," Kessler says, implying that an ad featuring a teen-ager attacking cigarettes would prove more effective than a simple advertising ban.
And if it doesn't?
"What the agency has said is that once we assert jurisdiction over tobacco, we will put these measures into effect. And then if there needs to be other measures, we'll take other measures."
This is what frightens the tobacco industry, the idea that banning vending machines and rodeo sponsorship isn't the end for Kessler. He won't say what measures he would take if the current proposals prove ineffective, but there is speculation that he would try to force tobacco companies to produce cigarettes that are less toxic and less addictive.
It may have been Kessler's threat of even more regulation that in May prompted Philip Morris, America's largest cigarette maker, to issue with another tobacco company a plan to curb under-age smoking. The plan, which Philip Morris said would be adopted only if the F.D.A. agreed to leave the industry unregulated, mimics the F.D.A. proposals in many ways. But it is laced with holes and semantic sleights of hand. For one thing, the F.D.A. proposal states that the text-only cigarette ads would appear in magazines with 15 percent or more under-age readers; the Philip Morris proposal calls for the ads in magazines with 15 percent or more under-age subscribers, which would allow visual ads in, for instance, Rolling Stone.
"Their proposal fell substantially short," Kessler says.
But what if the tobacco industry truly matched the F.D.A.'s plan, proposal for proposal? Would the agency step aside?
"They would still need to be regulated," Kessler says, giving no quarter. "It would have to be credibly done. There would need to be a regulatory framework."
Kessler can afford to feel cocky just now; the new rules aren't yet in effect and the lawsuits from the tobacco companies haven't yet been heard. It is far from certain, though, that the Supreme Court would view sympathetically any attempt by a Government agency to regulate advertising or let the F.D.A. force the tobacco industry to sponsor anti-smoking messages.
Over the course of a dozen conversations, it becomes clear to me that Kessler won't leave the F.D.A., willingly at least, until he humbles the tobacco industry. I asked him what he would do if he lost the fight. He replied that, with the fundamental perception about cigarettes so radically altered, losing is no longer possible. "The truth," he said, "will survive."
What Do Americans Truly Want From Kessler?
It is almost certainly true that David Kessler is the only regulator in Government service who draws admirers the way that, say, George Stephanopoulos does. This should help his battle against cigarettes, except that he doesn't seem to like the attention.
"You're David Kessler, right?" a woman asks as she passes him in the cereal aisle of a Bethesda supermarket. After an uncomfortable pause, he admits that he is. "Keep up the good work on cigarettes," she says.
This happens twice more in 20 minutes. Granted, the supermarket is located inside the Beltway, deep in NPR and Volvo country, but such attention is presumably not accorded, even here, to high officials of the F.T.C. or OSHA.
Kessler and I are visiting the supermarket to discuss the impact of one of his key achievements: the 1994 introduction of the more-than-you-ever-wanted-toknow food labels, which place into dietary context the amount of fat in nearly every product sold in American food stores.
"I suppose I'm guilty," Kessler says as he reaches for a pint of Ben & Jerry's Chubby Hubby ice cream (350 calories per serving, 210 from fat). "If you're asking for a public apology for every time you've put something back on a shelf after reading the label, I'm guilty and I apologize. Sometimes, I pick up something and read the label and put it back. Sometimes I don't."
Surely David Kessler, a man painted by his enemies as an abstemious kvetch, bent on bleeding the joy out of life in the name of public health, doesn't eat ice cream? "In moderation," he says, then puts the Chubby Hubby back in the freezer.
He sweeps his arm across the aisle. "Look at this selection," he says, referring to the logjam of "low fat" and "no fat" products. "What we did with the new food labels -- it's about information. Information created demand. The food label changed the way manufacturers manufacture. What the critics of the new food labeling don't understand is that it's about choice."
Kessler's critics, though, say that the new food label, like the battle to regulate tobacco, is an example of an imperious Government agency infantilizing the American public by deciding what's in its best interests.
Just ask Walker Merryman, a Washington lobbyist.
"Have you ever looked at what Kessler did with orange juice?" he says, referring to the F.D.A.'s 1991 seizure of thousands of gallons of Citrus Hill orange juice. The juice was made from concentrate, and the company, Procter & Gamble, had refused to remove the word "fresh" from the label.
"Now, I love orange juice, I really do," Merryman continues. "But I only like fresh. I remember seeing the advertising for this brand and I saw the word 'fresh,' right under the brand. I also remember that it was clear to anyone who looked at it that the orange juice was made from concentrate. But not to Kessler. I remember thinking when he seized it, that's the dumbest goddamn thing I've ever heard of. Does he think people can't read a [expletive] label? Kessler thinks consumers are too damn dumb to look at a label and make up their minds. There's a philosophical divide we're talking about here."
Now, discount for a moment that Walker Merryman, the orange juice lover, is also the vice president of the Tobacco Institute, the industry trade group, and a prime spokesman for the "adult choice" cigarette argument. Merryman's view of Kessler is similar to that of most F.D.A. critics, who see at the core of everything that Kessler's agency does a flock of theoretically brilliant, Ivy League-trained lawyers who believe in their ability to protect Americans from themselves and who believe even more in their own superiority.
It doesn't take long, in fact, for some of the F.D.A.'s detractors to veer away from specific, policy-based criticisms to a sort of amorphous, class-based attack rooted in the cultural divide between Eastern, Government-friendly elites and everyone else. Such is the case with Joe Barton, the Texas Republican. When I asked him to talk in specific terms about the agency's weaknesses, he said: "They've had regulatory authority for so long that they think they're above people. To me, they're like the professional bureaucracy of the French Government. If they're supposed to go to lunch at 12, they do, and they come back at 2, and you'll just have to cool your heels waiting for these people."
Kessler, predictably, is hesitant to analyze the culture gap that may explain some of the hostility directed at him. He usually prefers instead to make mention of an eternal verity about Government regulation: "If you want to make people happy, don't become a regulator." He says this ruefully, but only half seriously, because he does believe that most Americans, when faced with a choice in the matter, would opt to have the Government watching over "the stuff they put into their bodies," as he calls it.
What's at stake on Capitol Hill is just how much watching the F.D.A. will be allowed to do in the future. Though he was pessimistic in May about the Republican legislation, Kessler seemed less burdened by July. Some Republican leaders in the House began to worry that a push for radical F.D.A. deregulation, and the floor fight that could follow, would be unhelpful to the party, still reeling from the perception that it had tried to gut environmental regulation.
In late July, the Republicans agreed to one major compromise, on an issue concerning both the F.D.A. and the Environmental Protection Agency. Republicans had wanted to scrap the Delaney Clause, a 1958 law that banned carcinogens in food additives and pesticides. Most scientists agreed that the Delaney standards were outdated, but the Administration didn't want to repeal the law unless it was replaced with other safeguards. Congressional Republicans are now proposing to do just that, having devised pesticide standards that are acceptable to Democrats while keeping, for now, the food-additive provisions of the Delaney Clause.
But support for deregulation still runs strong and, even if Congressional Republicans jettison their F.D.A. wish list, they are still pushing to check the agency's power. Meanwhile, Kessler continues to fight the impact of previous deregulation. Trying to take action against ephedrine in the wake of George Korizis's death, the F.D.A. is now investigating, at its own expense, each of the several hundred adverse events linked to ephedrine in dietary supplements.
And then there is the F.D.A.'s plan to regulate tobacco, which could be unveiled by President Clinton even before the Republican Convention. The announcement would surely be the climax of Kessler's F.D.A. stewardship. A few weeks ago, I asked him if he worries about a Dole victory this November and the attempt to undo tobacco regulation that might well follow follow.
Kessler dodged the question, as he dodges nearly all partisan questions, instead taking the opportunity to scold the agency's critics for misunderstanding the American public. "I learned something when I was Senate staff in 1981 and 1982," he said, referring to the early Reagan era and its deregulatory fervor. "The public does not want deregulation in the areas of health and safety. Time and time again, there are people on the Hill who misjudge this."
I asked Kessler if perhaps, as a young Republican, he, too, misread the public's position and is now repenting for that misreading. It is the sort of question that Kessler doesn't like, so he gave a nonanswer: "I watched the Republicans coming to deregulate the F.D.A., and in the end it didn't work because it's not what the American people want."
He is too cautious to make bold predictions, but he sees the current push for deregulation as a pendulum swing brought on, as it were, by the agency's success. "The better we prevent things from ever happening, people will feel more and more secure about their food and drugs, and the more pressure there will be on us to stop regulating. But the pendulum is dangerous. People get hurt by the pendulum. It's been the same pattern for 40 or 50 years."
Kessler is confident that, current F.D.A. controversies notwithstanding, the pendulum will swing back in the agency's direction. "There's a disconnect in this country on this issue -- I mean, take a look at Britain," he said, referring to mad cow disease. "The Government could have fallen, and over what? A food issue. And here they want to lower safety standards. In Britain, the one thing they're crying out for is a commission that has no other conflicts or loyalties other than making sure the public health is well served. Well, we already have that here."
 
A Full Plate at the F.D.A.
 The F.D.A. is embattled these days, and not just because of opposition from Congressional Republicans and the tobacco industry. David Kessler's agency regulates just about everything Americans put in their bodies, and there are plenty of other contested issues on its agenda. Here's a sample.
 
 Cyclospora: This summer the agency is pouring resources into trying to understand cyclospora, the protozoa that have recently caused severe diarrhea in hundreds of people. Along with the Centers for Disease Control, the F.D.A. has been searching for the source of this outbreak in, among other places, raspberries from Guatemala. The F.D.A., which regulates all food except meat and poultry (the Agriculture Department oversees those), has the power to pull adulterated foods off the market and to keep them out of the country.
 
 Bovine Growth Hormone: The F.D.A. is awaiting a follow-up report on bovine somatotropin (BST), an artificial hormone used to increase the milk output of dairy cows. The F.D.A. has already approved the drug, but because consumer advocates and scientists feared that BST might harm humans, the agency demanded that Monsanto, its manufacturer, conduct a two-year study on its possible deleterious effects.
 
 Olestra: Fat-free potato chips made with the much-hyped olestra, a fat substitute manufactured by Procter & Gamble, are causing diarrhea and other intestinal problems. The F.D.A. is under attack from the Center for Science in the Public Interest for allowing olestra onto the market, even though the agency did insist on a label informing consumers of the potential for "loose stools" in products containing olestra.
 
 RU-486: The first "morning after" abortion pill, developed in France, is being fast-tracked for review and possible approval. A decision could come as soon as September; approval is probable, given that two weeks ago an F.D.A. advisory panel endorsed the drug.
 
 Med Guides: The F.D.A. under Kessler has reintroduced an idea proposed during the Carter Administration: demanding that pharmacies to provide more in-depth information about the drugs they dispense. These "Med Guides," as the F.D.A. calls them, would essentially be a drug equivalent of the information-packed food labels required by the F.D.A. two years ago. Med Guides now seem likely to be required, despite opposition by druggists and deregulation-minded members of Congress.
 
 Dexfenfluramine: The agency is under attack from some consumer advocates for giving the go-ahead to this drug, the first anti-obesity drug approved by the F.D.A. in 23 years. Sold by Wyeth-Ayerst Laboratories under the name Redux, dexfenfluramine is already on the market; some scientists speculate that it may cause neurological and lung damage.
 
 Xenotransplantation: An F.D.A. panel is now weighing the issues surrounding the transplanting of animal tissue into humans, like the recent use of baboon bone marrow in an AIDS patient and the possible transplantation of primate and pig organs into humans. The agency will most likely issue guidelines by the end of the year.

 CORRECTION:
The What's Doing column on page 10 of the Travel section today, about San Diego, carries an incorrect listing of vital statistics. It was prepared for another issue, about Mount Desert Island, Me. The correct listing appears in the main news section today, on page 39.
The What's Doing column on July 28, about San Diego, misstated La Jolla's municipal status. It is part of San Diego, not a separate town.
Because of an editing error, the What's Doing column on July 28 about San Diego misstated the admission policy of the San Diego Zoo. After Labor Day there is no entry after 4 P.M., though the zoo stays open to 6 P.M.; admission is not free at any time.
A photograph with an article on Aug. 4 about David Kessler and the Food and Drug Administration made an incorrect association between some potato chips and olestra, a fat substitute. The colored chips in the photograph, Terra Chips, are made with peanut, sunflower or canola oil, not olestra.

HEADLINE: Connecticut Q&A: Debra K. Pasquale;
A Fiscal Profile of Biomedical Research

BYLINE: By ROBERT A. HAMILTON

 BODY:
DEBRA K. PASQUALE is executive director of Connecticut United for Research Excellence, a group known as CURE that promotes biomedical research.
 Recently CURE surveyed the biomedical industry in the state, concluding that it is contributing nearly $2 billion a year to the economy, and that there is potential for much more. "No other state," she said, "has quantified the enterprise the way we just have." 

 CURE has five employees and an annual budget of about $500,000. Ms. Pasquale is a registered nurse with degrees from the University of Hartford School of Nursing and Yale who started in critical care and specialized in oncology and clinical trials before joining CURE.
 Recently she answered questions about CURE in an interview in Groton at Pfizer Inc., a founding member of CURE. Following are excerpts from that conversation.
 Q. Could you sum up what your studies concluded?
 A. Twenty-five years ago there was one company in Connecticut, Pfizer, and it had about a $15 million research and development budget. Today there are four large companies, which spent $1.2 billion in 1995. That represents 10 percent of all pharmaceutical research and development in the country.
 There are also a lot of Federal research dollars that come in; Connecticut ranks fourth in the nation per capita, and in the top 10 in total dollars.
 And we really haven't capitalized on the little companies. They love to associate with the big pharmaceuticals, which are already here. We have a tremendous opportunity to attract small biotechnology companies to the state.

 Q. Does $1.2 billion represent all spending by the big biomedical firms?
 A. No. It doesn't include capital investments, for instance. In 1995, capital expenditure was about $175 million. When you look at everything, the impact comes up to somewhere near $1.7 billion.

 Q. What about small companies?
 A. There are about 10 small biotech companies now and we estimate that you could go from about 300 research and development employees at those firms now to about 1,000 in five years. And each one of those jobs has a multiplier effect of about 2.5, which means each job created in the industry creates 2.5 indirect jobs.
 That would represent about $20 million in revenue to the state through taxes and other sources. And if any of the compounds they're researching do make it to the market, the potential could be greater.

 Q. How many people does the industry employ now?
 A. At our major pharmaceutical companies, it's about 5,500. If you total everything up -- universities, small companies, other research employees -- it's about 8,500.
 
 Q. Is the industry concentrating anywhere in Connecticut?
 A. It's currently concentrating in greater New Haven, and the majority of companies have spun off from Yale University. There's certainly that capability at the University of Connecticut in Farmington also. We're a small state with two key research universities and four large pharmaceutical companies; there's a tremendous potential for growth.

 Q. When and why was CURE formed?
 A. CURE was organized about six years ago. There were a number of large corporations, leading research universities and hospitals interested in providing factual information about what goes on in the research laboratories of Connecticut.
 The driving force was a lot of inflammatory information on animal research. People felt it was high time scientists started telling the public what went on in the laboratories. Very quickly they realized that while animal research was a hot button issue, there was also a need to promote science education and public support for science.

 Q. How many members do you have?
 A. There are 65, up from 32 at the start, including educational institutes, health care corporations, hospitals or health care systems, professional societies and heart and cancer societies.
 Normally there is competition between a lot of these groups. Hospitals compete with each other; it's their business. But in this forum, they really come together.

 Q. But don't hospitals have very different goals from, say, universities or corporations?
 A. They have very similar goals in medical research. No single entity can take a new medicine from concept to the patient. So you rely on years of basic science that is done at the university. Then the pharmaceutical company builds on that to get it into a product line. Then you have the hospitals involved in clinical trials.
 Even when you look at big versus little, there is cooperation. The large companies develop drugs in a very traditional way. But the small companies can develop an innovative concept very quickly, move it through the trial stage quicker. And they often form business relationships with large companies to get it to market.

 Q. What are some advantages of the biotechnology industry?
 A. Biotechnology represents a new trend for the labor market in Connecticut: a knowledge-based industry. It requires a very educated workforce, and the average R.&D. salary is about $63,000. Those people have very high buying power. It requires very sophisticated equipment, so there's a lot of capital investment.

 Q. What can the state do to bring more of these businesses in?
 A. We made three recommendations. The principal one was really making the state more of a partner in the work that we're doing.
 The state also needs a matching grant program. If a company is putting money into research at a university or hospital, the state should match that.
 No. 3, there should be a tax credit for research. Those credits already exist for manufacturers, but the little companies doing just research couldn't get sales tax credit. That proposal passed as a bill in May.

 Q. Does the state have disadvantages in attracting new companies?
 A. One of the biggest problems is that there is no laboratory space for emerging companies. It's not a question of building this many square feet of space in this town by this date; it's really a question of giving the scientific community and developers a loan to give them access to equipment and space fairly quickly.

 Q. Is there a continuing conflict with animal rights groups over the issue of laboratory animals?
 A. You need to put that topic within a broader context: If you want new medicines, new and important medical devices, then there are steps within the scientific process that you need to consider. That includes test tubes, cell cultures, animal studies and human clinical studies.
 I'm a pet owner. I love my poodle. And you say to people, "You don't kick the dog." The same notion holds for laboratory animals -- there have got to be, and there are, safeguards in place for humane care and treatment of those animals, for ethical considerations as well as for good science. If the animals aren't cared for properly the science is bad, and the medicines that are developed won't work.

 Q. Are you working with schools to promote science education?
 A. Because scientific information is doubling every 10 years, our focus from the very start has been to be very aggressive at the kindergarten through grade 12 level. We have an innovative publication called BioRap, available free to Connecticut middle school students. About 170,000 copies a year go out.
 We have issues dealing on topics such as AIDS, rabies, Lyme disease, sun and skin care, aging and genetics. Each issue includes information on careers, science and discoveries, and a lot of health care tips.
 Last year we embarked on a campaign to make it available to schools nationally. So far 44 states have ordered more than 300,000 copies.
 This year we've applied grant funding and some support from the National Institutes of Health, in collaboration with the University of Connecticut. BioRap is going to be on the Internet.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Debra K. Pasquale, executive director of Connecticut United for Research Excellence. (Thomas McDonald for The New York Times)

PUB-DATE: July 28, 1996

SECTION: Section 13CN; Page 08; Column 3; Connecticut Weekly Desk

LENGTH: 1580 words

HEADLINE: Help From Overseas in Raising Youngsters

BYLINE: By ROBIN F. DeMATTIA

 BODY:
SINGLE parents and two-income families alike face the dilemma every day: Who takes care of the children while the adults are at work? Traditionally their choices have been limited to day care centers, babysitters and nannies.
 But 10 years ago another attractive option became available: a Federally approved program that supplies European au pairs to American families. The first such program, Au Pair in America, a Greenwich-based operation, has brought more than 70,000 young Europeans to the United States, this year placing 4,000 au pairs with families in 36 states, including 408 in Connecticut. 

 "The bottom line is American families need child care, and they want quality, safe child care," said Barbara Cartledge, director of Au Pair in America, now one of eight such programs. "This gives them the added benefit of a cultural experience."
 Ms. Cartledge said the young women, more than 80 percent of whom stay with the program for the full year they are legally allowed, welcome the chance to travel and study in the United States while living in a protective environment.
 "The No. 1 reason Europeans want to come to the United States," Ms. Cartledge says, "is to experience American life through a different angle, not just an academic or work environment. And there's a real sense of security in knowing that you're coming to live with a family that's been screened."
 Until this year the au pairs came primarily from Western Europe. Late last year, however, the government authorized worldwide participation in au pair programs and Au Pair in America expects 450 au pairs from Eastern Europe, South Africa, Australia and New Zealand to sign up this year.
 In the fall, the program will also launch Au Pair in Europe, allowing Americans to join families in England, Spain, France and Germany as au pairs.
 Twelve-year old Kathleen Heagney of Simsbury may someday be among them. Seven au pairs have taken care of her and her siblings -- 13-year-old Walker, 9-year old Addison and 5-year old Emmet -- over the last nine years.
 As their mother, Barbara Heagney, a schoolteacher in Farmington, put it, "When my husband first suggested having someone live in our home it was a foreign idea to me, but I didn't know what else we were going to do."
 Her husband, Robert, a lawyer in East Hartford, broached the subject when Addison was soon to be born.
 "Our major concern was that we would give up a certain amount of our privacy," Ms. Heagney said. "When you get over that, you wonder if you can make the girl happy, especially one from overseas."
 The Heagneys' first two au pairs were from the United States, but it didn't work out. "They didn't seem to have this real desire," Ms. Heagney says. "They acted like this was better than nothing."
 Since then their au pairs -- from Germany, England and the Netherlands -- have fit in much better.
 "I want to share with them things about the United States as much as they want to learn it," said Ms. Heagney. "And they want to share things about their country as much as we want to hear it. It's give and take."
 Ms. Cartledge explains what her agency looks for in potential au pairs: "We don't want people who only want to travel. They must be open, have a lot of energy, want to work with children, and be proficient in English."
 The young women are interviewed, submit a medical form, have a criminal background check, provide references, complete a personality profile and write a 600-word essay telling why they want to be in the program. Finally, the families and the au pairs speak on the phone to determine if they are compatible.
 Because the au pairs' visas allow them to stay for only one year, Ms. Heagney must change each year, but then too, the family's needs change.
 When their third child was on the way, "We needed an au pair who could adjust to a baby who might cry regularly or was fussy. Now that the kids are older it's like a revolving door here with all their friends. It's important that the au pair can deal with the level of commotion."
 Their current au pair -- 27-year-old Rachel Buckle from Yorkshire, England -- says she adjusted easily.
 "I got involved in the children's sports, and I think they saw me as somebody who they could play with rather than somebody who told them what to do all the time," said Ms. Buckle. "You've got to find the balance between being the advisor and the big sister."
 Ms. Buckle says she always wanted to live abroad, and America was the top country on her list. She earned a degree in art and design five years ago in England, and wants to teach arts and crafts in schools. She takes jewelry design classes at the Farmington Valley Arts Center.
 Each au pair is allotted $500 a year for tuition reimbursement, paid by the family, and Ms. Cartledge says most of the young women attend community colleges. Families also pay their au pairs $115 to $125 a week, and give Au Pair in America $3,950 to participate in the program (a fee that covers recruitment expenses, air transportation, a four-day training program, insurance and support from a community counselor). The au pairs are expected to work a maximum of 45 hours a week, and are given two weeks vacation. That schedule tends to be flexible.
 "During the school year it's hard for me to keep to 45 hours a week," Ms. Heagney explains. "But we make up for it in the summer."
 Ms. Buckle, with a month off this summer, is traveling to Cape Cod and Mexico. She will miss this type of freedom, she says, when her year comes to an end. "You have no bills to pay and no major worries," she said. But most of all, she said, whe will miss the Heagney family. "I've grown very attached to them."
 Disrupting the lives of young children by having one au pair leave and another jump into the family could be stressful, but the transitions have gone smoothly for Cindy Keegan of Norwalk and her children.
 "It's amazing that there was never any crying or sadness when an au pair left," Ms. Keegan says of the seven au pairs she has had. "My children were like 'O.K., we know you have to go.' They accept the fact that an au pair will leave but that they'll keep in touch."
 Indeed, Ms. Keegan may sometimes feels as if she is running a hotel for French tourists. Each of her au pairs has been from France, and all have come back to visit.
 "Every summer now we have a French barbecue and invite all our French friends," says Ms. Keegan, who learned from her group that the French don't actually grill anything for a barbecue; instead they serve cold salads and wine.
 The Keegans adapted other French customs into their lives. During Easter they celebrate with the Easter mouse instead of a bunny. At Christmas the Keegan children -- Jeffrey, 11; Daniel, 8, and Maggie, 6 -- leave shoes under the tree instead of hanging stockings. They also eat later at night and use candlelight.
 The family has influenced their au pairs too. "When they get to the United States they start dressing more casually, they eat when they want to eat, and they stop ironing their clothes," says Ms. Keegan. "Their parents cringe, but it's very stimulating for the girls."
 Ms. Keegan, a single mother, said she originally associated au pairs with "wealthier families," but as found financial as well as other advantages in the au pair program.
 "The stress level in the house, and my own physical health, is like night and day compared to our route without an au pair," she said. "She gets the kids off to school, has a snack ready for them while they do their homework, vacuums, does the laundry and starts dinner. The day-to-day stress is off my shoulders."
 One of her concerns, still, is when the au pairs go out at night to socialize. The Au Pair in America program organizes monthly meetings, events and trips for the young women, who also get together on their own. But Ms. Keegan says she has only one car and can't afford to have anything happen to it.
 "I haven't had one girl who was a drinker or heavy partier, but I did have one au pair who stayed out late," Ms. Keegan said. "I gave the car a curfew because I didn't want it out at three or four in the morning."
 Both Ms. Keegan and Ms. Heagney point out that living in Connecticut is a big draw for the au pairs because the state is close to New York City, Boston, Philadelphia and many other tourist spots. They also say the European young women are mature, intelligent and motivated.
 "Because the economy is so poor in France," Ms. Keegan points out, "they're highly educated and hard workers but they just can't find a job over there. So they're not coming here out of boredom."
 Cathy Jacquemart, 23, Ms. Keegan's current helper, studied at a secretarial school in France and wants to work at a hotel reception desk.
 "If I have the opportunity to work in the United States I would like to stay," she said. "I don't miss France, because it's quite hard for young people to find a job. I like it here. It's very easy to live. Everything in France is expensive."
 Ms. Jacquemart admits there are a few things she's found difficult about life in the United States. For one, the food. ("It's horrible here. I put on so much weight from eating junk food it's amazing.") And the weather. ("I hate all the snowing and raining. I live in the South of France where it's always beautiful.").
 But she feels at home with the Keegans. "I'm part of the family, like a daughter. I'm not like an employee."

GRAPHIC: Photos: Cathy Jacquemart, of France, helps Cindy Keegan, of Norwalk, with her children: Jeffrey, 11, Daniel, 8, and Maggie, 6. (Janet Durrans for The New York Times)
