The New York Times

PUB-DATE: July 28, 1996

SECTION: Section 13; Page 01; Column 3; The City Weekly Desk

LENGTH: 3279 words

HEADLINE: The Chinese Menu Guys

BYLINE: By JANE H. LII

 BODY:
ZHU Q. JUEN is a mild-mannered 39-year-old immigrant from Fujian, China with a ready smile. But every afternoon, Mr. Zhu, who has delivered Chinese food for the last three years, joins the army of other deliverymen known as the Bicycle Brigade and goes into warrior mode. He flings a happy-face plastic shopping bag over the handlebars of his black mountain bike, races through the canyons of the Upper West Side, and charges his way into some of its best-guarded fortresses, bringing meals to those who have ordered them and menus to those who have not. Along the way, he dodges speeding buses and swerving cars and braves baseball bats, lit cigarettes and angry fists.
 Mr. Zhu acknowledges the job has turned him into Public Enemy No. 1 for those Upper West Siders eager to eradicate the menu menace. Distributing menus is the least favorite part of his work, he says, but he needs the job to pay off the remaining $10,000 of the $30,000 he paid to the men who smuggled him into this country. The ephemeral dinner rush, from which he makes most of his money, doesn't begin until 6 P.M. and lasts only until 10:30 P.M. And this being summer, when people prefer going out to ordering in, there isn't much money to be made on food runs, leaving even more time to fill distributing menus. 

 "What can I do?" he asked one recent afternoon as he darted from brownstone to brownstone in the 80's under the blazing sun, waves of sweat rolling down his face.
 Before Mr. Zhu could finish, Bruce Thomasahauer, a relationship coach, rushed out of his garden apartment. "Hey, cool it," Mr. Thomasahauer growled, crunching the menus into a ball and tossing them into the garbage can.
 Mr. Zhu bowed and smiled. "Thank you," he said, before dashing away.
 "I understand they need to make a living," Mr. Thomasahauer said, as he whipped out a collection of recent releases. "This is the fifth menu from the same restaurant in the last two weeks. One would last me a couple of years. There is no reason for them to keep this up."
 Today, as the appetite for takeout food continues to expand, neighborhood residents' tolerance for unwanted menus -- Indian, pizza, but mostly Chinese -- keeps dropping. "No Menu" signs in different languages and styles, a new urban art form, adorn building after building. Menus are torn, burned and thrown back with a passion once reserved, perhaps, for opposing foreign wars.
 Nowhere is this struggle more pronounced than the Upper West Side. As torrents of fliers appear daily and nightly, the residents have fought back. They have taken their complaints to court and to the streets, the community boards, elected officials and anyone with a sympathetic ear.
 They have lobbied to have their cause written into law, boycotted the worst offenders, and when laws failed them, engaged in hand-to-hand combat and even served time for the cause. But much to their chagrin, none of their tactics seem to work. No matter how loud they protest, the tidal wave of menus just keeps rolling in.
 Two years ago, Assemblyman Scott Stringer introduced a bill that would quadruple the current fine of up to $250 for distributing menus and other fliers on private properties that explicitly oppose them. But the bill has yet to be voted on because, since it would apply only to New York City, approval by the Council is necessary before it can be submitted to the Assembly. And Council members have expressed concerns over freedom of speech.
 By all accounts, Empire Szechuan restaurants are the most recalcitrant menu spreaders. Despite repeated promises from owners not to distribute them, their menus appear mysteriously underneath doors and inside vestibules.
 To the dismay of those living in buildings without doormen, menus are sometimes used to prop open the entrance door, allowing anyone in.
 "What makes this whole thing so frustrating is that no one takes the responsibility for this and yet the menus just keep on coming and coming," said Saul Lapidus, who owns a building in the West 70's.
 After years of gentle persuasion with no satisfaction, Mr. Lapidus finally took Empire Szechuan to Small Claims Court asking compensation for the cost of disposing of the menus. In March 1994, a judge awarded him $440.
 Officially, the owners and managers of Chinese restaurants on the West Side say they no longer distribute menus, even though they say the more menus they put out the better their business. But the army of bag-toting deliverymen roaming the city tells a different story.
 
 
Stuck in the Middle

 
 Caught between take-no-prisoners owners who equate menu distribution with profits and angry residents eager to fight back are the deliverymen. They must be aggressive enough to push, lie and sneak their way into as many buildings as possible, lest they be branded incompetent by the supervisors who sometimes surreptitiously trail them in cars, but they don't want to risk broken noses. If they are assaulted, as has happened frequently, forget about support from the owners. They have been known to tell angry protesters that the men were distributing the menus against their orders.
 Without hesitation, most of these men say their job is hellish. But with virtually no English, and with tens of thousands of immigrants flooding the job market, they say they feel fortunate just to have a job.
 In close-knit Chinese restaurant circles, distrust reigns supreme, and gossip and innuendo ricochet back and forth across town. To be hired, a person must have an impeccable reputation and a verbal guarantee, which in the Chinese tradition carries more weight than a written agreement, from a boss or a trusted employee.
 Mr. Zhu came with such a guarantee three years ago from a fellow villager who was also a deliveryman. Now, Mr. Zhu has become a trusted employee himself and the friend is a partner in a nearby takeout restaurant, Fat Ming, on Amsterdam Avenue near 92d Street.
 Relaxing in Riverside Park on his day off last week, Mr. Zhu, who permitted a reporter to accompany him provided his restaurant not be identified, envisions a similar career path for himself.
 "I have $10,000 left on my debt," he said after calculating with his fingers. "It'll take another year at the most. Then, I'll save enough money to open my own store. I'm going to start small with a takeout restaurant. Hopefully, I will have a real restaurant and some import and export business in a few years.
 "Then," Mr. Zhu said, breaking into a smile, "I will bring my daughter and my wife over."
 But for now, Mr. Zhu, who earns about $1,800 a month, must be content sharing a studio apartment on 97th Street with two nephews and two fellow villagers and putting in his 12 hours of work, six days a week.
 Shortly after he rises around 11:30 A.M., Mr. Zhu walks to his restaurant and heads straight for the kitchen to join the half-dozen or so deliverymen for a quick lunch of rice, stewed meat and a vegetable dish and some quick gossip about what happened in the world of delivery the night before. This is the time to find out who got the biggest tip, who was robbed or beaten and what's up with their favorite customers, the call girls who work out of an elegant apartment near the park.
 (These women are well liked because they are the nicest customers, Mr. Zhu hastened to explain. They tip well and correct his English, even teaching him a few words, which he declined to repeat when asked. "They have a big heart," Mr. Zhu said. "They talk to us and smile at us. They treat us like human beings.")
 After lunch, the men prepare for battle. They first stuff stacks of menus into brown paper grocery bags, which are then stuffed into plastic bags with a yellow happy face. Rags and crumpled paper are then placed on top to hide the menus from view.
 "Most doorman buildings won't let you go in if they see menus in your bag," Mr. Zhu said as he filled a plastic container with steaming Chinese tea, which would quench his thirst in a couple of hours of the scorching sun.
 By now, the men have memorized the buildings that are absolute no-no's. In an apartment complex in Harlem, security guards escort menu deliverers out of the building in handcuffs.
 "We don't go in there anymore," Mr. Zhu said.
 And then, there are borderline places where it's all right to distribute as long as you don't get caught, like the blocks of brownstones.
 When he reached his destination on West 113th Street, Mr. Zhu ran up the steps to ring doorbells.
 "U.P.S.," he answers cheerfully when a voice inquires.
 The door buzzes open. Mr. Zhu slides in.
 Once inside, he is careful not to go to the floor of the person who has buzzed him in, partly out of guilt, partly for fear of retribution.
 Starting on the highest floor, Mr. Zhu slides the menu underneath each door with Olympian speed and agility. He then hops down the stairs two steps at a time to another floor and repeats the process until he reaches the last apartment.
 Less than two minutes after he leaves the third floor, a thunderous groan is heard from an apartment there. "Oh no!"
 Close call. But no action.
 Right before leaving the building, Mr. Zhu arranges a stack of menus neatly on the radiator in the lobby.
 "I don't like to leave a mess," he says. "It helps to have a radiator."
 On to the next building. No fights. No injuries.
 A lucky day.
 By the time Mr. Zhu finished the block, sweat had soaked through his white shirt. Later, on a bench in Riverside Park, he savored the warm tea and took a good rest before returning to start his food run.
 An hour later, another group of five men crammed into a beat-up Celica and headed to Harlem. Without much ado, the men split into three groups and two men entered Delano Village on 139th Street in Harlem. With each man taking half of a building, apartments on 17 floors were blanketed with nearly 200 menus. A young woman greeted them with indifference in the hallway.
 
 
Lonely and Largely Alone

 
 Once the legion of deliverymen roaming Manhattan consisted of students and immigrants of Chinese ancestry from Malaysia, Taiwan and Hong Kong. But now, Fujianese immigrants, most of them smuggled into this country during the early 1990's, have completely taken over the trade. Their sheer numbers and desperate economic situations have made it possible for employers to lower the industry wage by at least 20 percent in the last five years. The basic salary in some of the city's better Chinese restaurants for deliverymen with private cars has fallen from $1,100 a month to $900.
 With their wives and children left behind and only a few worldly possessions filling several plastic shopping bags that are kept around their beds, most of these men lead lonely, solitary lives. But this particular group of men on West 97th Street, where many deliverymen live, have established a close bond, trading tips on subjects from the best places for their mountain bikes to sales in Chinatown. Laughter and words of encouragement fill the empty spaces between bunk beds. When the menu war gets tough in mid-afternoon, they retreat here for a short nap. On their days off, one man does the Chinatown supply run for the whole group, and they feast on what he brings back.
 "At least with this job we can make enough to survive," said Li W. Xiao. "You can do twice the work and make half the money in a garment factory."
 Until a year ago, Mr. Li, who was smuggled into America just four years ago, thought he was on top of the world. Inspired by the American entrepreneurial spirit, he and his brother took a gamble and borrowed $20,000 from friends and relatives to open a garment factory in Brooklyn. But they did not realize how fierce the competition would be. After they opened, orders never came in enough volume for them to pay the bills. They shut down the factory six months ago. Now, in addition to his smuggling debt, Mr. Li owes $10,000 more.
 "In this job, at least you don't have to worry about cutting paychecks for other people," he said. "This way, I can have a clear conscience."
 
 
All This, for $25 a Day

 
 Because he uses his own car for delivery, Mr. Li says he is paid somewhere from $900 to $1,100 a month plus tips, which average about $40 a night. But he must pay his own gas, which runs $150 to $200 a month. Those on bicycles, like Mr. Zhu, get $25 a day plus tips. Workers on the late-night shift, which ends at 2 A.M., receive an additional $300. Between tips and wages, the workers say they average $5 to $7 an hour.
 The best part of the day starts after 5 P.M., when the food runs start. From then on, they earn real money. But even this is far from ideal.
 To save time and keep themselves from being hit by reckless drivers on the road, the bicycle deliverymen ride on the sidewalks and ignore traffic lights, much to the dismay of pedestrians.
 But when they do ride on the street, cars cut close, sometimes knocking them down.
 "Yo, get a life," shouted a driver in a blue Mercedes after nearly mowing Mr. Zhu down on a recent Saturday. The passengers howled with laughter as the car zoomed away.
 Marks on either side of Mr. Zhu's face reveal the danger of his job. In three years, he has been hit once by a bus, once by a car, and robbed countless times.
 To many customers, a deliveryman ranks somewhere between a waiter, to whom a 15 percent tip is owed, and a bus boy, who is owed nothing.
 Unlike waiters who trot back and forth between the table and the kitchen in full view of the customer, a deliveryman's work is largely done out of sight. Although they pack the food into bags and then ride their bicycles at breakneck speed to insure it gets to the customer quickly, many customers feel no need to tip beyond $1 regardless of the order.
 One recent Saturday, when Hurricane Bertha toyed and teased Manhattanites with rain and strong winds, a couple in a fancy Central Park West apartment called in a $45 order. When Chen Rong arrived from 97th Street completely drenched, the man gushed with concern about Mr. Chen's health, cautioning him about catching a cold.
 Finally, the man took out four shiny quarters and dropped them one by one into Mr. Chen's hand.
 "Now young man, just remember," the man droned on. "Hard work will bring you rewards. This will make you rich. Now, goodbye, and stay dry."
 Forcing a smile, Mr. Chen bowed and said, "Thank you."
 As he was leaving the marbled lobby, Mr. Chen said deliverymen who dared to glower after a bad tip have prompted complaining phone calls from customers. Some have been dismissed, he said.
 Distance is no guarantee of a good tip either. After hustling through rush hour traffic that stretched a 13-mile round trip into 40 minutes, Zhang A. Sheng received $1 on an $30 order.
 "I dread these trips," said Mr. Zhang with visible agitation. "The tip doesn't even pay for gas. The boss wants good attitudes. How can I smile?"
 Then, reaching into his glove compartment, Mr. Zhang took out a traffic ticket, courtesy of an Upper East Side medical office manager who had refused to come down to pick up food. Mr. Zhang had to double park while he took the food up. On that trip, he earned a $5 tip for a $150 order.
 But there are plenty of sympathetic souls left in the city.
 On a $15 order, Okolo Schwinn-Clanton gave Mr. Chen a $4 tip.
 "I used to deliver pizza myself," Mr. Schwinn-Clanton, now in corporate life, said humbly.
 And on a $60 order, Pam Spitzer, a freelance editor, gave Mr. Zhu a $10 tip. "They work hard to make a living," Ms. Spitzer said. "I figure I'll do my part."
 But luck is not often with Mr. Zhu. Two days later, on a run to a Riverside Drive apartment near 97th Street, a group of men jumped on him and put a knife to his stomach, taking about $100.
 "It happens all the time," Mr. Zhu said the day after. "But you have to keep going."
 With a forceful push of his right foot, he mounted the bike and began another trip.
 
The Delivery War: In the Trenches
 
 ROMANCE was in the air on the hot, muggy August Sunday in 1994 and Philip Carlo, a writer, was walking out of his building on West 88th Street with an old flame.
 But the date soured when he spotted a lanky Chinese man putting menus from a restaurant called China Barbecue in his vestibule, ignoring the bilingual "No Menu" signs he had spent $30 to put up.
 "I told the guy to stop and gave him back the menus," Mr. Carlo said. The delivery man put them back. Mr. Carlo picked up the menus again. The delivery man put them back again. A shoving match broke out. Fists flew and the two men spilled out on to the street.
 The man suffered a broken jaw; Mr. Carlo a bloody nose.
 Mr. Carlo, who was then finishing "Night Stalker," his recently published book about Richard Ramirez, the Satan-worshipping mass murderer, was convicted of assault and sent to Rikers Island for 60 days. Charges were dropped against the delivery man.
 It was the bloodiest skirmish to date in the great Upper West Side Menu War.
 With drugs, homelessness and other more-pressing issues plaguing their neighborhoods, it may seem baffling that so many Upper West Siders are so offended by something as innocuous as Chinese restaurant menus. But Saul Lapidus, who has organized protests against all fliers, cites these dangers:
 * Uncollected menus indicate that nobody is home, an invitation to crime.
 * Building owners are often socked with fines from the Sanitation Department for not picking up the menus fast enough from their sidewalks.
 * People who live in buildings with marble floors say they fear they will slip on a menu and seriously injure themselves.
 * It is bad for the environment to waste so much paper.
 When the issue first came up in the 1980's, tolerant Upper West Siders were gentle in their opposition to the menus. "Please do not leave menus," they asked nicely. And when it finally dawned on them that the Chinese immigrants may not understand English , they had the signs translated into Chinese, some of which were promptly stolen. They complained directly to the restaurants, who promised to stop the menu distribution. But the menus kept coming.
 "They'll invite you over and listen politely to what you have to say and then they'd continue doing exactly what they were doing," Mr. Lapidus said. "They are a community business but they have no respect for the community's wishes."
 But Misa Chang, a partner of Empire Szechuan Gourmet, said those who complain do not give the restaurant enough credit for its role in revitalizing the area in the last 20 years.
 "The area was so bad back then that people just didn't come out," said Ms. Chang, who claims to have pioneered the delivery concept 20 years ago while the restaurant was a mere six-table hole in the wall. "I would bring them food in the snow and leave some menus with them. They were grateful to have had this."
 Although they concede that menus are a nuisance, owners and restaurant managers say they cannot afford to stop the practice because they bring in significant business.
 A manager at Hunan Balcony at Broadway and 98th Street who would not give his name said takeouts account for two-thirds of their total business.
 Betty Shau, the wife of another partner of Empire Szechuan, James Shau, said the restaurant has stopped distributing menus to those buildings that object and have resorted to bulk mailing.
 But on a recent day, underneath his "No Menu" sign, Mr. Carlo came home to yet another menu from Empire Szechuan.
 "You just can't win," he shook his head, picking up the menu and throwing it into the garbage. JANE H. LII

SECTION: Section 13NJ; Page 01; Column 1; New Jersey Weekly Desk

LENGTH: 2494 words

HEADLINE: Mountain Time

BYLINE: By DAVID W. CHEN

DATELINE: MONTVILLE

 BODY:
David Moore, who was leading the hike up Pyramid Mountain on a crystal summer day, stopped at a clearing, climbed onto a rock and admired the view. It was something to brag about, all right. Below was a summer camp surrounded by tidy squares of farmland. To the right, the dappled waters of Deer Lake. And in the distance, green ridges undulating all the way to the Manhattan skyline.
 "The first reaction people have when they come up here is, 'Wow, is this New Jersey?' " said Mr. Moore, executive director of the New Jersey Conservation Foundation. 

 He was standing somewhere near the geographic center of what may be New Jersey's least noted, least understood natural region: the New Jersey Highlands. Covering 750,000 acres of farmland, pristine watersheds, dense hard-wood forests and rugged, rolling mountains, the Highlands region is bigger than Yosemite National Park, spanning 7 counties and 83 towns.
 Its reservoirs, streams and rivers supply water to more than half of the state's population. It is home to 38 endangered or threatened species -- the short-nose sturgeon, Cooper's hawk, the blue-spotted salamander. In 1992, a study by the United States Department of Agriculture called it "a landscape of national significance, rich in natural resources and recreational opportunities."
 But that landscape is changing at a breathtakingly fast, some say alarming, pace.
 The change is difficult to measure or manage, because it is not spreading in any organized, concentric fashion but is popping up in random housing developments and strip malls -- "holes being punched all over the Highlands," as Mr. Moore put it. (In a few years, the view he stopped to admire at Pyramid Mountain will include a development of 400 luxury houses now being carved into its foothills.)
 Nowhere in the Highlands has the prospect of change received more attention than in Sterling Forest, 20,000 acres of mountains, wetlands and lakes straddling the New York State line. While the forest's 2,000-acre New Jersey section has been bought by Passaic County, the two states and the Federal Government have been locked in seemingly endless negotiations over buying the 18,000 acres in New York from a private concern that wants to build housing there.
 But Sterling Forest, for all the focus on it, is only one critical area of many. In the New Jersey Highlands, as much as 10,000 acres (the equivalent of half a Sterling Forest) is being developed piecemeal every year -- in a region that has seen a 60 percent increase in urbanization in the last 25 years and is expected to absorb a 14 percent increase in population in the next 20.
 Not everyone is alarmed by this prospect. Developers, along with many local officials, point out that development is simply a reflection of the demand for housing, which can be built more economically (and on larger lots) in the rural Highlands than in the built-up suburbs. To deny that demand, they say, would be artificial -- and might well run afoul of the State Supreme Court's Mount Laurel mandate that every developing town absorb its share of the regional need for low-cost housing.
 "Some people have created the illusion that this is a remote pristine area, where in fact it is part of the Northeast Corridor and has a tremendous number of jobs and people and growth right now," said Joanne Harkins, director of land-use planning for the New Jersey Builders Association.
 Environmentalists disagree, saying the Highlands are unique and fragile. "The Highlands, together with the Pinelands and the Delaware Bay, are the the last places New Jersey has left of its original landscape, and they are probably New Jersey's last opportunity to do something from a land-use standpoint that permits some of it to remain for generations," said Terrence D. Moore, who was the first executive director of the corporation that manages Newark's watershed in the Highlands and is now executive director of the State Pinelands Commission. "The question is whether you can do something in the Highlands on time."
 Governor Whitman has described the Highlands as "New Jersey's last frontier," adding that "it is an ecological imperative that we put a premium on preservation and conservation." But it is far from clear how much of a premium the state, or any government, can afford.
 Unlike the Palisades a century ago, the Highlands cannot simply be bought up and preserved. Only 15 percent of the region remains in public hands, and the number of private landowners is growing: the average size of a parcel of land has fallen sharply over the years, to 12.8 acres in 1988 (the last year available) from 22 acres in 1972. And unlike the Pine Barrens two decades ago, the Highlands probably cannot expect extensive land regulation, given the current push for decentralized state government and the strength of home rule.
 A growing number of planners and environmentalists, with some support from local officials, are promoting a third alternative for the Highlands: protection on a smaller scale, perhaps town by town. In an approach that has had some success in the Pinelands, as well as Long Island and Maryland, environmentally sensitive lands would be protected in exchange for denser "cluster" development on other land.
 "Nobody doubts the benefit of cluster zoning as opposed to your more tradtional 5- or 10-acre zoning, which is a cookie-cutter landscape not leaving any core area for habitat or watershed," said Rose H. Harvey, senior vice president of the Trust for Public Land in New York. "It's not the panacea, but it's a better alternative."
 There are many obstacles to such an approach, notably home rule and resistance from individual landowners. But as the Agriculture Department warned in its 1992 report, "regions that destroy the attractiveness of their landscapes and the health of their ecological foundation will simply not be competitive in the emerging economy of the 21st century -- an economy of businesses seeking places with a high quality of life." Here are three places where the struggle is being joined.
 
WEST MILFORD
Watershed's Watershed
 Since the early 1900's, the City of Newark has owned the Pequannock Watershed, which supplies drinking water for two million New Jersey residents from its reservoirs and forested lands across 35,000 acres and six towns. And nowhere has Newark's presence been felt more than in West Milford, of which Newark owns about 18,000 acres -- 30 percent of the entire township.
 In 1988, the Legislature imposed a moratorium on selling watershed lands after environmentalists protested the sale of Hackensack Water Company land in Bergen County. The moratorium prompted Newark to appeal its tax payments on the Pequannock Watershed, saying its vacant land should not be assessed at full-market rates. The courts agreed, ordering the towns to repay the city and sticking West Milford with a $1.6 million tab.
 That obligation has put intense pressure on the towns to allow more development on forested lands outside the watershed. Tax payments from new development would ease the tax burden on current landowners.
 "We don't view this as an environmental issue, we view this as a tax equity and financial survival issue," said John H. Eskilson, manager of Hardyston Township, which owes Newark $400,000. "We're basically rural with a limited tax base, and a limited ability to grow."
 West Milford has sought to change its zoning to suit the State Development and Redevelopment Plan, which would strengthen any application for state loans and grants. It has had help from the Regional Plan Association, the nonprofit group that studies tristate development issues; last year the association unveiled a plan to cluster new development around Pine Cliff Lake and to preserve all other areas as open space. The plan, complete with computerized 3-D scenarios, also sought to lift the local economy and knit a sprawling township whose de facto center now is an enormous, gray Shop Rite that looks like a Stalinist relic.
 Linda P. Morgan, director of the Regional Plan Association's New Jersey office, says the best way to achieve these goals is a procedure called transfer of development rights, in which landowners in protected areas are paid not to develop them; the money comes from landowners in other parts of town, who win the right to build denser clusters of housing on their land. The association and the Newark Watershed Conservation and Development Corporation, which manages the watershed lands, want West Milford to shift the development rights from 2,300 acres in the watershed to the proposed town center.
 The township has proposed a variation on that plan, but it has drawn mixed reviews. "It's going to be horrible from a pollution standpoint, it's going to be horrible from a tax standpoint, and it's going to be horrible from a traffic standpoint," said Jeff Tittel, a spokesman for Skylands Clean, an environmental group in Ringwood. "It'll be Paramus in the Pines."
 Even the Regional Plan Association has distanced itself from the township's report. "What we tried to do was give them a positive model that other towns can pick up in other rural areas, said Ms. Morgan. "But there wasn't anything strong enough in their draft report to insure that they would protect the green space, and it seemed like there would be seven centers in town."
 Kevin Byrnes, the township manager, dismisses those concerns, saying there would be ample room for open space as well as retail and residential development. But there is increasing pressure on the Legislature to repeal the 1988 moratorium. That would open as much as 10 percent of the watershed to development, and any regional planning process might fall by the wayside.
 
VERNON
Golf, or Country Inns?
 The debate over Eugene Mulvihill's plan to build a campground and a golf course on Hamburg Mountain in this Sussex County township -- next to his financially troubled but popular Great Gorge and Vernon Valley amusement and skiing area -- is part of a much larger debate: What kinds of recreation are appropriate for the Highlands?
 On one side are those who say activities like golf and skiing are critical to the economy of the Highlands, which already attract eight million visitors a year. On the other are those who worry that too much recreation induces too much development and that chemical runoff from golf courses could seep into the water.
 Mr. Mulvihill's plans, first floated in 1992, were part of a grander vision that included a hotel and conference center, movie theater and shopping center. But local groups objected, saying that when Mr. Mulvihill bought the land from the state in 1986, there were deed restrictions to keep it undeveloped. Now the project is in limbo, contingent on state environmental permits and the financial health of Mr. Mulvihill's Great American Recreation Inc., which filed for bankruptcy earlier this year.
 Neither Mr. Mulvihill nor his son and partner, Andrew, returned calls seeking comment. But Ralph Carnesecchi, Mayor of Vernon Township, said he "would love to see this golf course get off the ground" because it would mean more jobs. "I think you'll attract a quality clientele and I think the natural beauty of the area will be preserved," he said. "Admittedly, it won't be in its pristine state, but as close to that as humanly possible."
 But others say pristine areas are just what will attract tourists. "A golf course is not eco-tourism, because you're looking at an intense use of the land," said Dennis Miranda, special projects coordinator of the New Jersey Conservation Foundation. "You have to artificially create a giant lawn."
 An alternative that seems to please nearly everyone is tourist attractions that capitalize on the Highlands' rural, 19th-century character -- bed-and-breakfast inns, crafts villages. But political realities must be taken into account. "You can't lock up a huge chunk of the state anywhere and say we're going to freeze things," said State Senator Joseph Kyrillos Jr., a Monmouth County Republican who is chairman of the Committee on Natural Resources and Economic Development. "You have to have some economic development, but hopefully the kind of development that won't inordinately change the character of the Highlands."
 
GREENWICH TOWNSHIP
The Plant Next Door
 While West Milford and Vernon have focused on the supply and use of water, Greenwich has been concerned with its disposal.
 In this bucolic part of Warren County, developers have proposed an $8 million sewage treatment plant on a former goat farm in Greenwich (pronounced GREEN-witch), a 10-square-mile township of just 2,200 people. That would enable them to develop a 630-acre industrial park off Route 78.
 The sewage plant would pump 600,000 gallons of treated sewage a day into the Musconetcong River, with a potential for 440,000 more, and supporters say it is badly needed. The only other plant in the area, in Phillipsburg, is approaching its capacity of 3.5 million gallons a day. "We have been artificially held back by zoning, by various regulations, and we have missed opportunities," said Robert J. Schwartz of Land Equity Real Estate Inc. in Lebanon, who organized 16 major investors for the project.
 But the proposed sewage plant and industrial park apparently contravene the anti-sprawl guidelines in the State Development and Redevelopment Plan, which call for the area to remain agricultural, not industrial or commercial. Opponents say the new development would be another blow to a vanishing way of life, and they add that it would divert businesses from nearby Phillipsburg, which is desperate for industry.
 "This project will change the life style as we know it," said Jay Martino, a member of the Greenwich Township Citizens Action Committee. "We already have residential developments. We're under siege to become suburbia."
 There are also fears for the Musconetcong River, a popular trout-fishing ground. Despite developers' assurances that the the treated water being pumped out will actually be cleaner than the water now in the river, residents have appealed to the National Park Service to include the Musconetcong in its Wild and Scenic River System. For now, it is up to the State Department of Environmental Protection to decide whether design and engineering work can begin.
 Which gives Wilma E. Frey some time to put it all in perspective. On a hike recently up Pohatcong Mountain in Greenwich, Ms. Frey, coordinator of the Highlands Coalition, an association of more than 80 environmental groups, came to a spot whose sweeping view of ridges and farmlands recalls Pyramid Mountain, at the other end of the Highlands.
 "I hope we'll see more people who want some regional planning with local regulations," she said. "Otherwise, this could all be just gone, and the whole area will be covered to look like Bergen County and the rest of New Jersey."

HEADLINE: TECHNOLOGY: DIGITAL COMMERCE;
Education Technology recruits a masterful marketer of games to be its leader.

BYLINE: By Denise Caruso 

 BODY:
IT may be a sign of the times when the former president of Sega of America, Thomas Kalinske, leaves his job -- as he announced earlier this month -- to join a start-up called Education Technology L.L.C.
What could it mean when the master salesman of Sonic the Hedgehog takes the top job at a company set up to create and buy companies that sell educational technology? 
 It means that some big-money players have their eyes on what they hope is a very big market for what are called "edutainment" products.
Education Technology was announced in March by its two high-profile founders -- Lawrence Ellison, chairman of the Oracle Corporation, and Michael R. Milken, the former junk-bond financier. They hope to "do well by doing good," as Mr. Ellison puts it, by playing the educational-products market.
As described by Oracle, a leader in corporate data base software, this is an $833 billion worldwide market opportunity. But as it turns out, that number, compiled from various sources by Oracle, counts all the costs of providing education -- salaries, books, supplies, administration -- to schools and universities, government and corporations.
Presumably, edutainment would be a small subset. A report on the United States education market, published by the Software Publishers Association, found that for the 1993-94 school year, the latest data compiled, a total of $2.6 billion was spent in grades kindergarten to 12 for products and services that included personal computers, educational software and courseware, computer supplies and telecommunications connections.
And the association projects an increase to just $4.5 billion by 1999. Nonetheless, a new batch of players is joining the education software field, where the veterans include Wolfram Research, whose Mathematica program revolutionized the way mathematics is taught in many schools; Broderbund Software Inc., and the Learning Company.
Like some of these established players, the new entrants mean to combine education with entertainment. One example is a company called Lightspan Partners of Carlsbad, Calif., which has backers like Microsoft and the cable television giant Tele-Communications Inc., and a management that includes Hollywood screenwriters and animators, as well as education-software executives.
Similarly, the first investment for Mr. Ellison's Education Technology was to buy shares in the toy company Hasbro Inc.
"The Hasbro characters, in an educational context, have enduring value," Mr. Ellison said. "Mr. Potato Head might be able to teach you arithmetic."
But Mr. Ellison is banking on far more than Mr. Potato Head to make education a profit center. "If we can get the price down low enough, and I think we can, we see every kid having a network computer at home and at school -- as common as telephones and televisions -- by the year 2000," he says. "I don't know whether that's realistic, though; it make take until 2005."
This forecast may seem incredible, considering that most of the nation's schools struggle to pay teachers a competitive salary. And even if they could put a network computer on every school desk, few schools yet have the multiple phone lines in every classroom that such machines would require.
Some steps are being taken to correct this situation. Earlier this year, some 100,000 volunteers installed network cabling in 4,000 schools around the nation as part of a continuing program called Net Day 96. But there are some education technology experts who wonder whether the effort is worth the time and trouble.
The best of the educational software available today is excellent, according to Judah L. Schwartz, a professor emeritus at the Massachusetts Institute of Technology and a professor of education and a co-director of the Educational Technology Center at Harvard University.
As for the rest of it, Professor Schwartz says, "The metaphor of the Augean stables is wonderfully appropriate." (In Greek mythology, one of Hercules' tasks was to clean the stables of King Augeas, where celestial horse muck was being produced at a faster rate than Hercules could shovel it.)
But no matter how low the quality, or how high the cost, economics may yet drive technology into the schools, since much of the cost of education is those pesky salaries for teachers -- whose labor many people see as a natural target for automation.
The cost of higher education has tripled over the last decade, mostly because of teacher salaries, according to Robert Heterick, the president of Educom, a consortium of colleges and universities that works to integrate technology into education.
"Today you're looking at a highly personal, human-mediated environment," Mr. Heterick said. "The potential to remove the human mediation in some areas and replace it with automation -- smart, computer-based, network-based systems -- is tremendous. It's gotta happen."
The likeliest candidates include courses like basic math, English and science, which enroll some 50 percent of a university's students and are at the core of any K-12 curriculum. "If I could get a small piece of that market," he says, "there's huge potential out there."
Educators like Professor Schwartz are not so enthusiastic.
"If you think of education as a production-line function, to train the next generation of 'proles' in the Orwellian sense, then you're right," he says. "It is a big market. But the object of schooling is not simply to transmit knowledge. It's to get students to participate in the making of knowledge. That's an essential part of the human experience."
One industry veteran hoping that both perspectives can eventually co-exist in the classroom is Bud Colligan, who once led the education marketing team at Apple Computer. He is now the chairman of Macromedia Inc., which produces Authorware, one of the most popular software programs for creating computer-based training courses.
"You want to keep experimenting and finding the great examples of technology in the classroom," Mr. Colligan says. "But you want to temper your enthusiasm with some reality about the hype."

HEADLINE: Ratings Exceed NBC's Promises For The Games

BYLINE: By BILL CARTER 

 BODY:
At least until Saturday, when a terror bomb moved the nation's attention away from the Olympic competition, the first week of NBC's coverage of the Olympic Games in Atlanta has been among the most successful and profitable weeks in network television history, with ratings reaching levels not seen since Nadia Comaneci was swinging on a bar in the 1976 Olympics in Montreal.
In the first week, NBC exceeded its prime-time Olympics ratings guarantee to advertisers by more than 25 percent, providing a spectacular bonus for both its sponsors and the network. Indeed, ratings for the Olympics are well more than double NBC's previous summertime ratings. 
 Even better for NBC, a big part of the additional viewing has come from one group that the network aimed at especially hard with its Olympic promotions during the last year: younger women, an audience segment most desired by advertisers. After one week, NBC attracted 37 percent more women aged 18 to 34 to these Olympics than it had four years ago with the Olympic Games in Barcelona, Spain, and 67 percent more than the 1988 games in Seoul, South Korea.
NBC, which is owned by the General Electric Company, picked up gains across the board, with 30 to 50 percent increases in its ratings in the morning and late at night. All of those hours were stocked with promotions for coming NBC shows, creating a level of awareness among viewers that network programmers only dream of.
"NBC's numbers are simply phenomenal," said Paul Schulman, of the Paul Schulman Company, which buys media time for advertisers. "It just can't get much better for NBC."
NBC executives hope there will be no further terrorist activities that might threaten to disrupt the Games, and an NBC spokesman said the network had insurance in place to cover any cancellation.
Still, NBC executives are already calculating the positive fallout from the blockbuster performance of the Atlanta Games, and they predict the network may reap the following benefits:
*Its new prime-time shows may post better-than-expected ratings for the opening episodes as a result of the exposure they are getting to the 210 million Americans that NBC predicts will see some part of the games.
*A significant carryover among viewers may push NBC's "Today" show to a dominant position in the morning, enhance the first-place status in late night of the "Tonight" show, and perhaps nudge NBC's "Nightly News" into first place in the evening news competition.
*More cable systems are likely to pick up NBC's two cable channels, CNBC and MSNBC, which will be involved in the coverage of NBC's future Olympic ventures.
*NBC is expected to have the money it needs to keep its Olympic machine rolling -- and perhaps much sooner than the network expected.
NBC has committed itself to spending more than $3.5 billion to lock up five of the next six summer and winter Olympics, three of which -- the games of 2004, 2006, and 2008 -- have not even been doled out to host cities yet. But the tsunami from Atlanta may have already ended most of the economic risk the network faced.
A senior network executive, speaking on condition of anonymity, said that before the games were even five days old, NBC had been "approached by a half-dozen separate multinational advertisers who wanted to discuss multi-Olympic deals."
These deals could put in place a number of the big advertisers, enough to serve as a hedge, NBC executives said, against a future downturn in the television business that was really the only threat to a continued run of profits for NBC from the Olympics.
The Atlanta Games will make money for NBC, said Dick Ebersol, the president of NBC Sports. Mr. Ebersol is the man who made all those Olympic deals, and who conceived NBC's entire Olympic strategy, from acquiring rights far into the future to gearing the programming to appeal especially to women.
These games will make "just under $20 million," Mr. Ebersol said, a success that defies the conventional wisdom that followed NBC's bid of $456 million for the Atlanta rights. NBC won the Atlanta rights in a tight bidding contest against ABC, soon after NBC had lost about $100 million on the 1992 Games in Barcelona after paying $405 million for the television rights.
"I never doubted we'd make money from Atlanta," Mr. Ebersol said in a telephone interview from the NBC broadcast center in Atlanta.
For one thing these Olympics are in the United States, and Mr. Ebersol reckoned that the home turf advantage accounted for about 50 percent of the increased audience.
Moreover, NBC learned many lessons from Barcelona about how to program the games to maximum television effect. (The biggest of those lessons: Aim to draw in young women by telling dramatic stories of athletes, and don't worry at all about critics who complain about packaging events for prime time and pretending they're happening live.)
The first of NBC's future Olympics, the Games in Sydney, Australia, in 2000, represented the biggest leap, requiring a huge increase in the payment for the American television rights, to $705 million. Still, with advertisers already talking about signing up, NBC appears to have validated its long-range plans.
Mr. Schulman said he would advise his advertising clients unhesitatingly to sponsor NBC's future Olympic coverage. "There is nothing on television like it," he said, adding that Sydney represents an even bigger opportunity for NBC because the games will take place in late September and early October, thus assuring that NBC will obliterate all the competition from other networks trying to start a new television season.
"I think I can predict the winner of the 2000-2001 television season right now," Mr. Schulman said.
The current games will end about six weeks before this year's new television season begins, but rival networks are already nervous about the prospect of NBC's already dominant prime-time schedule getting an additional bounce from the Olympics. "It's almost frightening to think of them getting any stronger," the head of a rival network's entertainment division said.
Warren Littlefield, the president of NBC Entertainment, said, "What the performance of this Olympics means is that having climbed the mountain to get on top in prime time, we're getting a lot of help to continue the momentum."
Mr. Littlefield also pointed out that with the Olympics, "you're often reaching people who may have drifted away from network television." He said NBC's program department was making sure that Hollywood producers knew that the network would have hugely rated summer nights every four years on which to promote new shows.
"In program development sessions, we say: 'We've got five of the next six Olympics. If you're impressed with what's happening now, you ought to think about being with us in the future.' "

HEADLINE: Mubarak Visits U.S. to Win Assurances on Arab-Israeli Talks

BYLINE: By NEIL MacFARQUHAR 

DATELINE: CAIRO, July 28

 BODY:
President Hosni Mubarak of Egypt traveled to Washington today to seek assurances that the effort for Arab-Israeli peace will not be deferred by Israel's switch to a Government that is harshly critical of existing agreements and negotiating blueprints.
Mr. Mubarak's visit, through Wednesday, comes at a time when Egyptian-American relations, while not strained, are not particularly warm either. 
 Egypt is uneasy about the commitment by the United States to press Israel toward compromise. And there are differences over sanctions against other Arab countries, over a second term for Boutros Boutros-Ghali, an Egyptian, as United Nations Secretary General, and over Egypt's perception that it is treated more as a dependent than as an ally.
But the question that looms largest is whether the Clinton Administration will be willing in an election year to press Prime Minister Benjamin Netanyahu of Israel to accept the formula previously used in the peace talks -- that Middle East wars will end only when Israel returns land captured from the Arabs in 1967.
"Mubarak's mission is to make the Americans understand that backing away from the land-for-peace formula is harmful not only to the Arab countries, but to Israel and the huge American interests in the region," said Taha Abdel Alim, deputy chairman of Al Ahram Center for Strategic Studies, a Government-supported research center. "If Israel does not withdraw from occupied Arab land, most of the radical groups opposed to the ruling moderate Arab regimes will become stronger and more able to execute terrorist attacks."
Egypt's earlier confrontational attitude toward Mr. Netanyahu was somewhat mollified by the amiable tone he adopted during his visit to Egypt on July 18, his first to an Arab country.
But Egyptian commentators closest to Mr. Mubarak are still wary, and three weeks after Mr. Netanyahu's first visit to Washington as Prime Minister they are still talking about his ardent reception there.
The Arab countries had been hoping to hear some word then that the United States would push the Netanyahu Administration at least to respect the agreement for Israeli troops to redeploy around the West Bank city of Hebron, leaving most of the city under Palestinian control. It was delayed first by a series of bus bombings directed at Israelis that killed 63 people last winter and then by the elections. The Arabs were dumbstruck to watch the United States Congress give Mr. Netanyahu thunderous applause when he said that Jerusalem was not a negotiable issue.
"There should be a stronger commitment on the part of the United States to act as a full partner and an unbiased mediator," wrote Ibrahim Nafie, editor in chief of Al Ahram, the main Government-run daily newspaper.
The final status of Jerusalem, of settlements on the West Bank, of borders and of Palestinian refugees are all supposed to be subject to negotiations between the Palestinians and the Israelis. In addition, Egypt has supported the position that peace with Syria can be achieved only through returning occupied territory.
Along with rejecting negotiating over Jerusalem or a future Palestinian state, Mr. Netanyahu has said that Israel would not withdraw from the Golan Heights, captured fom Syria in the 1967 war.
On regional issues, Mr. Mubarak is likely to raise the point that the United States should be more flexible with regard to economic sanctions on Libya as well as sanctions imposed on Iraq over its invasion of Kuwait. Egypt has taken the position that the two Libyans suspected of blowing up Pan American Flight 103 over Lockerbie, Scotland, in 1988 can go to trial before an international tribunal, while the United States and England insist the two face one of their courts. Libya is under United Nations sanctions for failing to turn the two men over for trial.
Although this month's destruction of T.W.A. Flight 800 has yet to be definitively ascribed to a terrorist attack, suspicion of possible links to the Middle East adds pressure on the two sides to continue their high-level consultation on terrorism issues.
Sheik Omar Abdel Rahman, the spiritual leader of the Islamic Group, is serving a life sentence in the United States for plotting terrorist attacks on New York City landmarks, and he has called on his followers to avenge him. Some of his followers were also convicted in the 1993 World Trade Center bombing. The group also seeks the violent overthrow of the Egyptian Government, and confrontations have left about 1,000 people dead here during the last four years.
On other issues, Egypt considers the attempts by the Clinton Administration to dump Mr. Boutros-Ghali after just one term as Secretary General to be a personal affront. Egypt has been rallying African and other non-Western nations behind Mr. Boutros-Ghali, one of its most respected diplomats.
Finally, every meeting between Presidents Clinton and Mubarak gives occasion for Egyptian political leaders to pronounce that the more than $2 billion in annual aid that the United States gives Egypt should not be cut.
At the same time, they argue with a certain wistful tone that the aid does not mean Egypt must blindly accept American policy in the region -- rather that the two countries are equals. Egyptian analysts said Mr. Mubarak would stress this again in his meeting with Mr. Clinton on Tuesday.
"Giving aid does not give the donor countries the right to treat countries receiving aid as a master treats his slave," wrote Ibrahim Seda, editor in chief of the Government-run weekly Akbar Al Yom.

HEADLINE: Buyouts Leave School Board Racing to Fill 3,000 Jobs

BYLINE: By JACQUES STEINBERG 

 BODY:
For the second consecutive summer, the Board of Education is scrambling to cope with a self-inflicted staffing headache: the departure of thousands of the most experienced teachers in New York City's public schools, who are planning to retire early under a buyout program.
The loss of nearly 10 percent of the city's 64,000 teachers within 14 months -- one of the largest turnovers since 12,000 teachers were laid off in the fiscal crisis of the 1970's -- has opened a host of vacancies, many in positions that are traditionally difficult to fill, in fields like mathematics, science and bilingual education. 
 To plug the gaps, the board has stepped up its recruiting. For the first time, it has sent letters to thousands of unemployed teachers across the state, particularly those licensed in math and science.
And it has initiated an intensive effort to woo young teachers to schools in the city's poorest neighborhoods, enlisting parents, principals and other teachers as a traveling sales force that has visited colleges in New York City and surrounding counties, and then given receptions in the applicants' honor.
So far, officials said, 2,376 teachers, 93 principals, 143 assistant principals and 134 guidance counselors have told the board they plan to retire under the program, which offers additional pension credits as an incentive. In years without a buyout program, about 1,200 teachers usually retire.
This year's departures come as the system continues to grapple with the retirement of 3,100 other veteran teachers last year.
As was the case last year, many of this year's retirees will be replaced by recent college graduates brimming with energy and enthusiasm but, in some cases, lacking state credentials.
All told, there will be more than 3,000 openings for teachers in New York City schools this fall, with the additional openings resulting from normal attrition and jobs created to handle increased enrollment, board officials said.
"When you look at the volume involved, and the turnover, it's a monumental task to see that you have the best-quality teachers serving our children," said Gary Barton, the board's deputy executive director of human resources.
To that end, the board has also expanded other recruiting strategies begun in recent years. It continues to staff a part-time office in Puerto Rico, where 300 candidates for bilingual education posts were interviewed this year. It provides a toll-free information number, (800) TEACH-NY, advertised on subways and in college newspapers across the country.
The board works with Teachers College at Columbia University to recruit Peace Corps volunteers returning to the United States. And it is committed to hiring 80 recent college graduates trained by Teach for America, a national clearinghouse for urban school districts.
In recent weeks, as many as 400 applicants a day have passed through the board's hiring office in downtown Brooklyn, and most will be offered jobs, board officials said.
The number of teachers taking the current buyout is expected to grow daily until Aug. 10, the last day to enroll. While teachers have until Aug. 31 to change their minds, only a few hundred did so last year.
While the early retirement program is intended to save money by eliminating some of the schools' highest salaries, it robs many schools of teachers who, like the veteran players on a baseball team, have served as mentors to other teachers for more than a decade. A new teachers' contract prohibits layoffs for three years.
"The people you are most likely to lose are those with the highest competency," said Frank Smith, a professor in the department of educational administration at Teachers College. "To do that across the system drains it of the expertise it needs to run quality programs."
Many of those leaving have logged more than 20,000 hours in the classroom. One is Martin Abramson, 53, who taught junior high for 33 years, 32 of them at Intermediate School 78 in Mill Basin, Brooklyn.
Tempted by the opportunity to retire two years early, with a bonus of one month of service credited to his pension for each year he worked in the system, Mr. Abramson said he needed little persuading.
"This is something I've always wanted," said Mr. Abramson, who doubted he would miss the classroom. "I want to play golf, tennis and travel, anyplace and everywhere." His first trip, he said, will be a cruise through the Panama Canal.
Filling such openings will be relative newcomers like Mike Swirsky, 24, a graduate of the University of Central Florida in Orlando.
Mr. Swirsky, a substitute teacher in District 21 on the southern tip of Brooklyn last year, learned last week that the board had tentatively approved a full-time assignment for him. He will teach elementary school in nearby District 18, provided he clears a final interview with district interviewers on Aug. 28.
"I just love teaching," Mr. Swirsky said, clutching the piece of paper confirming his appointment.
Like Mr. Swirsky, most of those newly hired already have state certification, which requires them to have passed undergraduate courses in education and worked as student teachers. But board officials concede that there will be far more openings than qualified applicants in courses like high school math and science.
In such cases, the jobs will be filled by recent college graduates who majored in those subjects but have no teaching experience. They will be hired conditionally if they promise to complete the education courses required for certification and earn master's degrees in five years, board officials said.
Working closely with them will be trainers from the board, local school districts and the United Federation of Teachers.
The board has relied on such uncertified teachers in the past but said it had pared the number to 7,500 last year, from 14,000 in 1990.
In assessing the board's replacement of experience with youth over the last two years, Lewis H. Spence, the deputy chancellor for operations, said he saw "a mixed story."
"Some proportion of the retirees have grown tired of the task and are no longer bringing to the kids what they were once able to bring," he said. "So the renewal is a good thing."
"But," he added, "learning to excite a classroom and assess students' capacity takes time."

HEADLINE: A Chastened Latin Left Puts Its Hope in Ballots

BYLINE: By LARRY ROHTER 

DATELINE: SAN SALVADOR, July 28

 BODY:
The Communists were here, and so were the Intransigents, the Laborites, the Radicals and the Socialists. Former Sandinista, Tupamaro and Montonero guerrillas were here too, all under one roof to discuss the issue that has preoccupied the Latin American left throughout this decade: how to rebuild in a world dominated by capitalism and the United States.
A decade ago, such a conference might have taken place in Havana and been accompanied by ringing declarations of the inevitability of the triumph of socialism. 
 But when they met here this weekend, leaders of the Latin American left had to content themselves with more modest goals, and seemed more reflective than boastful.
All told, representatives of 112 parties and political groupings from more than 20 Latin American and Caribbean countries gathered here for a three-day conference that ended today. While there were many differences about which path was the most appropriate to take, there was general agreement that recent years have been full of unexpected setbacks and difficulties.
The decade opened with the startling defeat of the Sandinistas in the 1990 presidential election in Nicaragua. Since then, Fidel Castro has reluctantly embraced some capitalist measures in an effort to keep his revolution alive, the guerrilla insurgency here in El Salvador has lain down its arms, and former leftists in good standing like Fernando Henrique Cardoso in Brazil and Juan Bosch in the Dominican Republic have seen their parties come to power after adopting more centrist positions.
The left also finds itself under attack in a caustic and influential new book called "Manual of the Perfect Latin American Idiot," published in Spain four months ago.
Mocking intellectuals, liberation theologians, reflexive anti-Americanism and revolutionary nostalgia in a biting 300-page jeremiad, the book argues that the Latin American left is inflexible and has thus become archaic and largely irrelevant.
Rather than examine their own doctrines, Latin American leftists continue "looking for alibis and scapegoats" like the World Bank, the International Monetary Fund, the Central Intelligence Agency and multinational companies, the Peruvian novelist Mario Vargas Llosa argues in the preface to the book, which was written by his son Alvaro and Colombian and Cuban colleagues. That allows the left "always to feel in the comfortable position of victims and, in all good conscience, to eternalize its error."
At the opening ceremonies at the National Gymnasium here on Friday, there was much that the three authors would no doubt have found laughable.
Members of the Farabundo Marti National Liberation Front, the host party of the event, waved red flags and banners proclaiming "The Struggle Continues" as a high school marching band, with cheerleaders and majorettes with pompoms, pounded out American rock-and-roll songs from the Elvis Presley era.
Outside the arena, vendors wearing the black beret that Che Guevara made famous sold cassettes of revolutionary songs, memoirs by veterans of a half-dozen guerrilla wars and T-shirts showing Lenin in profile and bearing the slogan "Only for those in the know." But neither was there a shortage of Coca-Cola, the beverage the left regards as the embodiment of American imperialism.
Inevitably, there was much splitting of hairs over what should be contained in a statement of principles. One of the more controversial topics was whether to congratulate China, Vietnam, Laos, North Korea and Cuba for their efforts "to develop their own projects for the advancement of the socialist alternative."
If one thing was clear during three days of workshops and debate, though, it was that the Latin American left's 30-year obsession with armed struggle as a means to revolution has come to an end.
Even the leaders of the last major guerrilla insurgency in attendance here, the Guatemalan National Revolutionary Unity, were preaching the virtues of politics by more conventional means.
"Objectives vary according to historical circumstances," Rolando Moran, a Guatemalan guerrilla commander, said in an interview. "The armed struggle had as its objective the seizure of power by assault. For the moment, that is not feasible, so we must develop our political struggle."
In place of bullets, leaders of leftist parties expressed a commitment to reaching power through the ballot box. But to do that, they acknowledged, they must change some of their most traditional and deeply held beliefs.
"We are looking for positions that will allow us to win the support of the voter in the middle, the ordinary citizen, without losing the support of the organized left," explained Porfirio Munoz Ledo, a leading strategist of Mexico's Revolutionary Democratic Party. "It is not enough to fill the stadiums and the squares. We also have to fill the ballot box."
That, of course, implies a return to grass-roots organizing, at which the Latin American left has always excelled. Many speakers emphasized the importance of electing mayors, state governors and legislators at all levels, from city councils up to national congresses, as a necessary prelude to some day winning a presidential election.
"We need to convince society that we have the competence to govern," Luis Inacio da Silva, the Brazilian trade union leader and former presidential candidate of his country's Workers' Party, said in an interview. "People still think we only know how to march, strike and protest, so we need to show them that we can also exercise power through realistic programs adapted to the conditions of each country."
The meeting was held under the auspices of the Sao Paulo Forum, a Brazilian-sponsored regional organization.
As expected, the air rang with attacks on the experiments with the market policies that one Latin American country after another has undertaken, what in the jargon of the left is known as "neo-liberalism."
But in a reflection of Mr. da Silva's call for pragmatism, there were also workshops focusing on issues that were rarely part of the traditional discourse of the left during the years of armed struggle, like the environment, feminism and migration.
But the participants showed few signs of taking a less romantic or more skeptical view of the Cuban Revolution, whose triumph in 1959 marked the start of the fascination with armed struggle.
Though Marco Aurelio Garcia, the main organizer of the forum, argued that Cuba is not "a model or a paradigm for us," Cuban Communist Party representatives were treated with great deference, and the opening ceremonies featured a wildly applauded "Dance for Cuba" performed by a local ballet troupe.
Some former guerrillas who have already made a leap toward the center argue that the continued willingness to overlook Cuba's failures robs the left of credibility.
"It does us damage" and hands a weapon to the right, said Ana Guadalupe Martinez, a former guerrilla commander here who has now split with many of her colleagues to become a leader of the new Democratic Party, which chose not to take part in the conference.
"They are still mired in attacks on the system and arguing the innate goodness of leftist ideas," Ms. Martinez, who is now a vice president of this country's Parliament, said of the parties gathered here. "They still do not realize they have to change not only their strategy, but also their ideas if they hope to compete with the right."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Buyers were snapping up the Che Guevara T-shirts this weekend at a gathering of Latin American leftists in San Salvador. There were the usual disputes, but everyone agreed that these are hard times for the left. (Cindy Karp for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Deadline Nears for Renewal of Chip Pact

BYLINE: By ANDREW POLLACK 

DATELINE: TOKYO, July 28

 BODY:
The United States and Japan have entered last-ditch negotiations to renew a semiconductor trade agreement that expires on Wednesday. If past trade talks are any guide, negotiators will squabble right up until Wednesday's deadline before reaching a vague agreement that will be interpreted as a victory by both sides.
But is all that haggling worth it? There is much less riding on the outcome this time for the microchip industries of either country than there was when the first agreement was signed in 1986, or when it was renewed in 1991. 
 The share of Japan's market held by foreign chip makers has risen from less than 9 percent in 1986, to more than 30 percent today, with American companies accounting for about two-thirds of the imports. With Japan's market now much more open than it was, a new agreement probably will not bring about big gains for American companies or force much more change in the buying habits of Japanese companies.
"We can live with it," an executive at one major Japanese electronics company said.
Similarly, American manufacturers could probably live without a new agreement.
"Most American producers will tell you today that they have a fairly decent crack at the Japanese market," said Kenneth Flamm, an economist at the Brookings Institution and author of "Mismanaged Trade?", a recently published book on international semiconductor competition.
The talks began today in Vancouver, British Columbia, at the vice ministerial level and will be joined on Tuesday by Charlene Barshefsky, the acting U.S. trade representative, and Shunpei Tsukahara, Japan's Minister of International Trade and Industry. Industry representatives from the two countries will meet simultaneously to hammer out an agreement on private-sector cooperation that could be a foundation for the Governments' agreement.
Negotiators from the two countries have been in Vancouver since late last week trying to reach a separate agreement on access to Japan's insurance market, also by a self-imposed deadline of Wednesday.
Japan argues that the existing semiconductor agreement, which set an expectation that foreign producers achieve a 20 percent share of its market, has succeeded and that government intervention in the market should now cease.
Japan not only opposes any new target for market share, it wants to eliminate collection of market share data and monitoring of the market by the two Governments. It wants to replace bilateral consultations with multilateral ones, bringing in Europe and perhaps some other chip-producing countries.
Washington is not calling for a market-share target in a new agreement, but it wants to continue data collection and periodic consultations between the two Governments over access to Japan's market.
"Our view is that the agreement has had a very positive effect but that the progress in market access and industrial cooperation is both recent and somewhat fragile," a senior U.S. trade official said.
Still, some American semiconductor executives have argued in the past that once they cross the 20 percent threshold, they would be so well enmeshed in the fabric of the Japanese electronics industry that they could not easily be pushed out again.
With the industry seemingly having little to gain or lose, analysts say, the negotiations are in large part being driven by the Governments.
President Clinton wants to help his re-election by doing something for Silicon Valley. Japanese trade officials, meanwhile, view the semiconductor agreement as one of their greatest blunders. The inclusion of the 20 percent figure led to sanctions being imposed on Japan by the United States for the first time in 1987, and the chip agreement has served as a model for subsequent American demands for measurable results in other trade agreements.
So the Japanese ministry, one of the world's most notorious practitioners of government intervention in industry, has donned the mantle of free trader to condemn the semiconductor agreement as an abhorrent example of "managed trade."
Japanese officials make no secret of the fact that they would prefer there be no new agreement at all, and at first they refused to even negotiate with Washington. But such a defiant approach could sour overall relations and lead to Japan-bashing in the Presidential campaign.
So, in an effort to allow for some of the government involvement sought by Washington, Japan has proposed creating a multilateral forum that would discuss issues like intellectual property protection, environmental matters and other things besides access to Japan's market. There would also be a separate multilateral forum involving companies. The two groups would meet jointly once a year.
Washington has not rejected those ideas but considers them diversions from the focus on increasing access to Japan's market. Ultimately the United States will probably come away with something that it can say keeps the pressure on Japanese industry to buy foreign chips and keeps the Japanese Government somehow involved. Japan will probably say that it has eliminated managed trade and has given away nothing.

HEADLINE: METRO DIGEST

 BODY:

THE CRASH
 
Piecing Together a Crash
The front end of Trans World Airlines Flight 800 broke away and plunged toward the Atlantic Ocean while the rest of the plane continued to hurtle through the air before bursting into an enormous fireball seconds later, investigators said. Several Federal law enforcement officials said the new finding strengthened their belief that a bomb blew off part of the forward section of the plane. [Page A1.]
 
Many of the families still waiting to reclaim their loved ones are choosing to take the first painful step toward reclaiming their lives: they are going home. [B4.]
 
With the Internet, Montoursville, Pa., which lost 21 people on Flight 800, has been helped through its grief by the global village. [B4.]
 
Holes in the Safety Net
Despite the visible reassurance offered by crisply uniformed guards tending metal detectors, security experts re-examining airline safety after the explosion on T.W.A. Flight 800 say one of the weakest links may be the very people who work in the nation's airports. Dozens of workers -- hired with only the most cursory of background checks -- have access to planes waiting on airport tarmacs and, security experts say, could easily plant a bomb aboard a plane. [A1.]
 
A Push for Crucial Evidence
Some divers turned their primary attention to recovering evidence instead of human remains from Flight 800, increasing the hope that they are close to finding the first piece of physical evidence that will resolve whether the fireball in the sky was an accident or a crime. Recovery of human remains is still the priority, an investigator said, and other work will stop when remains are discovered so they can be brought to the surface. But some of the equipment on hand is better suited to bringing up metal, he said. [B5.]
 
Impact of Debris on Searchers
In a week of sweeping the Atlantic, nothing gave the crew of the Driftmaster pause more than a footlong wooden object they spotted amid floating debris from Flight 800. Bobbing in fuel-slicked waves was a whimsical bit of folk art, with the face of a bearded man chiseled into the bark on the curved side. [B5.]
 
NEW YORK
 
School Board Races to Fill Jobs
For the second consecutive summer, the Board of Education is scrambling to cope with a self-inflicted staffing headache: the departure of thousands of the most experienced teachers in New York City's public schools, who are retiring early under a buyout program. The loss of nearly 10 percent of the city's 64,000 teachers in the last 14 months has opened a host of vacancies. To plug the gaps, the board has intensified its recruiting, for the first time sending letters to thousands of unemployed teachers across the state. [B1.]
 
A Lonely Fight to Add a Term
As the City Council begins campaigning to extend the term limits that voters imposed on them and other elected city officials in 1993, it has to grapple with an uncomfortable reality: The borough presidents and other citywide elected officials are showing little inclination to press the issue actively, even though they would stand to benefit from the proposal. Political memo. [B1.]
 
Unsung Chef and His Chicken
With the decline of New York City's waterways for both commercial and recreational boat traffic, the once-busy job of city bridge raiser is not what it used to be. And Leonard C. Thomas, like a number of the 85 other city bridge tenders, has become a necessary anachronism in a water-bound city where someone still needs to raise a bridge if a boat comes by. Some bridge raisers work on a college degree. Others put together small businesses. Mr. Thomas thinks about chicken. He first called his catering company Lenny's Stuffed Chickens, but then, in a confluence of luck and publicity, he changed the name to the Chicken Man. [B1.]
 
Sanctuary Is Marred by Arson
For Jewish mothers and their newborns, the Hilltop Maternity-Convalescent Center in Kiryas Joel, N.Y. -- a Hasidic village of 12,000 ultra-Orthodox Satmars -- was a serene haven for recovery from childbirth. It was a neutral sanctuary, open to all regardless of their loyalties in the squabble dividing the village's neighborhoods. Arson has now shut it down. The crime has reduced the center to just another element in the angry, sometimes violent factionalism of Kiryas Joel. [B1.]
 
Chancellor on SUNY's Ills
Thomas A. Bartlett came out of a brief retirement to take the reins of the State University of New York. Eighteen months later, he abruptly handed the reins back. But in walking away from the leadership of the 64-campus system, Mr. Bartlett said that he was taking with him more than a few impressions about what had gone wrong. "Behind my departure were differences in perception about where the system is and where it ought to go, and about the role of the board," Mr. Bartlett said. [B2.]
 
Pregnant Girl Is Slain
A pregnant 15-year-old died after she was accidentally shot in Queens by a friend showing off his new pistol, the police said. Her 4-month-old fetus was struck in the skull and died instantly. [B3.]
 
Stock Offer For Harlem Bank
Carver Federal Savings Bank, a Harlem institution that is the country's largest black-run financial services company, is the target of a hostile stock offer to take over a sizable part of the company. The bank received an unsolicited offer to buy about one-third of the company's shares for $7.5 million, roughly 15 percent above the current stock price. [B3.]
 
NEW JERSEY
 
6 Arrests in Assault on Officers
A crowd of 200 to 300 people, many perched on tenement roofs, pelted the police in Passaic, N.J., with rocks and bottles after officers tried to arrest people at a local soccer game, the authorities said. Six people were arrested for assaulting a police officer and rioting, but no one was seriously injured, the police said. [B2.]
