HEADLINE: THE CLINTON RECORD: Foreign Policy;
Clinton's Three Big Objectives Include Peace Through Trade

 BODY:
Following are excerpts from an interview with President Clinton conducted on July 8 with reporters and editors of The New York Times:
 
ON HIS FOREIGN-POLICY GOALS
We had basically, you know, three big objectives. One was to finish the unfinished business of leaving the cold war behind. That's our relationship with Russia, our relationship with China, the North Korean nuclear problem and building a united Europe. 
 The second issue was dealing with the new security threats, ethnic hatreds and other kinds of internal blood-baths. That's the Middle East, Bosnia, Northern Ireland, Haiti. Terrorism, which you know there's no point in my elaborating that. You know what we've done there. Drug running, organized crime and these emerging environmental and health related transnational threats.
And the third issue is building a new structure of opportunity and peace through trade investment and commerce. And that's NAFTA, GATT, 200 trade agreements, Ron Brown's 10 emerging national initiatives, what I did with APEC leaders and what we did with the Summit of the Americas.
See, I think that the commerce thing, I think it's been wrongly positioned as are you choosing money over values. It seems to me that if America is in the center of these emerging networks, it dramatically increases our leverage to work with people for peace, for human rights and for stability in the world. . . .
 
ON CHINA
First of all, I think that it would be fair to say that my policies with regard to China have been somewhat different from what I talked about in the campaign. I believed in the campaign that the threat of revoking the M.F.N. might work to change the behavior of the Chinese, particularly on the human rights front. . . .
And after I was here and thought about it and had a chance to assess the Chinese and the Chinese leadership and the nature of the changes going on in China and the way they looked at the world and us, I concluded that that was simply not right.
I concluded that if we were to revoke M.F.N. we night cause serious short-term damage to them economically, but that they would not change their human rights policies -- if anything, they would become more repressive -- and that we would risk creating a new, I hesitate to say, cold war, but a very long-term fissure with a country that I think we still have some chance of influencing in a very positive way, to be a constructive partner in the 21st century.
And I, frankly, concluded that based on what I saw after I came here, that using M.F.N. was a blunt instrument that would have serious longterm adverse consequences and would not work in the near-term or have any impact on human rights. . . .
 
ON BOSNIA
First and foremost, we wanted to stop the slaughter and make sure that we could contain it. And there's no question in that sense that IFOR [Implementation Force, the NATO-led peacekeeping mission] has been a terrific success in reducing violence and fighting and shooting with very little rates of casualties, unrelated to direct conflict with the warring parties.
Secondly, we wanted to restore democracy in a way that maintained the federation between the Croats and the Muslims and had both a central government that involved the Serbs and a separate regional government that gave them a lot of autonomy. That will require the holding of successful elections, which will require, among other things -- and this is the big hang-up there, but not the only one, there are tensions, as you know, still between the Muslims and the Croats -- that will require that Karadzic be out of office and that Karadzic and Mladic be out of influence.
And it is a difficult thing because the IFOR mission was established with the agreement of all the NATO partners not to be a police action to go get them, but instead to be, in effect, fair to all parties in trying to secure the borders in the safety zone, secure free movement.
Then the third issue is the redevelopment issue. And that's a very important issue also for whether these people will ever be able to live in peace with one another again. You know, will there be enough grass-roots stake in the future of the peaceful and united Bosnia, so that leaders who might otherwise fall into the old habits of stirring up the passions of their constituents against one another instead know that what they're supposed to do is work out their differences? And, of course, that's what Ron Brown's last mission was about. It was really an attempt to put the power of American commerce to use for peace.
 
ON THE NEXT CENTURY
And I believe among the great security questions of the 21st century -- and there are, you know, five or six really big questions -- one of them is how will China and Russia define their greatness? Will they define their greatness in terms of their internal achievements and what kind of values and character they have, what kind of economic achievements they can have, how they can bring their culture to bear on more of their people, how they can solve their problems? How will they define their greatness in terms of their ability of force others beyond their borders to yield to their rule?

HEADLINE: PC Market Remains Strong, But Slower Growth Is Predicted

BYLINE: By Reuters 

 BODY:
The worldwide market for personal computers maintained its strong growth in the second quarter, dispelling recent concerns about a possible slump, according to Dataquest Inc., a market research firm.
According to research to be released today, the worldwide PC market, while slowing from its record growth of 24.7 percent in 1995, is on track for about 17 to 19 percent growth in 1996. 
 Dataquest, which is based in San Jose, Calif., said that in the second quarter, the worldwide PC market grew at a 16.5 percent rate, with 15.95 million PC's shipped in the quarter, up from 13.68 million a year earlier.
 The International Business Machines Corporation, based in Armonk, N.Y., had surprising gains in the quarter, edging closer to the Compaq Computer Corporation as the top PC vendor in the world. Compaq, which is based in Houston, had 9.7 percent of the world PC market, but it lost some market share to I.B.M. Compaq had 10.4 percent of the world market in the second quarter last year.
I.B.M.'s market share jumped to 8.8 percent of the market, versus 7.7 percent a year ago. In the second quarter, I.B.M. had the strongest volume growth among the top five vendors worldwide, rising 33.6 percent in the quarter.
"Compaq went down and I.B.M. went up; they are less than a point of market share apart," said Kimball Brown, vice president and chief analyst of Dataquest's PC program. "It was a big gain in share for I.B.M."
Information from the International Data Corporation of Framingham, Mass., shows similar trends within the United States. International Data said that the United States market grew 18 percent in the second quarter, to 5.96 million units.
Compaq was the leader in the United States, with 11.8 percent of the market, shipping 702,000 units in the quarter.
Packard Bell Electronics, based in Westlake Village, Calif., was second in the United States, with 9.7 percent of the market and 580,000 units shipped in the quarter. I.B.M. trailed Packard Bell with 511,000 units in the United States, but its market share jumped to 8.6 percent from 7.6 percent a year earlier. International Data said that in the United States, sales accelerated in portable computers and commercial desktops but that consumer sales were seasonally slow, as many people waited for a wave of new home PC's.

HEADLINE: If Immigrants Lose U.S. Aid, Local Budgets May Feel Pain

BYLINE: By TIM GOLDEN 

DATELINE: OAKLAND, Calif., July 27

 BODY:
The elaborate tattoos that adorn Bech Chuom's chest are meant to protect him from wayward bullets, and he trusts the precepts he follows as a strict Buddhist to safeguard his soul.
But a decade after Mr. Chuom, 72, arrived here as a refugee from Cambodia, he depends entirely for his food and rent on a $551 monthly welfare check. Where he might turn if that aid is taken from legal immigrants under the plans to overhaul welfare now moving through Congress, he says, is a mystery. 
 "I am old," he said, speaking in Khmer through a translator. "My arms are weak. There is nothing I can do anymore. If I do not receive the help, I will starve and die."
With growing alarm, many local officials around the country are predicting another result if welfare restrictions are enacted: that thousands of immigrants like Mr. Chuom will turn to them, swamping nursing homes, local welfare agencies and financially precarious public hospitals.
In Los Angeles County alone, officials say, the added burden in local welfare and medical costs could climb to nearly $400 million a year, forcing drastic cuts in other programs.
Officials in New York City estimate that 10 percent of the people who receive Medicaid or benefits for the elderly and disabled could be cut off, reducing the amount of aid flowing into the city by more than $290 million a year.
"The impact on states with high populations of legal immigrants will be profound," said Christine C. Burch, the executive director of the National Association of Public Hospitals. "The immigrants will still get health care. But it will be the responsibility of local government or, in the case of private hospitals, it will come out of their charity budgets."
For months, immigration advocates have focused their attention and energy on a pending immigration bill that would reduce benefits to noncitizens by $5.4 billion over seven years. The magnitude of the proposed cuts in the revived welfare legislation has left them stunned.
The welfare bill passed by the House on July 18 would make most legal immigrants ineligible for Medicaid, food stamps and Supplemental Security Income, or S.S.I., the Federal program that provides cash support for poor elderly, blind and disabled people. Of the $61.6 billion that the legislation would trim from the Federal budget over seven years, about half would come from reducing aid to these immigrants.
A second version of the bill, passed by the Senate last Tuesday, would allow the states to decide whether to take Medicaid benefits away from legal immigrants already living in the United States. Like the House's version, the Senate bill would give states the option to bar those resident immigrants from some other programs as well, and it would prevent future legal immigrants from receiving most Federal benefits until they had been in the country for five years.
The Senate bill would save about $56 billion by 2002. Almost half of that would come from aid to legal immigrants, who would remain liable for Federal and state taxes.
House and Senate negotiators are expected to produce a final draft of the legislation next week. On Friday, one of the House conferees, Representative E. Clay Shaw Jr., Republican of Florida, said House Republicans might be willing to compromise on several issues, including Medicaid benefits for legal immigrants.
Under either version of the bill, Medicaid coverage for emergency health care would be preserved, even for those who enter the country illegally.
Even though much of the political support for the cutbacks can be traced to public anger over the social costs of illegal immigration, the welfare bills would specifically restrict illegal immigrants only from some smaller programs, including school lunches for children and gang-prevention programs.
The cuts in benefits for legal immigrants have also been fueled by the growing belief, particularly among conservatives, that immigrants have taken unfair advantage of the system.
Despite the concern of state officials in Texas that the welfare cuts would shift much of what is now an annual $614 million Federal liability to the local authorities there, Senator Phil Gramm of Texas, among others, has argued in strong support of the bill.
"I think that the vast majority of the people of Texas believe that immigrants should come to Texas with their sleeves rolled up, ready to go to work, and not with their hands out, ready to go on welfare," said Mr. Gramm, a Republican.
In an apparent reflection of the complicated politics of the issue, Gov. Pete Wilson of California, a Republican who has led a campaign to get the Federal Government to reimburse states for the costs of illegal immigration, kept largely silent about the cuts in benefits for legal immigrants until late last week.
But in an interview on Thursday, Mr. Wilson said that although he had some concerns about the local impact of the legislation, his complaints about it centered on the refusal of Congress to grant states wider authority to redesign their welfare and Medicaid programs. He called the proposed reductions in benefits to legal immigrants "the kind of step that needs to be taken to prevent Federal taxpayers from being inundated with people who come, especially late in life, and become public charges."
Population surveys have shown that in the last three decades, immigrant noncitizens have become somewhat more dependent than native-born Americans on Supplemental Security Income and Medicaid.
To some researchers, including George J. Borjas, an economist at Harvard University, that shift clearly fits into a demographic trend in which the immigrants in the newer waves from Asia and Latin America have come to the United States with less education and fewer job skills than the immigrants who preceded them, most of them European.
Other experts contend that the welfare statistics for legal immigrants are skewed by the heavy use of benefits by two groups -- refugees and the elderly -- that have grown more numerous because of changes in immigration laws since 1965.
A look at some of the lives that fall into those two categories suggests that simply withdrawing benefits from older immigrants and refugees is unlikely to jolt them from dependency.
On a quiet street in Oakland's San Antonio district, one of the city's poorest, a decrepit, two-story apartment building has become home to a small community of Cambodian refugees. The days seem to come and go with only the most tentative forays outside: shopping, visiting the doctor, getting children off to school.
After providing a fresh coat of stucco and perhaps a swimming pool in the drab, concrete patio, a hopeful suburban landlord might have given the place a name like Palm Gardens or Tudor Court. Instead, it seems exotic only for the occasional smells that waft from small kitchens and for the elderly men and women who sit chatting quietly in Khmer.
Tith Chin, 40, a former Cambodian soldier who survived a Khmer Rouge labor camp and the long trek to refuge in Thailand, said he had worked in an Atlanta bakery when he came to the United States in 1983.
He might work again, he said, if he could find a way to care for his ailing wife and three children. He said he would also like to apply for United States citizenship but did not think he spoke enough English to pass the test. Still, he could not imagine how he would make up for the loss of the $707 that he and his wife get each month under Aid to Families With Dependent Children, the main Federal program of cash assistance.
"When we first came here, the Americans said, 'We will help you,' " he said in hesitant English. "And now they want to change this law. Why?"
Unlike other legal immigrants, who are at least initially the economic responsibility of the American citizens who sponsor them, refugees are eligible for Federal benefits upon their arrival. Both the Senate and House bills would continue benefits to refugees during their first five years in the United States. The legislation would also waive the new restrictions for immigrants who have worked for a total of 10 years without drawing Federal aid and for those who have served in the military.
At Coney Island Hospital in Brooklyn, where the signs are printed in English, Spanish and Russian, the director, Howard C. Cohen, said the proposed Medicaid cutbacks would strip insurance from many of the elderly immigrants who are already his patients. He said it would also inundate the hospital with many more such patients, those turned away for other than emergency care by private hospitals and private doctors.
"We would just get dumped on," he said.
Nowhere is that kind of shift expected to be felt more acutely than in California, home to nearly 40 percent of the roughly 1.5 million legal immigrants who now get Federal benefits.
Officials said most poor and elderly immigrants would still qualify for general assistance, the locally financed aid program of last resort. In Los Angeles County, the 93,000 immigrants who could potentially seek such help would almost equal the number now on the rolls.
That problem is compounded across California by the fact that although the state government would expect to save close to $300 million of the money it now spends annually to supplement Federal S.S.I. payments to legal immigrants, the state contributes nothing to the county to help pay for general assistance.
"If no other relief is obtained, this could be very extreme," said Sandra M. Davis, the interim chief administrative officer for Los Angeles County, which faced a $1.2 billion shortfall last year.
"It would mean closing the Parks Department, closing the beaches and museums -- all administrative operations would be significantly reduced," she said. "It could throw the county into an insolvent position."

HEADLINE: ATLANTA DAY 10 -- BASKETBALL;
Dream Team Takes Care of a Formality: Winning Group

BYLINE: By MALCOLM MORAN 

DATELINE: ATLANTA, July 28

 BODY:
Aleksandr Gomelsky, whose 1988 gold-medal team from the Soviet Union led directly to the unleashing of the Dream Team, was standing in a quiet Georgia Dome the other day and talking about an Olympic basketball competition that from the start has featured brave and passionate efforts.
"This is not bad tournament," Gomelsky said. "But only one problem. This is not competition for gold medals." 
 This reality does not seem to be a concern for the shrieking crowds that fill the dome, their tiny flashbulbs bursting from cameras pointed from another ZIP code. While the rest of the world is taking part in an intense tournament, the United States is working to make basketball a demonstration sport.
Tonight's exhibition, a crisp 102-71 United States victory over Croatia, formally secured first place in Group A at the end of the preliminary round. The Americans (5-0) will face Brazil, the fourth-place team in Group B, in a quarterfinal Tuesday.
The United States established its running game from the start, led by Mitch Richmond, who had 16 points. Charles Barkley scored 14 points on 7-of-9 shooting and had 12 rebounds. David Robinson made 4 of 5 shots and scored 13 points. Gary Payton had 7 assists without a turnover and Grant Hill had 3 of the United States' 15 steals.
After a comfortable but inconsistent start, as the Americans struggled to mesh 12 National Basketball Association All-Stars into a unit, the United States has begun to operate with the efficiency, if not the legendary status, of the group that won in Barcelona four years ago.
But the thread that connects Dream Teams past and present has to do with more than margins of victory and accomplishments on the floor. It provides a measure of how opponents are treated and how far relationships have come in four years, from the time Toni Kukoc of Croatia was considered, all at once, the future of the Chicago Bulls and a threat to their cohesiveness.
His experience in a preliminary-round game in Barcelona -- 4 points, 2-of-11 shooting -- was a result of an initiation rite supervised by Scottie Pippen and Michael Jordan that fell somewhere between ostracism and humiliation. Kukoc was Bulls property but not yet a teammate. Pippen and Jordan saw the opportunity to make points in their economic wrestling with management.
"The first time they were much more serious," Kukoc remembered. "I didn't expect him and M. J. to be so serious. Today we can have some laughs."
In the day-to-day existence of N.B.A. life, the change in the relationship has been gradual. When measured by a comparison of Olympiads, the difference is astonishing. The Lithuanian team rested injured players against the United States but Kukoc played 33 minutes today with a broken left thumb. "If I didn't play," he told reporters with a small smile, "you would say I was scared to come out against these guys."
He scored 10 points with 10 assists. This time, with the Americans in control early, Kukoc and Pippen traded 3-point baskets -- Pippen steal, Pippen 3-pointer, Kukoc 3-pointer, Pippen 3-pointer -- and the kind of verbal abuse that makes an N.B.A. season go by. "I know Scottie much better now," Kukoc said.
"I didn't know him four years ago," Pippen said. "I wanted to go out and defend him and shut him down and shut up all the critics who said he was the best European player."
 
REBOUNDS
In the other quarterfinal games on Tuesday, Yugoslavia, the undefeated Group B winner, will meet China. Lithuania will face Greece, and Croatia will oppose Australia. . . . Greece managed to improve its positioning in the medal round by losing to Australia, 103-62, earlier Sunday. By finishing third in Group B, behind Australia, Greece would not have to meet the United States until the final.

HEADLINE: Dole Reverses Stand on White House Security

BYLINE: By ADAM NAGOURNEY 

DATELINE: BILLINGS, Mont., July 28

 BODY:
Bob Dole reversed himself today and said that he opposed President Clinton's decision last year to close a two-block stretch in front of the White House to guard against a catastrophic terrorist attack on the President's home.
Mr. Dole's assertion that Pennsylvania Avenue should be reopened to traffic, which he made during a brief appearance where he read a statement deploring the bombing in Centennial Olympic Park in Atlanta, came in response to a question and seemed to catch his aides by surprise. When Mr. Clinton announced last year that he was following the recommendation of the Secret Service and closing the stretch of Pennsylvania Avenue to vehicles, Mr. Dole applauded the action. Until today, he had given no public indication that he was reconsidering his position. 
 "Well, my view is they ought to open it up," Mr. Dole said during a four-minute appearance before a small group of reporters at his Washington campaign headquarters, before flying here this evening. "There ought to be other ways to protect the President."
As Mr. Dole then left the lectern, a reporter asked him whether the bombing in Atlanta might change his view, and Mr. Dole called over his shoulder: "No. It wouldn't change it."
Mr. Clinton's aides said they were surprised when they learned of Mr. Dole's new position. "It's a complete repudiation of what the Secret Service said," said George Stephanopoulos, a senior adviser to Mr. Clinton. "I'm surprised. The President and the Secret Service and the Congressional leadership all agreed that this is a prudent course of action."
Mr. Clinton ordered the stretch of Pennsylvania Avenue closed to vehicles in May 1995, converting it into a pedestrian mall. He acted on the recommendation of Treasury Secretary Robert E. Rubin and the Secret Service.
The decision came a month after the truck bombing of the Federal Building in Oklahoma City, which killed 168 people. But new security measures at the White House had, by then, already been under consideration because of two earlier events, involving a drunken pilot crashing a light plane into the South Lawn and a gunman firing a semiautomatic rifle at the White House.
Since then, the road between Lafayette Park and the White House continues to be closed to vehicles, pending the resolution of a debate about what to do with it. On most days, it swarms with tourists snapping comparatively unobstructed pictures of the White House, and with roller-bladers and pedestrians.
Today's events illustrated a new concern for Mr. Dole's campaign as he enters into what he had hoped would be a critical two weeks before the Republican National Convention in San Diego. Mr. Dole's aides had expected this to be a high-profile period for the candidate, when he would set the stage for his convention by taking advantage of rising interest in his selection of a Vice Presidential nominee and in the drafting of an economic plan.
But the crash of T.W.A. Flight 800, and the Olympic bombing, are threatening those prospects, as Mr. Dole faces a daily chance that anything his campaign does might be overwhelmed by a major development in either investigation. Mr. Dole's televised statement of concern about the bombing today was intended to make the best of the situation, by essentially piggy-backing on developments in Atlanta.
"We must continue searching for ways to improve our defenses against terrorism to better insure the safety of our citizens," Mr. Dole said, reading from prepared remarks. "It's equally important, however, that we remain firm in our resolve never to let the terrorists defeat the American spirit."
Mr. Dole did not offer much explanation today for his change of position about Pennsylvania Avenue, and by all accounts there was no political design behind his making his remarks on a weekend when public concern about terrorism was so pitched, as evidenced by heightened security checks on his own plane.
When Mr. Clinton decided to close off the street, Mr. Dole, then the Senate majority leader, said, "I think we did the right thing." He said then that a Secret Service briefing persuaded him that "there weren't that many options."
Mr. Dole today offered a slightly different recollection of last year's Secret Service briefing, saying "the only option they had was closing it."
"I think there is some, there are some who feel strongly the other way, that it's not going to stop terrorism," he said. "If people are determined, want to just give their lives, they're going to get through somehow."
Nelson Warfield, Mr. Dole's press secretary, explained Mr. Dole's new position by saying: "There is only one President. He was willing to acquiesce to this President's judgment on Pennsylvania Avenue. When he is President, his object will be different."
Mr. Clinton's decision to close the street was reached, by his aides' account, reluctantly, in part out of concern for the political fallout from giving the appearance that the White House was caving in to fear of terrorism. As it turned out, the fallout was stronger in the District of Columbia than nationally.
Mayor Marion S. Barry Jr., a Democrat, noted at a Congressional hearing last month, that diverting traffic around a street that had been used by 13,000 cars a day led to congestion on H, I and K Streets. At the same hearing, the director of the Secret Service argued strongly in favor of closing off traffic there.
"If you open Pennsylvania Avenue," said Eljay Bowron, the Secret Service director, "they can and at some point they will destroy the White House. It is not a matter if this will occur, but when."

HEADLINE: A Cap on Taxes in Washington Draws White House Opposition

BYLINE: By ERIC SCHMITT 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, July 28

 BODY:
The Clinton Administration opposes a broad cut in Federal tax rates for District of Columbia residents, the Republicans' favorite idea for bailing out the city, a senior White House official said today.
The cuts, first proposed by Eleanor Holmes Norton, a Democrat who is the District's nonvoting delegate to Congress, would put a 15 percent cap on Federal taxes paid by city residents to entice citizens to stay in the District and encourage those who have fled to the suburbs to return. Washington's population has dropped to 554,000 from 706,000 just 20 years ago. 
 But the White House chief of staff, Leon E. Panetta, said today that doling out tax breaks to cities piecemeal was poor policy.
"If you just provide an across-the-board tax cut for Washington, D.C., and we're going to go out of our way to help congressmen and senators and others that live in Washington, D.C., that just is not going to happen," Mr. Panetta said on the ABC News program, "This Week with David Brinkley."
"I mean, what happens to Detroit? What happens to New York? What happens to Boston? What happens to other cities?" Mr. Panetta said. He said that urban issues must be addressed on a national basis.
"If you're going to address urban issues, it's got to be done on an across-the-board basis."
Ms. Norton's 15 percent tax proposal, which would lower Federal taxes from the current maximum rate of 39.6 percent, is appealing to District residents but is strongly opposed by the Administration.
The cap would cost the Federal Government about $750 million a year in lost revenues. Economists also note that it would be a huge tax cut for residents earning more than $200,000.
Reached today at home, Ms. Norton accused Mr. Panetta of being ill-informed about the plan's details. "Leon doesn't know what he's talking about," Ms. Norton said in a telephone interview. "I think he's all mixed up in election-year politics.
"The District is uniquely disadvantaged compared with other cities because it has no state to recycle funds from wealthier areas, yet it is required to pay state, county and municipal costs."
After two decades of experimenting with self-rule, the city is virtually insolvent. "This city has had a hemorrhage for 15 years of people who work for a living," Mr. Gingrich said earlier this month. "They've moved out to the suburbs, and the result is the city is imploding. To have your national capital implode is a disaster."
Most other Republican leaders, including the House and Senate majority leaders, Representative Dick Armey of Texas and Senator Trent Lott of Mississippi, support the tax cuts as a way to stimulate economic growth in the city and raise the confidence of its residents. But the Republican chairman of the Ways and Means Committee, which writes tax laws, Representative Bill Archer of Texas, has been cool to the idea.
The tax-cut proposal will be debated this week at a hearing of the House Government Reform and Oversight District of Columbia subcommittee.

HEADLINE: THE CLINTON RECORD: Foreign Policy;
Bosnia Policy Shaped by U.S. Military Role

BYLINE: By ELAINE SCIOLINO 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, July 28

 BODY:
Two snapshots of Bill Clinton capture his decision to come to terms with the foreign policy crisis that has dominated his Presidency: Bosnia.
The first is one the White House shows with pride: a determined President Clinton telling his national security advisers at a breakfast meeting in July 1995 that the war in Bosnia "is killing the U.S. position of strength in the world." He also wanted the war off the front pages in an election year. 
 Concluding that only American leadership could prevent a crisis that might jeopardize a second term, Mr. Clinton initiated a plan that eventually ended the worst fighting in Europe since World War II.
The second snapshot is one the White House keeps hidden: a vacillating Mr. Clinton meeting with President Jacques Chirac of France just one month earlier.
Overwhelmed and confused, Mr. Clinton pressed the French President to argue against a move in Congress to arm the Muslim-led Bosnian Government and to raise money to better protect United Nations peacekeeping troops in Bosnia.
Mr. Clinton kept the French entourage and his Cabinet waiting for nearly two hours while he arranged for Mr. Chirac to meet with Bob Dole, then the Senate majority leader, and Newt Gingrich, the House Speaker. When Mr. Chirac returned home, he announced derisively that the position of leader of the free world was "vacant."
Both impulses -- determination and desperation -- were part of two and a half years of uncertain diplomacy over Bosnia. It began with Mr. Clinton's accusations that the Bush Administration was doing too little, then shifted to the view that the conflict was largely Europe's problem, and ended with peace, but a peace so fragile that it may unravel when NATO troops withdraw at the end of the year.
What pushed Mr. Clinton to act was the realization that American troops would inevitably go to Bosnia, either to help extricate United Nations peacekeeping troops in failure, or to enforce a peace. The decision is fraught with paradox but blessed with good fortune.
The paradox is that having worked so hard to keep American troops out of Bosnia, Mr. Clinton had to send 20,000 of them to the region in an election year. The good fortune is that the mission -- the result of peace agreement hammered out in November -- has been largely free of casualties.
In an interview, Mr. Clinton described the 60,000-member NATO force as "a terrific success" in preventing further bloodshed in Bosnia. Indeed, because of Bosnia, the President, who came into office with little passion for or experience in foreign affairs, has both won a modicum of respect from his peers and become more comfortable as Commander in Chief.
Just listen to the soundbites. "I'm doing the best I can," Mr. Clinton lamented in discussing Bosnia at a CNN town meeting in mid-1993. But last November, when Dan Rather, the CBS News anchor, asked whether Mr. Clinton felt comfortable about risking American lives when he had never served in the military, he finally got it right. "I am the President," he said.
Asked why the Europeans feel better about Mr. Clinton, Prime Minister Romano Prodi of Italy said, in an interview: "Because of Bosnia. This is the most important event for Europe. He is playing foreign policy very strongly. Because of Bosnia."
But there are those who assert that the United States stepped in too late, that the peace is holding only because of the NATO deployment, that making it last will require major outside help.
"It's wrong to say something is a success when there was massive 'ethnic cleansing' and two million people displaced," said Morton I. Abramowitz, the president of the Carnegie Endowment for International Peace. "However desirable and important the peace agreement was, it's grotesque to call Bosnia a successful policy for either Bush or Clinton. The United States has suspended the war for the election; it didn't create a peace."
A Policy Quandary
Just after the 1992 election, Mr. Clinton said his Administration had two choices when it came to a crisis like Bosnia: to "come up with a decent policy and aggressively pursue it" or "ignore it for a while; wait for it to explode."
For much of his Presidency, he ignored his own advice.
In the campaign, Mr. Clinton accused President George Bush of lacking "real leadership" on Bosnia, advocated strangling the economy of Serbia and insisted that its President, Slobodan Milosevic, be brought to justice for "crimes against humanity."
Once in office, however, Mr. Clinton faced the same paralyzing problem: how to end the war without risking American lives. He began to call Bosnia a "civil war," swinging back and forth between strong proposals and stronger doubts.
Early on, Mr. Clinton refused to support the Vance-Owen plan, which would have divided Bosnia into 10 semi-autonomous provinces along ethnic lines. His stated reason was that it was wrong to impose a solution that ratified "ethnic cleansing" by the Serbs. Unstated was the fact that enforcing it would have been a military nightmare.
That impotence in coming to terms with Bosnia was underscored by Secretary of State Warren Christopher's disastrous trip to Europe in May 1993, where he presented an American plan to exempt the poorly armed Bosnian Government from the United Nations arms embargo. If the Serbs went on the offensive before enough arms arrived, NATO would attack with pinprick air strikes.
But this approach was opposed by the Europeans, who, unlike the Americans, had thousands of peacekeeping troops on the ground. Mr. Christopher went only to listen, he said afterward. Mr. Clinton did not give his Secretary of State the authority to present the plan as non-negotiable.
The President explained later that preserving good relations with the alliance was more important. "We have bigger fish to fry with the Europeans," Mr. Clinton said.
In retrospect, Mr. Christopher acknowledged that the mission was a mistake. "The way that I made the trip to Europe in May 1993 was not consistent with global leadership," he said in an interview.
The Administration was weakened by the image of an America unwilling to use its power to end the terror, rape, torture and killing that seared Bosnia. Anthony Lake, Mr. Clinton's national security adviser, said in an interview that the war "became a symbol of our foreign policy as a whole."

Road to an Accord
A series of outside events in mid-1995 finally pushed Mr. Clinton to act.
Mr. Chirac, the French President, was pressing for a more aggressive policy against the Serbs while threatening to remove his peacekeeping troops. The capture of the Bosnian Muslim towns of Srebrenica and Zepa exposed the emptiness of American promises to protect the Bosnian Muslims and undermined the credibility of NATO.
By far the most important factor in transforming the political landscape came in August, when a Croatian offensive expelled the Serbs from a large chunk of territory in central Croatia known as the Krajina. Mr. Milosevic abandoned his Serbian allies in Bosnia to make peace. Ethnic separation in the region was nearly complete. A Bosnian Serb attack on Sarajevo triggered serious NATO air attacks on the Serbs.
In the United States, both the House and the Senate voted in early August, by veto-proof margins, to require the President to lift the arms embargo to help the Bosnian Government forces. Mr. Clinton had once sought that goal, but he now knew that such a step could provoke the Europeans to carry out their threat of a military withdrawal, which they could not do without American troops on the ground.
When President Clinton understood that he would have to send troops to Bosnia one way or the other, he redrew American policy, abandoning his earlier insistence on ethnic pluralism and accepting an imperfect peace as the only practical alternative.
Thus Richard C. Holbrooke, Assistant Secretary of State for European affairs, began a diplomatic marathon that produced an improvised peace agreement in Dayton, Ohio, in November. Mr. Milosevic, the man considered most responsible for starting the war, was transformed by the American side into a statesman; the Bosnia Government was persuaded that a tenuous peace was better than more war.

Redefining U.S. Interests
So what are the lessons learned? Throughout the process, most policy makers from the President on down argued against more American involvement. Revisionism has already set in.
The Defense Secretary, William J. Perry, now says the United States should have pressed NATO into action much earlier even though that would have put American lives at risk.
"The war in Bosnia was against the national interest of the United States," Mr. Perry said in an interview. "It was clearly against the interest of our European allies. We should have been prepared to use or to threaten to use military force from the beginning."
The Defense Secretary went on: "I do not see any other way of doing that except through NATO and, therefore, from the beginning we should have been prepared to use NATO. That implies to me commitment of U.S. forces."
Gen. John Shalikashvili, the Chairman of the Joint Chiefs of Staff and another reluctant warrior, argues that the United States should have joined its allies on the ground.
"We thought we could somehow let them be on the ground and we would be in the air and that would be enough," he said in an interview. "There is a very important lesson that you cannot be in an alliance with nations and somehow step back from a leadership position."
Administration officials concede that the peace agreement has serious flaws, and no one is rushing to arrest those who have been indicted on war crime charges, particularly Radovan Karadzic, the Bosnian Serb political leader, and Gen. Ratko Mladic, the military commander. Even Mr. Clinton calls that situation "the big hang-up there." The Administration is notably silent on the question of ever indicting President Milosevic.
As for elections in Bosnia, scheduled for September, severe restrictions on the freedom of movement and expression -- and the failure to insure the return of refugees -- will make it difficult for the vote to be free and fair. The most likely outcome is that hard-line Serbian and Croatian nationalists will retain power in areas they control.
Yet Mr. Clinton said in the interview, "I still believe we'll be able to have elections in September, and I believe we can have elections that will be successful." He did not define what he meant by success.
Most worrisome is that although the peace agreement preserves Bosnia on paper, it is essentially a partitioned country.
"At the end of the day, the peace won't be perfect," NATO Secretary General, Javier Solana of Spain, said in an interview. "The forces of separation are still too strong."
That is not the Clinton Administration's fault, Mr. Lake insisted. "I don't think you can use American or NATO or other forces to change human hearts," he said. "That is not something outsiders can do."
So was Bosnia an American foreign policy success?
"Too early to say," Mr. Lake said. "We won't know that for some years."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Washington faced growing pressure in August 1995 to find a solution to end the Bosnian war as Serbian forces continued their shelling of Sarajevo, above. (Agence France-Presse)

HEADLINE: On World Stage, Many Lessons for Clinton

SERIES: THE CLINTON RECORD: Foreign Policy --Second of seven articles.

BYLINE: By STEVEN ERLANGER and DAVID E. SANGER 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, July 28

 BODY:
Every evening, President Clinton, the baby boomer who came to office vowing not to be consumed by problems beyond America's shores, receives a brief personal note from Warren Christopher, his cautious, 71-year-old Secretary of State and veteran of four Democratic administrations.
At first the notes were tutorial, guiding a President who had accused George Bush of paying too much attention to foreign affairs. Candidate Clinton had argued that human rights in China were more important than trade, that the fratricide in Bosnia was Europe's problem, that the United States could not and should not play the role of world policeman. 
 Today, Mr. Christopher's notes are more practical, an effort to provide early warning to a President who now regards his foreign policy performance as a highlight of the last two years. With clear if fragile progress in Russia, Haiti, Bosnia, the Middle East and Northern Ireland -- and success in saving Mexico from bankruptcy and forcing modest change in the trade policies of Japan and China -- Mr. Clinton is becoming more comfortable as deal-maker and statesman.
The idealism of the 1992 campaign has been replaced with a pragmatic, better-organized and more politically astute use of power, more reminiscent of his Republican predecessor than of Jimmy Carter, the last Democratic President. But it has been an odd journey, with a chaotic beginning and extraordinary lows. Mr. Clinton's first year was symbolized by searing images of weakness: an American soldier's corpse being dragged through the streets of Mogadishu, Somalia, and a United States military transport ship being turned around in Haiti by a rowdy rent-a-mob on the docks of Port-au-Prince. At the end of 1994, Mr. Christopher, tired and dispirited, offered to resign.
Mr. Clinton seemed to be floundering, reacting rather than imposing American leadership on world events. The President was not ready or able to articulate the structure of American foreign policy or explain its themes to a confused global constituency.
Neither were his aides. There was the publicity-shy national security adviser, Anthony Lake, and the lawyerly Mr. Christopher, who managed rather than led. The strongest voices belonged to the aggressive Mickey Kantor, the trade representative, and Robert E. Rubin, the cool investment banker who led the National Economic Council before becoming Treasury Secretary. This configuration was no accident in an Administration that has made domestic economic concerns central to foreign policy.
Mr. Clinton has gradually become his own foreign policy spokesman. He has reversed himself on China, put American troops in Bosnia and discovered the value of quiet engagement and decisive leadership from the only superpower in a post-Soviet world.
The man who argued that the United Nations should take a larger role in international security is now preaching American interests and pushing for a new Secretary General at the United Nations.
The man who tried to rebuild Somalia refused to commit forces to halt the massacres in Rwanda, saying no American interests were at stake.
The Oxford student who labored to avoid military service in Vietnam has ordered American troops to the Iraqi border, Haiti and Bosnia and dispatched two aircraft carriers off Taiwan as a blunt warning to China.
Mr. Clinton is unashamed of his learning curve, however steep. He admits to an early mistake on China, and came to believe that revoking Beijing's trading status with the United States would do nothing to improve its record on human rights or enhance Asian stability.
"After I was here and thought about it and had a chance to assess the Chinese and the Chinese leadership and the nature of the changes going on in China and the way they looked at the world and us, I concluded that that was simply not right," Mr. Clinton said in an interview earlier this month. Revoking China's most-favored-nation status "would not change their human rights policies -- if anything, they would become more repressive."
One of Mr. Clinton's proudest achievements is his success in linking American foreign policy with the domestic economy and jobs.
"Something I need to take on even more," Mr. Clinton said, "is trying to figure out a way to make the American people believe, not just episodically but instinctively, that there's no longer an easy dividing line between foreign policy and domestic policy, that the world we're living in doesn't permit us that luxury any more."
Just as Richard Nixon elevated the National Security Council as a counterweight to the State Department and the Pentagon, Mr. Clinton created the National Economic Council to coordinate domestic and foreign economic policy. In the biggest policy arguments -- confronting China on its piracy of intellectual property, securing financing for Russia and Mexico and balancing trade and security in Japan -- the Departments of Treasury and Commerce and the trade representative have been major players.
"Trade and international economics have joined the foreign-policy table," Mr. Kantor said. "Clinton is the first President to really make trade the bridge between foreign and domestic policy."
Some of the President's successes arose from this new focus: overruling Congress to save Mexico from financial collapse (and thus Texas and California from a deluge of illegal immigrants); pressing Japan more aggressively to open its markets; winning passage of the North American Free Trade Agreement. And he has brought energy to global concerns that transcend blocs and borders: terrorism, organized crime and nuclear proliferation.
"On foreign policy," an Administration official said, "we've gone from woeful chaos to what I think you have to concede is competence. We've learned to work with the President, and the President has learned the subject. There's organization to the process and some reasonable longer-term goals."
Americans seem more willing now to give Mr. Clinton the benefit of the doubt. According to a New York Times poll taken in June, with an accuracy of plus or minus three percentage points, half of the 1,121 respondents approved of his handling of foreign policy, and 54 percent said he had achieved the right balance between foreign and domestic policy. Asked for the President's "greatest achievement," the same number of Americans chose foreign policy as chose the economy -- 16 percent. And 55 percent approve his performance as Commander in Chief in the survey.
It was in China that this complex brew of trade, regional security and human rights all came together.

China Policy
After Rough Start, U.S. Turns Corner
When Bill Clinton ran for President in 1992, he accused George Bush of "coddling dictators" to promote trade with China. But now Mr. Clinton concedes that he did not understand the complexity of America's relationship with the world's most populous nation -- and he paid a price. It took his Administration nearly three years to undo the damage wrought by his reversals and confused agenda. Although White House officials today cite the China experience as a prime example of Mr. Clinton's maturation in foreign policy, many allies were unnerved by his angry confrontations over trade, Taiwan and nuclear proliferation.
In his first year, Mr. Clinton was forced into full retreat from his campaign position that revoking China's trading status was the best way to help China's dissidents. He saw the dangers of using what he now calls "a blunt instrument" once he focused on the consequences -- abandoning America's position in the world's biggest emerging market, losing contacts with a broad spectrum of Chinese society and slowing the spread of computer technologies, like the Internet, that could undercut the Communist Government's political control.
President Clinton also worried about the cost of constant friction with an emerging superpower and the risk another cold war -- or "a very long-term fissure with a country that I think we still have some chance of influencing in a very positive way to be a constructive partner in the 21st century."
But the reversal of policy on human rights was just the beginning of his time of troubles. The Administration seemed to bounce around aimlessly, waiting vainly for cooperation from the Chinese leadership. It had too many spokesmen on China -- partly because Mr. Christopher had ceded the making of China policy to others. With little coordination at the White House, they all defined America's goals in different terms.
Commerce Secretary Ronald H. Brown, who was killed a plane crash in April, had traveled to Beijing and brought along executives from companies like Boeing and General Motors, declaring that China was the center of "commercial diplomacy."
The State Department official overseeing human rights, John Shattuck, would pointedly meet with dissidents -- once within days of Mr. Christopher's arrival in March 1994, angering the Chinese enough to poison Mr. Christopher's only visit.
Then Charlene Barshefsky, Mr. Kantor's deputy and now his successor, would arrive, breathing fire. Ms. Barshefsky went to Beijing eight times on one issue alone: Chinese piracy of American movies, music and software, a problem that cost American industry billions of dollars, especially in important states like California. Twice in a year -- first to win an agreement and then to compel its enforcement -- Mr. Clinton had to threaten to cut off billions of dollars of China's exports to persuade Beijing to play by global rules.
"It was a cacophony, not an orchestra," said one senior Administration official. "There was no sense of setting priorities, signaling the Chinese about what was truly important."
Stanley Roth, who headed Asia issues at the National Security Council until January and who still retains an influence on policy, said he believed that the Chinese decided early on that "this was the unfriendliest Administration they had faced since before Nixon opened China." Chinese leaders appeared to be biding their time, looking for a victory by Bob Dole in November.
Matters worsened when Mr. Clinton, under pressure from Congress, allowed Lee Teng-hui, Taiwan's President, to make a private visit to Cornell University for a reunion in June 1995. The Chinese, thinking that Mr. Christopher had assured them that no such visit would be allowed under the "one-China" policy, were outraged. Relations went into deep freeze, and 1996 seemed crammed with potential conflicts -- over trade, nuclear proliferation, a United Nations human rights conference and the Taiwan elections.
The looming crisis brought about a measure of policy coherence when Mr. Lake and the White House realized that they had to manage the relationship with greater care, and Mr. Lake began having regular breakfasts with independent China scholars.
"We knew there were a lot of boulders in the river," said Samuel R. Berger, Mr. Lake's deputy. "The trick was not to let them overwhelm us at once."
While Mr. Kantor was restrained from pressing the piracy issue too quickly and human rights were played down, President Clinton took a risk many of his advisers thought he would not have tried earlier, sending aircraft carriers to warn China to stop harassing Taiwan during its elections in March. But faced with ambiguous evidence that China had shipped nuclear components to Pakistan, Mr. Clinton accepted Chinese promises not to do it again and did not impose sanctions.
China's violations of its agreements on intellectual piracy were too obvious to ignore, however, and here Washington decided that its credibility was truly at stake. The Administration vowed to impose sanctions unless China cracked down on the pirates who brazenly copied compact disks and CD-ROM's.
The showdown seemed to restore some respect for Mr. Clinton, and at the end of the negotiations President Jiang Zemin invited Ms. Barshefsky over to his compound to signal his interest in improving relations. Ms. Barshefsky said she told him that "both countries tended to go out of their way to define their interests in as mutually incompatible ways as possible, and we both had to change."
As part of the White House's belated decision to seize control of China policy, Mr. Lake set up his own channel of discussions with his Chinese counterpart.
"We're working to build an atmosphere of mutual respect and confidence, within which we can pursue issues of interest to us and to them." Mr. Lake said after visiting China this month. Mr. Christopher is to return in November to arrange a possible summit meeting.
But the question remains whether China, in the midst of its power struggle, is ready to change beyond the trade arena, and only when facing costly sanctions. For his part, Mr. Clinton has yet to define a strategic goal for the relationship; his success so far has been to dig himself out of a hole largely of his own making.

Economic Policy
Pressure on Trade Can Irritate Allies
The biggest change Mr. Clinton has wrought, and one of the most annoying to America's trading partners, is the use of America's power to bolster its economic growth -- even if do so risks destabilizing traditional alliances. The foreign-policy establishment calls that short-sighted; Mr. Clinton's aides call it realistic.
"Twenty years ago, we were willing to enter non-reciprocal agreements," Ms. Barshefsky said. "We had economic and political leeway to place other priorities well ahead of trade. Now, we expect foreign countries to provide the same level of access to us that we have provided to the world. You get what you give."
Mr. Clinton has engaged in far more brinkmanship over market openings than his predecessors. On the campaign, he boasts about the results and avoids mention of the deals that failed. Over all, exports are soaring and the trade deficit with Japan is shrinking. And Mr. Kantor's biggest confrontation with Tokyo -- to force it to open more American car dealerships and purchase American car parts -- has proven a surprising success.
The strategy has been coordinated inside the National Economic Council, created by Mr. Lake and Mr. Rubin over a cup of coffee in late 1992. Its top aides -- "well-balanced schizophrenics," Mr. Lake calls them -- report both to Laura D'Andrea Tyson, the council's director, and to Mr. Lake.
"The big change now is that the economic component of any problem gets on the table at the same time as other issues," Mr. Rubin said. But the Administration has learned that an unrelenting focus on trade can grind away at the trust that allies built up when facing a common Soviet enemy, and recently that has forced some retreats.
Tackling Japan, Mr. Clinton thought he could work around the intransigent Japanese bureaucracy and go over their heads to the reform-minded politicians he had embraced on his first Tokyo trip in 1993. But the reformers proved powerless, and by 1996, the White House was relieved by the return of the ossified Liberal Democratic Party, which was obstructionist but at least knew how to deal.
But two years of relentless pressure had taken its toll on the military alliance. To Japan's delight, when Mr. Clinton next visited this spring, he was forced to focus on security. The confrontation with China had left the region uneasy, and the rape of an Okinawan girl by American servicemen was undermining America's most important military presence in the Pacific.
Mr. Clinton also discovered that maintaining the global economy can be costly. Mexico's peso crisis in late 1994 raised the specter of a run on "emerging markets," which could devastate the fastest-growing consumers of American goods. That, combined with fears of a bigger flood of illegal immigrants, led Mr. Clinton to overrule Congressional objections and lend $12.5 billion, despite Mexican corruption and drug trafficking. The risk paid off: Mexico stuck to a stringent fiscal and monetary policy, triggering joblessness at home but persuading private investors to return. Last week, to the White House's relief, Mexico announced that it was ready to pay back three-quarters of the debt, years early.
Today Mr. Clinton counts his decision to save Mexico as one of his biggest triumphs, despite polling data that showed little support for doing so. Mr. Rubin, the plan's architect, is relieved. "If this had failed," he said, "we'd be facing hearings on the Hill every day."
Some question whether Mr. Clinton's focus on trade and exports will be viewed by historians as an indulgence while the country faced no direct security threat. Mr. Christopher, who served President Kennedy as a textile negotiator, does not think so.
"We've passed the point where we can sustain prosperity on sales just within the United States," he argued. American business has understood there is no turning back, but "it took a long time for Government institutions to cross that barrier with comfort."

Military Policy
Threat or Use of Force Can Bring Success
As America's first President born after World War II, and one who like many in his generation avoided the war in Vietnam, Mr. Clinton was always going to have a hard time with the military.
But Clinton officials give an odd answer when asked about early obstacles to an effective foreign policy: Gen. Colin L. Powell, Chairman of the Joint Chiefs of Staff, a former national security adviser and a Bush holdover. The White House deferred to the General, a national political icon.
But he demanded such an overwhelming commitment of force before agreeing to support any use of American troops that the very idea became unpalatable.
"You cannot exaggerate the influence, both military and political, that Colin Powell had on the early Administration," a senior official said. "Everything we did effectively over the following two or three years, we did without the shadow of Colin Powell and the so-called Powell doctrine."
That doctrine calls for a massive and decisive use of American force in pursuit of clear political objectives involving vital American interests -- as was the case in the Persian Gulf war -- so that success is virtually guaranteed, casualties are minimal and the image of the armed forces is protected.
During his nine months under Mr. Clinton, General Powell, who had helped plan the Somalia mission, opposed using limited American military force in Bosnia. He argued against air strikes alone and wanted no fewer than 300,000 American troops, which was "sufficient to kill off the whole idea," an official said.
Madeleine K. Albright, the United States delegate to the United Nations, said: "There was this doctrine that you'd be dealing with a crazy dictator with six months to prepare and the earth was flat and you'd use overwhelming force and somebody else would pay. But those circumstances don't come along very often."
The White House credits Mr. Powell's successor, Gen. John Shalikashvili, for embracing the carefully limited use of American military might.
"A President must have in his tool bag, in addition to the diplomatic tools, the economic tools, also the tool of military power to protect American interests, even if those interests are not vital," General Shalikashvili said.
"We do not have the luxury of standing back and saying, 'You Europeans take care of that' and if something happens in Africa, 'You in Africa take care of it.' We still save ourselves for this major confrontation with an enemy who was rapidly disappearing before our eyes, and the rest of the world didn't buy into it. We learned that in Bosnia."
In North Korea and Haiti, Mr. Clinton learned that the modest use of force, as well as a credible threat to use it, could work wonders. In 1994, faced with North Korea's determination to continue its nuclear-weapons program, he threatened economic sanctions despite the North Korean declaration that sanctions would mean war. North Korea negotiated, freezing its program in return for aid from United States, Japan and South Korea to build conventional reactors that would not produce bomb-grade material.

Foreign Policy
White House Defining Vital U.S. Interests
Mr. Clinton met the challenges of world crises unevenly, with harsh lessons learned. Time and again, he had to decide what constituted a vital American interest, and whether the burden of leadership -- and its cost -- could be shared or avoided. In essence, Mr. Clinton concluded that the world is more distressed when the United States fails to lead than when it does.
From the beginning, he recognized a vital interest in the relationship with a fragile Russia as well as the other states of the former Soviet Union, especially those like Ukraine, which inherited nuclear weapons. The United States led the West in a program of aid and technical help to promote democracy and a market economy.
Early idealism about partnership, however, faded as Russia's transition from Communism proved less successful than Mr. Clinton hoped. American policy, while it supported Mr. Yeltsin against a resurgent Communist Party, grew harder, emphasizing American security interests and favoring NATO expansion despite Moscow's opposition. And a Republican Congress sharply cut aid.
In the Middle East, Mr. Clinton pushed hard for a comprehensive peace and presided over the reconciliation of Israel and the Palestine Liberation Organization. But neither Israel nor Syria proved willing to take the risks required for a larger peace.
The assassination of Yitzhak Rabin, Israel's Prime Minister, was a huge setback for Washington's aspirations. In subsequent elections, Israelis rejected Mr. Clinton's favorite, Shimon Peres, for Benjamin Netanyahu, who puts security before peace. Washington is now simply trying to insure that Israel lives up to its previous commitments.
On Bosnia, Mr. Clinton temporized, and his early actions, including a disastrous visit to the European allies by Mr. Christopher in May 1993, became an object lesson in how not to exercise American leadership. When Mr. Christopher returned home empty-handed after merely discussing, rather than pressing, Mr. Clinton's case for a new policy, it only reinforced the perception that while the new President might talk about "multilateralism" to manage post-Soviet security, he did not know the first thing about how to do it.
The trip "was not consistent with global leadership," Mr. Christopher now says. "We have learned the way we must conduct foreign policy," he said, "and that is to be very purposeful, to be determined with United States leadership." Mr. Clinton finally exercised that leadership last summer, after it became clear that he would have to send American troops one way or another. When he finally bit the bullet on Bosnia, it helped redefine his Presidency.
A senior official described Mr. Lake's pleasure when he made his own Bosnia trip to Europe last August. "You can't imagine the look on people's faces when you say, 'The President wants it done,' " the official described Mr. Lake's recounting. "You have to say, 'This is what we're going to do.' "
Early on, Mr. Christopher's own Under Secretary had argued that America was too poor to lead, while Mrs. Albright, trying to solve the same disparity between global goals and shrinking American means, asserted a doctrine of "assertive multilateralism," which seemed to make the United Nations the only repository of legitimacy for the use of force.
American officials say that the problems in Somalia, Haiti and Bosnia were left on their plate by the departing Bush Administration, but Clinton officials added their own dash of chaos and inflated goals.
The blistering experience in Somalia, where 18 Army Rangers were killed as they hunted the leader of one faction in October 1993, has forced Mr. Clinton to shy away from seeking to apprehend the Bosnia Serb leaders Radovan Karadzic and Ratko Mladic so they can stand trial on war crime charges. President Clinton also refused to intervene in an arguably more horrible case of genocide in Rwanda, because there was no clear American interest involved.
In Rwanda, where America refused to deploy troops to stop a spasm of violence, an official said, "the task was too big and the interest involved was too small."
Haiti, however, was closer to home. After an embargo failed to persuade the military junta to step aside, Mr. Clinton decided in October 1994 to use American troops. With a United Nations resolution authorizing the action, the intervention provided both a success and a model. As peacemakers, American troops soon handed off the operation to United Nations peacekeepers.

Policy Objectives
Building a Framework For Peace and Trade
President Clinton has discovered that foreign policy affords freedom of action, and that the American President has the biggest bully pulpit in the world. But he makes no real effort to provide a strategic vision, a task dismissed jokingly by a senior official as "stratocrap and globaloney." Instead, in the interview, Mr. Clinton talked of "three big objectives." The first is "to finish the unfinished business of leaving the cold war behind," including relations with Russia, China and North Korea as well as "European unity," by which he presumably meant NATO expansion.
Second, he said, is "dealing with the new security threats, ethnic hatreds and other kinds of internal blood-baths," in which he included the Middle East, Bosnia, Northern Ireland and Haiti, as well as the new "transnational" threats that include terrorism, crime, nuclear proliferation and environmental hazards.
Third, he said, is "building a new structure of opportunity and peace through trade, investment and commerce," in which he included trade agreements with individual nations and larger accords like the North American Trade Agreement.
But Mr. Clinton has found little international consensus on his broadest goals. While cooperation has improved on terrorism and crime, there are enormous divisions with Europe over the wisdom of isolating hostile nations like Iran, Iraq and Libya.
The President's regular resort to the threat of sanctions has irritated traditional allies, most recently over his election-year decision to demonize Cuba. Reversing policy, he signed the Helms-Burton bill, which punishes foreign companies for investing in Cuba. Another bill does the same with Libya and Iran. "This is bullying," said Lloyd Axworthy, the Canadian Foreign Minister. "But in America you call it global leadership."
In private, some top advisers worry that the cumulative effect hurts Washington's ability to rally its allies and undercuts its reputation as a reliable trading partner. But a senior White House official shrugs the problem off.
"The whole bureaucracy hates this stuff, but we're out front fighting terrorism," the official said. "It breaks the rules, but it works, and the President says, 'We're doing it.' In the end, they'll get over it. We're America, and they'll get over it."
Mr. Lake urged a farmer's patience. "You don't solve foreign societies," he said. "You plant, you plow, you seed, you water, you weed and then you harvest." He admits the Clinton team has gotten better at driving the tractor. "You just have to keep working," he said. "If you don't learn from experience, you're not concentrating."
Mr. Clinton remains eager to keep talking, in the conviction that seems to run through his remarkable political life that given enough time, he can win any argument. He wants to break down "the wall in our minds" between domestic and foreign policy. "I try to see these connections, and I think that slowly I'm being able to communicate to the American people in a way that resonates with them."

HEADLINE: BOMB AT THE OLYMPICS;
Bombing Victim's Home Is Burglarized

BYLINE: ALBANY, Ga., July 28, AP 

 BODY:
One day after Alice Hawthorne was killed by the bomb in Atlanta's Centennial Olympic Park, someone burglarized her home, police said today.
"As far as I know, no arrests were made," said Cpl. Derrick Reed of the Albany Police Department. He said the break-in was discovered on a routine patrol. 
 A neighbor nailed a piece of plywood over the broken front window near the front door and the Police Department stationed an officer inside the house to protect it.
The window is hidden from the street by shrubs. A bent window screen and shattered glass littered the area.
Cpl. Ryan Ward, who discovered the broken window, said a neighbor had told the police that a VCR and television were missing. Another neighbor reported a suspicious white Jeep Cherokee in the area that morning.
Neighbors, still in shock from the death of Mrs. Hawthorne, a community leader and entrepreneur, called the burglary a contemptible act.
"It was a sick person that killed her and it had to be a sick person who broke into her house," said one resident, J. D. Roberts.
Another neighbor, Pat Horton, said she believed the robbers knew the house was unoccupied because of news reports of Mrs. Hawthorne's death.
Mrs. Hawthorne's husband, John Hawthorne, rushed to Atlanta on Saturday morning when he learned his wife had been killed and her 14-year-old daughter, Fallon Stubbs, was injured by the bomb.
The Hawthorne's lived in a middle-class neighborhood. She was a sales representative for the local cable television company and the part-owner of a strip mall. John Hawthorne works for the City of Albany's Community and Economic Development Department.

HEADLINE: CREDIT MARKETS;
Bond Prices Drop Sharply Ahead of Data

BYLINE: By ROBERT HURTADO 

 BODY:
Prices of Treasury securities dropped sharply yesterday as investors braced for a blizzard of new economic data this week.
By the close of the New York cash market the 30-year benchmark Treasury bond was down 28/32, to a price of 86 19/32; the yield, which moves in the opposite direction of the price, shot to 7.09 percent, from 7.01 percent on Friday. 
 Josh Stiles, senior bond strategist at IDEA, a British financial consulting firm, said that one reason for early jitters in the market was comments by Alfred Broaddus, president of the Richmond Federal Reserve. In remarks reported yesterday, Mr. Broaddus pointed to tentative signs of wage pressures and the possibility of a second-half slowdown.
"Because he is known as an inflation hawk, and because he is a nonvoting member of the Federal Open Market Committee, the combination of the two diluted much of what he did say," Mr. Stiles said. Mr. Broaddus was in Asheville, N.C., speaking to a conference on farm credit. The committee is the rate-setting arm of the Federal Reserve.
Mr. Stiles said the market was likely more concerned about what economic data to be released later this week would show. "I get the feeling from some market participants that they are expecting the bigger risks will come with the National Association of Purchasing Management and payroll numbers," he said. "The employment-cost index will set the tone of trade going into Thursday. There are a lot of bullets to dodge this week."
Mr. Stiles was referring to three economic reports this week that many analysts are convinced will be pivotal when Fed policy makers decide whether to tighten credit to fend off inflation.
Mr. Stiles said that the market was likely to further discount prices for the added risks it faced, saying that he believed it was probably better to approach this week's release of economic data down at a price of 108 on the September bond futures contract, than above at a price of 109. "It gives the market some room to move to the upside," he said.
By the close of futures trading yesterday, the September bond contract had settled at a price of 107 27/32, after opening the day at 108 8/32.
Bill Gamba, senior vice president and head of government bond trading at Cowen & Company, said that no one wanted to make big bets in the face of the coming data. "Tomorrow's second-quarter employment-cost index is a number what would normally not be a market mover," he said, "but because of the increase in hourly earnings for the June employment data, it will be watched a little more closely than normal."
Mr. Gamba also noted that the quarterly refunding announcement by the Treasury would add to the pressure in the market.
Last quarter, the employment-cost index -- the measure of total compensation for employment, including pensions and health benefits -- rose seven-tenths of 1 percent; the market now looks for the index to rise strongly, in a range of nine-tenths of 1 percent to a full percent higher.
Mr. Gamba said that if the rise in nonfarm payrolls exceeded 250,000, there could be a fairly substantial selloff, which would push the yield on the long bond up to 7.15 percent or 7.20 percent.
Traders said the results of the Treasury's regular weekly bill auction yesterday were a bit soft, with the average rate up six basis points, to 5.20 percent, for a three-month maturity and four basis points, to 5.34 percent, for the six-month. A basis point is one-hundredth of a percentage point.
Following are the results of yesterday's Treasury auction of new three- and six-month bills:

HEADLINE: Bankers Still Avoid Inner-City Lending

 BODY:
To the Editor:
Prof. George Benston of Emory University says he knows "of no studies showing that inner cities are underserved by lenders" (Economic Scene, July 25). But his hometown newspaper won a Pulitzer Prize a decade ago for a series that uncovered how underserved inner-city neighborhoods were by lenders in Atlanta. Related studies have been conducted by the Federal Reserve Bank of Boston, regulators and research entities that confirm that minority borrowers receive loan rejections disproportionately, despite incomes and credit histories comparable to nonminority counterparts. 
 Most analysts expect 1997 grades of banks by regulators will show disparate business lending activity with larger gaps than in previous studies, which focused on mortgage lending. Even enlightened bankers admit that providing loans to start-up businesses remains a challenge.
The partnerships that the Department of Housing and Urban Development seeks to catalyze in its new program supporting Community and Individual Investment Corporations feature banks, local government and neighborhood loan fund operators working to insure that pre-loan and post-loan managerial assistance is provided to emerging companies. Last year, the Committee for Economic Development, a policy group financed by business, issued a report urging financial and technical assistance for distressed areas, using partnerships with community development corporations. Will these efforts pose a threat to the pawnshops that Prof. George Kaufman, whom you quote, worries are undercut by Government-aided loan programs?
It is unfortunate to read of urgings for laissez-faire policy when partnerships between public, private and community sectors are making small-business growth in inner-city neighborhoods as high a priority as affordable housing has been in comparable partnerships in the last 10 years.
 
MARCUS WEISS
Boston, July 26, 1996
 
The writer is a community development consultant to HUD and the Commerce Department.

HEADLINE: CURRENCY MARKETS;
Dollar Is Steady as Traders Await Week's Economic Data

BYLINE: By Bloomberg Business News 

 BODY:
The dollar was little changed yesterday despite news of a decline in Japanese industrial production and comments from a Bundesbank official that German interest rates could be cut further.
Currency traders were awaiting a number of economic reports this week that might point to higher interest rates in the United States. If reports on second-quarter labor costs today and July employment on Friday show quickening inflation and robust growth, the Federal Reserve may raise interest rates at its Aug. 20 policy meeting, traders said. Higher rates often help the dollar by making bank deposits in the United States more attractive. 
 "The best thing for the dollar would be good signs of growth," said Richard Koss, currency strategist at MFR Inc., a money management and consulting firm.
In late trading in New York yesterday, the dollar fell to 108.14 Japanese yen from 108.37 on Friday. The dollar traded at 1.4802 German marks, down from 1.4822 marks. The British pound rose to $1.5585, from $1.5545.
The dollar was also under pressure yesterday, as it was two weeks ago, from declines in American stock and bond markets.
"There is a general air of concern about the asset markets," said Kevin Raphael, manager of foreign exchange at Rabobank Nederland. "If the stock market stabilizes, the dollar can rise."
While traders awaited indications of the next move on United States interest rates, there were signs that Japanese and German rates would remain steady or head lower.
Japan reported yesterday that industrial production fell a larger-than-expected 3.9 percent in June.
"That put off the notion of a near-term hike in interest rates, which is what keeps hurting the dollar," Mr. Koss of MFR said.
In Germany, a Bundesbank council member, Franz-Christophe Zeitler, said German money-market rates could be cut further if money supply expansion slowed toward the central bank's target. Many analysts had expected the Bundesbank to lower its key 14-day repurchase rate at its council meeting on Thursday. When it refrained, the mark gained.
"The market was unimpressed with Zeitler's statements," Mr. Koss said. "They had every opportunity to cut rates last week, and they didn't."
