HEADLINE: COMAPNY REPORTS;
3M Earnings Climbed 7.9% in 2d Quarter

BYLINE: By Bloomberg Business News 

DATELINE: ST. PAUL, July 29

 BODY:
The Minnesota Mining and Manufacturing Company said today that its second-quarter earnings rose 7.9 percent, bolstered by strong sales in the United States and lower raw material costs.
The company earned $381 million, or 91 cents a share, in the quarter, up from $353 million, or 84 cents a share, in the quarter a year earlier. 
 But the 1995 quarter included earnings of $7 million a share on operations that have been discontinued, including the company's audio tape and videotape business, and other operations spun off as the Imation Corporation on July 1.
The most recent results were slightly better than the 90-cent average estimate of 11 analysts surveyed by Zacks Investment Research.
Shares of Minnesota Mining rose 50 cents today, to $63.875, on the New York Stock Exchange.
"The U.S. rebounded pretty sharply," said Jack Kelly, an analyst with Goldman, Sachs & Company. Unit sales in the United States rose 5 percent after being unchanged during the first quarter.
Sales in terms of the dollar rose 7 percent in the United States, with price increases adding two percentage points.
The company credited strong demand from the domestic auto, chemical, consumer and office markets. In its life sciences unit, gains in drug-delivery systems, commercial graphics, diaper tapes, dental products and safety and security systems were offset by slower sales of medical products.
Outside the United States, sales rose 8 percent, with volume up 7 percent and prices up 1 percent.
A stronger dollar, however, canceled out all of that gain. Minnesota Mining estimated currency exchange rates cut second-quarter earnings by 5 percent, or 4 cents a share.
Most of the international sales gains came in Japan, the rest of Asia and South America. Japanese unit sales increased 11 percent, helped by an improved economy and new products tailored for that market. Volume increased about 15 percent in Asia outside Japan and more than 20 percent in Latin America.
By contrast, Europe showed a 3 percent increase because of sluggish economies there.
Minnesota Mining was also helped in the quarter by lower costs. The cost of goods sold dropped to 56.3 percent of sales, from 60.3 percent in 1995 and 59.6 percent two years ago.
"Raw material costs, which had a substantial negative effect on gross margins in each of the past several quarters, started to show a small, positive impact this quarter," Giulio Agostini, the company's chief financial officer, told analysts at a San Francisco meeting.
He said the trend should continue to help earnings over the next few quarters.
Mr. Agostini also said the company expected 1996 earnings to rise by more than 10 percent.
"We haven't seen signs of slowing in major world economies versus what we experienced in the second quarter," he said.

HEADLINE: G.M.-Union Talks to Resume After Accord

BYLINE: Reuters 

DATELINE: DETROIT, July 29

 BODY:
The General Motors Corporation, removing a thorn in the side of the United Automobile Workers union that has held up contract talks, said on Friday that it had agreed to reinstate a U.A.W. official who was dismissed in April.
G.M. said Al Alli would return to his job at the Lordstown, Ohio, assembly plant, and would be reinstated as chairman of U.A.W. Local 1112 and will lead local plant negotiations. 
 Mr. Alli's dismissal on April 13 led to a three-day wildcat strike at the Lordstown plant, where G.M.'s popular Chevrolet Cavalier and Pontiac Sunfire small cars are built.
G.M. said Mr. Alli was discharged for using fraudulent time-card procedures to obtain pay for time that he had not worked. The auto maker said that two years ago, Mr. Alli's time and attendance cards were stamped to show that he was at work when he was in Las Vegas, Nev.
In a statement Friday, G.M.'s vice president of personnel, Gerald Knechtel, and a U.A.W. vice president, Richard Shoemaker, said Mr. Alli was reinstated "following in-depth discussions in the grievance procedure regarding the importance of employees properly recording their actual time at work, and management's requirement to properly pay such employees."
The agreement barred Mr. Alli from commenting. He will not receive back pay for the time he was unemployed, and a long-term disciplinary layoff will remain on his record, they said in the statement.
U.A.W. members said the dismissal of Mr. Alli, a popular leader among the plant's workers, had been a sticking point in triennial labor contract negotiations at G.M., which began in June.
In addition, G.M. and the U.A.W. said they had agreed to a "renewed commitment to promptly addressing issues raised by either party and maintaining and improving the Lordstown assembly plant operations and the quality of its products."

HEADLINE: Dow Falls 38.47 in Slowest Session of Year

BYLINE: By Reuters 

 BODY:
Stocks fell in the slowest session of the year yesterday as investors awaited a raft of economic reports this week that could determine whether the Federal Reserve raises interest rates.
The Dow Jones industrial average lost 38.47 points to close at 5,434.59.
In the broader market, declining issues beat advances by 1,465 to 905 on volume of only 281.6 million shares on the New York Stock Exchange. 
 It was the lightest trading volume since 252 million shares traded the day after Christmas last year.
The Nasdaq composite index fell 12.97 points, to 1,066.47. Nasdaq volume of 418 million shares was the second slowest this year, after 399 million shares on Jan. 2.
"I don't think anyone is prepared to do anything rash prior to the slug of important economic data we'll be getting over the course of this week," said Philip Orlando, chief investment officer at Value Line Asset Management.
If the Fed policy makers decide at their Aug. 20 meeting to raise short-term rates to ward off inflation, it could also hurt corporate earnings, thereby depressing stock prices.
The first report, due today, is the Employment Cost Index for the second quarter, which many analysts regard as a key indicator of a possible pickup in inflation. Analysts estimate the index rose nine-tenths of 1 percent after a seven-tenths of 1 percent increase in the first quarter.
The index will attract more attention than usual, coming nearly a month after the Government reported that average hourly earnings jumped 8 cents in June, the highest monthly increase since tabulations started 31 years ago.
The National Association of Purchasing Management's report on manufacturing is due on Thursday, the first broad glimpse of the economy's performance in July, and the July jobs report is due on Friday.
Yesterday's quiet stock market action was in stark contrast to the hectic trading linked to corporate earnings reports in recent sessions, and analysts said that earnings would take a back seat for now.
"Not many people are aggressively bidding stocks or bonds until they see how some of these numbers come out," Bill Meehan, an analyst at Prudential Securities, said.
With bond prices already dropping ahead of the economic reports, the reports themselves may prove anti-climactic for stocks, analysts said.
"It would be like getting a sprained ankle after a heart attack," said Roy Blumberg, chief investment strategist at Josephthal, Lyon & Ross.
Among the big movers in the stock market were companies involved in security after the pipe bomb explosion at the Atlanta Olympics and the T.W.A. plane crash off Long Island.
Barringer Technologies rose 2 1/4, to 10 3/8, on news that its Ionscan explosive detector met Federal Aviation Administration security rules.
Magal Security Systems fell 2 1/8, to 9, after hitting a 52-week high of 15 3/8. Josephthal, Lyon & Ross cut the stock to sell from hold, noting the shares have soared 450 percent since the July 17 crash.
The airport security personnel trainer CTS Holland Production rose 3 5/8, to 12 3/4.
CalFed Bancorp gained 1 1/4, to 22 5/8, after it announced a merger with First Nationwide Bancorp valued at $1.2 billion.
Newbridge Network slumped 6 1/2, to 41 5/8, after Needham & Company raised concerns over the company's earnings.
Reebok climbed 3 1/2, to 34 3/4, on news of a big stock buyback.
The Standard & Poor's composite index of 500 stocks fell 4.99 points, to 630.91.
The American Stock Exchange index lost 3.03, to 536.12.
The New York Stock Exchange composite index of all listed common stocks fell 2.23, to 338.64. The average share was down 24 cents.
The Wilshire Associates Equity Index -- the market value of Big Board, American and Nasdaq issues -- was 6,174.668, down 45.608, or 0.73 percent.

HEADLINE: Destructive Engagement in Myanmar

 BODY:
The Burmese dissident leader Daw Aung San Suu Kyi's quiet eloquence and courage have always spoken louder than her Government's attempts to silence her. But last week that Government won a double victory over the dissident. Her voice, it seems, cannot be heard over the rustle of money.
Earlier this month Mrs. Aung San Suu Kyi called for international economic sanctions against Myanmar, formerly Burma, for the first time. In May the State Law and Order Restoration Council, or Slorc, as the Government is known, arrested 262 members of Mrs. Aung San Suu Kyi's party, and the arrests go on. The crackdown is business as usual for a regime that put Mrs. Aung San Suu Kyi under house arrest for five years after her party won elections in 1990. The Slorc's brutality and opium trafficking have made Myanmar an outlaw state. 
 But Mrs. Aung San Suu Kyi's courage has produced a collective international shrug. Last week Myanmar assumed observer status at the Association of Southeast Asian Nations, a step toward membership. An Asean forum endorsed a policy of constructive engagement, seeking to open the nation gradually through trade and investment.
Constructive engagement in this case is not a policy, it is a dodge. Asian investment in Myanmar is growing. Given Asean's membership, which includes the authoritarian Governments of Indonesia, Vietnam, Brunei and Singapore, the indifference to democracy is not surprising.
The Slorc's second victory came in Washington when the Senate rejected a pullout of American investment in Myanmar proposed by Mitch McConnell and Daniel Patrick Moynihan. After intensive lobbying by Unocal, the American company with the largest financial interests in Myanmar, the Senate approved a threat of sanctions that can be imposed by the President if there is further harassment of Mrs. Aung San Suu Kyi and her supporters.
The Clinton Administration, which had been showing promising rhetorical gumption toward Myanmar, must insure that the Senate vote is not a dodge as well. Sanctions are not an all-purpose weapon, but President Clinton must use them forcefully here. The European Parliament has approved a non-binding sanctions resolution. The big investors, Britain and France, are under increasing pressure to pull out.
American investment in Myanmar over the last five years was $245 million, mostly in oil and gas. Most projects are joint ventures with government or military companies. The biggest American project is Unocal's stake in a proposed gas pipeline, also financed by the Slorc.
The Senate's willingness to bow before this minimal American investment shows the risks of constructive engagement. Instead of American engagement changing Burmese values and policies, the engagement seems to be affecting Washington. The United States has softened its policies toward an outlaw nation and let down a leader who deserves its full support.

HEADLINE: INTERNATIONAL BUSINESS;
Argentine Financial Markets Hold Steady

BYLINE: By CALVIN SIMS 

DATELINE: BUENOS AIRES, July 29

 BODY:
Argentina received a much-needed vote of confidence today as its financial markets remained steady, defying dire predictions that investors would abandon the debt-pressed country after the ouster of its Finance Minister, Domingo Cavallo, last week.
The Merval index of the 28 most-traded stocks on the Buenos Aires stock exchange -- which tumbled 4.1 percent on Friday -- rose 2.8 percent today, or 14.36 points, to 519.22, in heavy trading. Argentine bonds declined only slightly today, while in New York, Argentina's benchmark Brady bonds posted modest gains. Also, foreign-exchange operators reported no major increase in exchanging pesos for dollars, as was the case Friday. 
 Analysts and economists attributed the rise in Argentine stocks mainly to bargain hunters who snatched up undervalued shares following a 20 percent decline in the market during the last six weeks.
In addition, the analysts and economists said that investors were encouraged by statements made over the weekend by Mr. Cavallo's replacement, Roque Fernandez, former director of the central bank, who said that Argentina would not devalue its currency and would not stray from its current economic course.
Luis Corsiglia, a well-known stock broker here, said that today's gains signaled a partial recovery of the market and underscored rising confidence in Argentina among investors at home and abroad, especially since stocks in other Latin American countries closed down today.
"Many people thought the bottom was going to fall out of the stock market today, but clearly that did not happen and that bodes well for the future," Mr. Corsiglia said.
But many investors remain very nervous and Argentina's financial markets are expected to be rattled by volatility in coming weeks as Mr. Fernandez devises a plan to offset the country's huge budget deficit amid growing unrest over taxes.
Many investors are still wary following Mexico's currency crisis last year. Argentina is the Latin American country that most resembles Mexico in terms of economic reforms and debt.
"Whoever's buying so much stock must be extremely confident in Argentina's future, especially since we still don't know any specific plans of the new finance minister," said Roger Heale, an analyst with Interacciones, a local brokerage firm. "We prefer to wait another week or so."
President Carlos Saul Menem dismissed Mr. Cavallo on Friday after the two had a heated argument over the Finance Minister's belt-tightening measures.
"Roque Fernandez is an excellent, low-profile technician, and his team is, technically, perhaps even better than Cavallo's," said Ricardo Alverez Rojo, an independent economist. "However, he doesn't have the power to rein in the political lobbies. He is an office economist and he isn't street smart and this is very important here. In this respect, Cavallo was really savvy."

HEADLINE: Fearing Strife, U.N. Withholds Report on Death of Burundian

BYLINE: By BARBARA CROSSETTE 

DATELINE: UNITED NATIONS, July 29

 BODY:
Apparently fearful of provoking more violence in Burundi in the wake of a military coup last week by the Tutsi-dominated army, the Security Council is withholding from publication a report on the 1993 assassination of the country's first freely elected President, a Hutu.
The report, written by an international commission of inquiry set up last August by the Security Council, is believed to implicate or possibly identify people still prominent in Burundi. 
 Only representatives of the 15-member Council, which voted today to condemn the latest coup, are being permitted to read it. Members can examine the report for only 30 minutes and only in the private chambers of the Council President, who this month is France's representative, Alain Dejammet.
"This report has arrived on our doorstep at precisely the most sensitive time in terms of Burundi's internal political situation," one Western representative who has seen it said. "The timing could not be worse."
Hutu members of Burundi's just-dissolved Parliament and others who say they will resist the military takeover under Maj. Pierre Buyoya are charging that he will be implicated in the 1993 violence, although other less official investigations have not accused him in the assassination of President Melchior Ndadaye. But high-ranking officers in the Burundi's Tutsi-dominated army have been accused of involvement and might be incited to further violence.
Barnett Rubin, director of the Center for Preventive Action at the Council on Foreign Relations in New York, pointed out that the commission's report is not linked to any judicial process or other course of action that could make a difference.
"A lot of international efforts thus far consist of well intentioned statements without any adequate power to back them up, which then have the opposite effect of what was intended," he said.
Hutu politicians are also suspicious of Major Buyoya's links with the United States. Today the Agency for International Development said that it had given his private foundation more than $145,000 in the last two years for democratization programs. He has made several recent trips to the United States, including one to the Carter Center in Atlanta in February at the invitation of Jimmy Carter, who has been a leading mediator in the Great Lakes region of Africa.
Democratic Party officials said today that Major Buyoya had been invited before the coup to attend the Democratic National Convention in Chicago in August but that the invitation was being reconsidered.
Human rights advocates say they find it ironic that Africans, not Americans, have been loudest in their condemnation of the coup in Burundi last week. The United States and Europe have been prepared to give Major Buyoya a chance, while Africans have called for his removal from the office he seized by ejecting an elected president -- for the second time.

HEADLINE: Cray's Battle Might Be for A Lost Era

BYLINE: By JOHN MARKOFF 

 BODY:
Cray Research Inc. formally opened a trade battle over supercomputers yesterday, charging that the NEC Corporation had unfairly won a Federal contract by "dumping" its machine on the market at a price below the cost of building it. But unless Cray moves nimbly, it may find itself making the old soldier's mistake of fighting the last war.
Regardless of how sympathetic the United States Commerce Department may be to Cray's accusation that NEC, the Japanese computer giant, won a $35 million contract last spring from the National Center for Atmospheric Research by illegal underbidding, Cray will need to demonstrate that its own view of the supercomputer market remains valid. 
 And that could be a hard case to make in a high-technology industry where the fastest computers are now sometimes among the least expensive.
It is a very different industry from the one that Cray Research helped found nearly two decades ago, when supercomputers were one-of-a-kind machines wrought from custom-built hardware so sensitive they required specially designed cooling equipment. For a specialty company like Cray, a $20 million sale or two to a Government weapons laboratory, university or other supercomputer buyer could mean a profitable year -- then or now.
In the last decade, though, the high-performance computing world has come to use many of the same components that run inexpensive personal computers and work stations, and new computing technologies now routinely appear first in consumer electronics, not supercomputers. That shift is a trend that has put more than one supercomputer boutique out of business -- including Kendall Square Research and Supercomputer Systems in recent years.
Indeed, the trend so hobbled Cray Research that the company, a venerable mainstay of the supercomputing industry, found it necessary to sell itself earlier this year to the work station maker Silicon Graphics Inc. just to stay in business.
The economics of supercomputing now favor large, diversified technology companies like NEC of Japan -- or American behemoths like I.B.M., which on Friday won a $93 million contract from the Department of Energy to design and build the world's fastest supercomputer. I.B.M. is expected to draw upon its many existing in-house technologies as it works on the new machine.
Yesterday, in filing an antidumping petition with the Commerce Department and the International Trade Commission, Cray argued that NEC had underpriced its machines by as much as 454 percent.
The atmospheric-research contract was the first Federal supercomputing contract ever awarded to a Japanese company. And Cray, which has sold about a dozen supercomputers to the Japanese Government, said that if NEC's bid prevailed, it would hurt the United States industry.
The short-term effect would be a lost sale, Cray argued, and in the long term, prices in the American market would be artificially lowered.
"We've been ultraconservative in making our argument," said Earl Joseph, a market analyst at Cray Research who was involved in preparing its complaint.
But NEC officials in the United States said that Cray had made erroneous assumptions in analyzing NEC's actual cost of the supercomputer in question -- an NEC SX-4 -- and how many machines NEC could expect to sell in recouping its research and development expenses.
What may look like dumping to a speciality shop like Cray, NEC executives said, could be lucrative business to a big, broad-based company that can spread its development costs over a broad product line.
"There are serious problems with their estimates," said Samuel W. Adams, a vice president for NEC's United States subsidiary, HNSX Supercomputers Inc.
Cray executives contend that it is impossible for NEC to design and build the 128-processor weather and climate modeling supercomputer called for in the contract for the $35 million that was NEC's winning bid. But they would not disclose what their own bid was.
NEC executives counter that Cray is comparing an older supercomputer technology, known as bipolar logic, with a semiconductor technology called complementary metal on silicon, better known as CMOS. NEC has employed CMOS technology in personal computers and other consumer electronics products.
"The most serious flaw is that the Cray analysis is equating cost and prices of their current bipolar product line to our CMOS," Mr. Adams said. "CMOS is just much cheaper to make."
Analysts say that Cray has generally trailed the rest of the supercomputer industry in the shift from bipolar to CMOS technology.
And one supercomputer industry executive not involved in the disputed contract said that Cray's decision to seek Government relief was a sign of the company's increasingly difficult position in the face of changing technology and more aggressive foreign competition.
He noted that Cray was beaten by I.B.M. for the contract awarded on Friday for Lawrence Livermore Laboratories. "They lost the bid to I.B.M. at Livermore," he said, "and they can't cry foul as they did in the NEC bid."

HEADLINE: Governor Says Aides Earned Inaugural Pay

BYLINE: AP 

DATELINE: KINGSTON, N.Y., July 29

 BODY:
Gov. George E. Pataki today defended payments made to top campaign aides by corporations established to finance his inaugural celebration and transition into the Governor's office.
"They worked extremely hard and they had to get paid by someone," Mr. Pataki said. "They had to pay their bills." 
 He said he wished that information about the corporations' fund raising and spending had been made public earlier, adding that he had learned about the size of payments -- more than $50,000 in at least one case -- only in the last few days. The sources and beneficiaries of about $2.5 million in donations were released only after a successful court challenge by Assembly Democrats.
The decision to withhold the information until last Friday was made by officials at the corporations and not by him, Mr. Pataki said.
The Governor said he supported the idea of providing public funds for the official activities of incoming governors in the future, but he declined to say whether he would introduce such legislation.
Decisions about how much transition workers would be paid were made by the head of the transition committee, Robert E. Boyle, Mr. Pataki said. Mr. Boyle, who was treasurer of the fund-raising corporations, was appointed by Mr. Pataki to be president of the Jacob K. Javits Convention Center.
The Governor also said that he had not made the decisions concerning the lavishness of his three-day inaugural celebration in Albany.
"I had much higher priorities and I still do," Mr. Pataki said.
Michael C. Finnegan, the Pataki administration's chief counsel and an old friend of the Governor, was paid $53,750. Zenia Mucha, a former press secretary to Senator Alfonse M. D'Amato and now Mr. Pataki's director of communications, was paid $31,597. Robert Ryan, Mr. Pataki's campaign manager, was paid about $42,000. Mr. Ryan is now a top official in the state's economic development office. Mr. Boyle was paid $34,500.

HEADLINE: Mayors Fear Welfare Cuts May Increase Homelessness

BYLINE: By MICHAEL JANOFSKY 

DATELINE: PHILADELPHIA, July 29

 BODY:
For the second time in a week, more than 100 homeless people marched on City Hall today, protesting the city's decision to restrict access to shelters because of cuts in state and Federal social programs over the last few years.
In an announcement this month that single adults will now be turned away from the shelters as a cost-saving measure, Mayor Edward G. Rendell said that the city had already exceeded its current budget for the homeless by $4 million and that if access were not limited officials would be unable to satisfy the needs of homeless people in the winter, when the demand on shelters increases. 
 The new restrictions were roundly criticized by boisterous protesters as they convened in a plaza before moving into the corridor outside Mr. Rendell's office. Many held up signs of protest, and at one point they chanted, "Hey, Ed, give us a bed."
But advocates for the homeless here and elsewhere contend that Philadelphia's decision is only the beginning. They say that cutbacks in Federal programs, combined with changes in state welfare policies and the bills imposing time limits and spending restrictions on welfare that were recently passed by the House and Senate, would profoundly increase the nation's homeless population, leaving local governments ill-equipped to contend with a growing demand for services.
"When you have that kind of funding decrease, it's like taking away one third of your army in a battle you are already losing," said Mayor Marc Morial of New Orleans, a Democrat who is chairman of the United States Conference of Mayors Task Force on Hunger and Homelessness. "And the cuts we have experienced are certainly not as dramatic as those that are proposed."
This year, Federal spending for at least 10 programs that assist the homeless was cut from 1995, and in nearly every case, the financing proposed for 1997 under House and Senate bills would hold at current levels. And the most recent report on homelessness by the Conference of Mayors found that in 1995, requests for emergency shelter increased by an average of 11 percent over the previous year in 29 cities surveyed and that an average of 19 percent of the requests went unmet.
The National Law Center on Homelessness and Poverty, a nonprofit legal advocacy group in Washington, has predicted that thousands of families could become homeless if either the House or Senate version of welfare overhaul is signed into law.
"We're very concerned," said Maria Foscarinis, the executive director of the law center. "It's clear that both bills would increase the overall level of homelessness."
Supporters of the new Federal welfare bills disagree. They contend that block grants to the states, which would replace many Federal programs, would enable states to tailor programs to meet their own specific needs.
But like other opponents of the bills, including Senator Daniel Patrick Moynihan, a New York Democrat, and Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani of New York, a Republican, who contend that state welfare policies will be unable to prevent more people from falling into poverty, Mr. Morial said that "it's hard for anybody to make the case that homelessness is not going to get worse; you can only hope that it does not get dramatically worse."
While officials from many cities have already complained of adverse effects of cuts in Federal programs in recent years, Mr. Rendell is one of the first big city mayors to cite new state welfare regulations as a direct factor in the need to cut back local services.
In June, the Pennsylvania Legislature passed a measure that could cut off welfare benefits for more than 40,000 single men and medical benefits for as many as 220,000 more welfare recipients.
Mr. Rendell, a Democrat who called the state measure "inhumane and unconscionable," said that even before the new state legislation, the city experienced a 10 percent increase in the number of people seeking shelter services since last year, to 23,153 from 21,477. He predicted that the state measures would increase demand for shelters in Philadelphia over the next two years by 18,000 single adults in the city who are losing cash assistance and 62,000 who are losing medical benefits as a result of the change in policies.
But it is more than the growing number of those needing shelter that worries Mr. Rendell and other mayors as they strain to keep their programs operational. They say a rising tide of homelessness would ultimately cost states more than what welfare changes are meant to save, through an increase in crime, the need for job training programs and the economic loss from people who can no longer afford to rent housing and purchase goods.
"We're left with a Hobson's Choice," Mr. Rendell said in an interview. "Either we're letting our needs go unmet, creating havoc, with crime, homelessness and people living in the streets. Or we meet them dollar for dollar, forcing us to raise taxes and continue a horrible vicious cycle of losing our tax base, which gives us less revenue for programs."
"If that happens," he added, "it kills any chance for the future viability of cities. They'll drown."
Steven Black, 26, a high school graduate and former restaurant cook from North Philadelphia who became homeless after he was laid off, said he was turned away from a shelter by the new rules. But after weeks of sleeping on the streets, he learned that as a recovering substance abuser, he qualified for shelter under a different program.
Standing at the fringe of the demonstration, he said he has remained free of drugs and alcohol since moving off the streets.
But his view of the future is bleak. He said he worries that another change in state policy might force him out of this shelter, and he has no idea how he might become a productive citizen again.
"If I could, I would go back to school," he said. "I've always wanted to be a teacher. I'd love to go to college. But I'm no kid anymore, and I just don't see how I can get it done."

GRAPHIC: Photo: With Philadelphia having already exceeded its budget for homeless services, Mayor Edward G. Rendell has banned single adults from the city's shelters. The move was protested yesterday at City Hall. (Bill Cramer for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: BOMB AT THE OLYMPICS: THE VICTIMS;
Woman Who 'Lost Her Life While Celebrating the Human Spirit'

BYLINE: By RONALD SMOTHERS 

DATELINE: ATLANTA, July 29

 BODY:
Although Alice S. Hawthorne did not run a single lap or win a single competition, her name is likely to be long associated with the 1996 Summer Olympics as a casualty of the Games' spirit of harmony and openness.
Mrs. Hawthorne, who was 44, died in the Saturday morning bomb blast that sprayed shrapnel over a swath of Centennial Olympic Park, the 21-acre grass and brick-paved area that stands at the physical heart of the Olympic Games. The park had been reclaimed from slums and old warehouses to become a place for visitors and locals to meet, greet and celebrate -- no high-priced tickets or special passes needed. 
 "Mrs. Hawthorne will forever be remembered as one who lost her life while celebrating the human spirit at the Olympics," said Gordon Joyner, a member of the Fulton County Commission.
Today, Mr. Joyner joined other elected officials and various celebrities in rallying around Mrs. Hawthorne's family: her husband, John, who works for the City of Albany's Community and Economic Development Department; her 14-year-old daughter, Fallon Stubbs, who is recuperating at Georgia Baptist Hospital here from wounds suffered in the blast, and an extended network of grandparents, aunts, uncles and cousins.
Family members learned just this morning about the memorial service planned by the Atlanta Committee for the Olympic Games for tomorrow, soon after the park reopens to the public. The service, involving public officials, senior representatives of the Olympics and athletes, will honor Mrs. Hawthorne; the Turkish television cameraman, Melih Uzunyol, who suffered a heart attack while rushing to cover the incident, and the 110 people who were injured in the blast.
But in Albany, Ga., some 200 miles south of here, where Mrs. Hawthorne lived, people who knew her insisted that she would be much more than a footnote to the 1996 Summer Games. A former United States Army lieutenant, she was a co-owner of Fallon's Ice Cream Parlor and an employee of the local cable television company. Friends cited her tireless civic work for the Chamber of Commerce. And they pointed out that she was one of the first women to be accepted as a member of the American Legion chapter.
Mrs. Hawthorne, a volunteer in the city's literacy program, had completed a chamber-sponsored leadership training program and was managing the campaign of a local candidate for the State Legislature.
But virtually everyone who spoke of Mrs. Hawthorne agreed that she will best be remembered as someone who brought people together. "The Hawthorne family lived their lives for others," Mayor Tommy Coleman of Albany said. "And this lady will be remembered as a person who believed in the coming together of others, and not because of a cowardly act of violence."
Mr. Coleman added, "We all feel a deep sense of loss for our community."
Curt Kennedy, Mrs. Hawthorne's business partner at the ice cream parlor, stood in the shop today where a simple wreath with satin ribbons adorned the door. On the counter was a vase of carnations and lilies; leaning against it, a hand-lettered sign saying, "We feel your loss and sorrow."
Mr. Kennedy's sadness could barely suppress his anger as he noted that burglars had broken into the Hawthorne home sometime Sunday morning, making off with a television and a VCR.
Today, while the police were stationed outside the home in a quiet middle-class subdivision, Mr. Kennedy could only shake his head and ask: "How can all this happen because a mother took her daughter to the Olympics as part of a birthday present?"
Friends in Albany said that Fallon, who turned 14 years old on July 24, had asked her mother to take her to the Games. The youngster had been close enough to the blast to suffer large gash-like wounds, doctors reported. Still, they said today that she was in good condition and was likely to be released on Tuesday.
Her visitors today ranged from local elected officials to Andrew Young, the co-chairman of the Atlanta Committee for the Olympic Games, to Olympic athletes like Matt Ghaffari, a silver medalist in wrestling. What they found was a talkative girl who seems to have fended off the shock of her loss, and a family whose initial bitterness at feeling ignored by Olympics officials had subsided. Over the weekend, Mr. Hawthorne had complained that no senior representative of the Games had spoken with the family.
Speaking a few hours after he had visited Fallon in her hospital room, the Rev. Jesse Jackson said today, "She realizes that her mother is dead and she will eventually have to deal with that."
He added, "She talked about what college she wants to go to -- Georgetown University -- and I saw tremendous strength there."
Mr. Joyner brought gifts from his own 14-year-old daughter: one of the original Atlanta 1996 pins minted when the city was announced as the site of the summer games, and a Teddy bear dressed in stars and stripes and carrying a balloon saying "You're Awesome."
In talking with family members, Mr. Joyner said he sensed a certain calm that overlay their grief.
"They didn't have any hate in their hearts and they didn't mention a thing about the investigation or wanting someone to be caught," he said. "These were warm people and they seemed mostly concerned today about Fallon getting better."
Mr. Ghaffari said that Fallon had spoken not of loss, but of his size -- he weighs 286 pounds. Fallon, he said, asked if he would be her bodyguard if she can get tickets to see the Dream Team play, as Atlanta's Mayor, Bill Campbell, has promised. He agreed.

HEADLINE: Summary of Major Actions in the 219th Session of the Legislature

BYLINE: By JAMES DAO 

DATELINE: ALBANY, July 29

 BODY:
The New York State Legislature concluded its 219th session at 5:28 P.M. on July 13 in a mad, confusing rush to adjournment, as Gov. George E. Pataki linked final negotiations on the state budget to many nonbudget bills.
The year began with a major confrontation between the Governor and Legislature, when both houses voted to override Mr. Pataki's veto of a bill that would make it easier for New York City police officers to win larger salary increases. It was the first time the Legislature had overturned a veto in more than a decade. 
 The conflict continued for much of the session, as Mr. Pataki and leaders from the Republican-controlled Senate and Democratic-controlled Assembly hit impasse after impasse over the budget. The Legislature finally enacted the spending plan in the session's closing hours, 104 days overdue. It was probably the latest state budget in the nation's history.
For all the budget squabbling, though, the Legislature had several major accomplishments this year. It approved changes in the state's $5 billion workers' compensation system, which are expected to cut employers' insurance premiums. It required the state to tell mothers the results of H.I.V. tests on their newborns. It discarded the state system of setting hospital rates, allowing the hospitals to negotiate prices directly with insurance companies. It made it much easier for candidates to get on the ballot.
The Assembly passed 1,042 of the 2,900 bills introduced in that house. The State Senate passed 1,358 of 2,253. A total of 811 bills passed both houses. Of those, Mr. Pataki has signed 344 and vetoed 18. The other bills are awaiting the Governor's decision on whether to sign them into law.
These are the major actions of the year:
 
The Budget
Mr. Pataki introduced his budget a month early, on Dec. 15, hoping to make good on his campaign pledge to end the state's run of 11 straight late budgets. It became not just the 12th consecutive overdue budget, but the latest in the state's history, eclipsing the previous record by five weeks.
The Governor's original plan was based heavily on expected changes in Federal entitlement policies that would have authorized the state to drastically reduce spending on welfare and Medicaid, the health care program for the poor. Mr. Pataki also called for sharp cuts to mental health services, higher education and programs for disabled students. He proposed block grants under which local governments would receive less money for entitlement programs but be given more freedom in spending it.
His proposal would have reduced spending from state taxes by $1.9 billion, or 5.7 percent, which would have represented the second consecutive decline.
But two things undermined the Governor's plan. First, the Federal Government failed to enact the policy changes his budget was based on, removing pressure on the Legislature to make major entitlement cuts. Second, all of the Legislature's 211 seats are up for election this year, and almost none of the lawmakers, Republican or Democrat, were in the mood to reduce spending.
In the end, the Legislature restored most of the Governor's cuts to Medicaid, welfare and education. They rejected his major criminal justice ideas and his block-grant plan. But in a nod to voters, they maintained the second year of a three-part income tax reduction, added new business tax cuts worth $83 million this year and included $150 million for favored local programs, known as members' items.
The final budget is expected to spend $33.1 billion from state taxes, about the same as last year, while spending from state and Federal sources is expected to reach $66 billion, up 2 percent.
As the session drew to a close, several lawmakers proposed measures intended to force the Legislature to enact the budget on time. But they decided not to take up the debate until after the elections.
 
Welfare and Families
 
LEGISLATION SIGNED
Authorizes the state to release confidential records in certain child-abuse cases, when a child has died, for example, or when someone has been charged with abuse.
Raises the number of people allowed to live in foster-family boarding homes as a way of encouraging the authorities to place siblings together.
Requires state family court judges to consider domestic violence as a factor in child custody cases.
 
LEGISLATION FAILED
Would have set a 60-day time limit on Home Relief, the welfare program for childless, able-bodied adults, and a five-year lifetime limit on receiving Aid to Families with Dependent Children.
Would have reduced welfare benefits by an average of 27 percent.
Would have allowed welfare recipients to keep more of their earnings from work without losing any welfare grants.
Would have prevented the state from increasing benefits to families who have additional children while on welfare.
Would have authorized local governments to conduct drug tests on welfare recipients and applicants and to require treatment as a condition for receiving benefits.
 
Environment and Energy
 
LEGISLATION SIGNED
Increases civil penalties for unlawful taking of bears.
Requires Fresh Kill Landfill on Staten Island to close by 2002.
Allows local legislators to decide whether to fluoridate publicly owned water supplies.
 
AWAITING GOVERNOR'S SIGNATURE
Would place a question asking voters whether the state should sell $1.75 billion in bonds for environmental projects on the November ballot.
Would allow 30,000 homeowners to install solar cells and reduce their electric bills, to zero if possible.
 
LEGISLATION FAILED
Would have allowed owners of billboards to cut down trees that obscured their advertisements.
Would have allowed the snaring of beavers in certain areas.
Would have allowed Hunter Mountain Ski Resort to acquire state forest preserve land to expand its ski slopes.
Would have banned commercial logging in all state parks.
Would have allowed citizens to sue companies that violate state pollution laws.
 
Taxes
 
LEGISLATION SIGNED
Reduces taxes on railroad and trucking companies.
Eliminates a 10 percent tax on profits from the sale of real estate worth over $1 million.
Exempts the first 250 acres of a farmer's land from local property taxes.
Exempts clothing items worth less than $500 from the state's 4 percent sales tax for one week next January, as a one-time experiment.
Allows local governments to grant property tax exemptions to people who are totally and permanently disabled.
 
LEGISLATION FAILED
Would have created a tax deduction for the purchase of long-term insurance covering nursing home care.
 
Health and Insurance
 
LEGISLATION SIGNED
Requires hospitals and insurance companies to provide 48-hour maternity stays to mothers.
Sets up a program to test newborns for H.I.V., the virus that causes AIDS, and notify their mothers of the results.
Increases availability of homeowner's insurance in coastal and other areas where it is often difficult to get coverage.
Prohibits insurance companies from discriminating against people who are victims of domestic violence.
Sets up a registry to track the use of pesticides to aid scientists in determining chemicals' relationship to cancer and other diseases.
 
AWAITING GOVERNOR'S SIGNATURE
Would deregulate state hospitals by lifting the controls on how much hospitals can charge health insurance companies.
Would provide a range of new protections for people who enroll in managed care plans.
Would stiffen rules for doctors who are negligent or commit malpractice, in an effort to prevent them from practicing.
 
LEGISLATION FAILED
Would have banned cigarette and alcohol advertisements near schools and playgrounds.
Would have required insurance companies to pay for certain experimental treatments.
Would have lifted decades-old regulations that limited the sizes of commissions for life insurance agents in an effort to curb certain illegal practices.
Would have denied health care services, except in emergencies, to illegal immigrants at publicly financed health care centers.
Would have made it a Class E felony to knowingly perform a so-called partial birth abortion.
 
LEGISLATION VETOED
Would have required insurance companies to pay for chiropractic care.
 
Government and Ethics
 
LEGISLATION SIGNED
Would encourage the state to use vegetable-based ink in its printing operations.
 
AWAITING SIGNATURE
Would repeal some of the rules that make it difficult for candidates to qualify to run in elections.
Would allow victims of domestic violence to use special ballots so they can vote if they feel their safety is in jeopardy.
Would establish a procedure to insure the confidentiality of genetic-testing records.
 
LEGISLATION FAILED
Would have improved the public's access to the state's campaign finance records by putting them on computers.
Would have set up a system to finance election campaigns with state money.
Would have loosened the rules for qualifying for the ballot for the Republican Presidential primary.
Would have restricted campaign contributions that are given to political parties for their general expenses.
Would have prohibited discrimination based on sexual orientation in employment, credit, public accommodations, education and other areas.
 
LEGISLATION VETOED
Would have penalized people who improperly disclose information from a statewide registry of orders of protection.
 
Consumer Affairs
 
LEGISLATION SIGNED
Requires child protective devices on shopping carts.
Expands protections governing new motorized wheelchairs.
Insures that consumers who pay for a property appraisal, or a consumer credit report, to get a loan, have a right to obtain a copy of the report.
 
AWAITING GOVERNOR'S SIGNATURE
Requires auto dealers to provide more information on whether a customer is being charged for securing registration or certificate of title.
 
LEGISLATION FAILED
Would have imposed stiffer penalties on automobile rental agencies that refuse to rent to people because of where they live.
Would have significantly increased the penalties for false advertising.
Would have required that bottled water be labeled with an expiration date.
Would restrict access to minors of pornographic telephone services by requiring that users of these services have a personal identification number.
 
Crime and Justice
 
LEGISLATION SIGNED
Allows family members of a slaying victim to speak at the sentencing of the defendant.
Reverses provisions in current law that require children to remember specific times when incidents of repeated sexual abuse occurred.
Makes possession of child pornography a Class E felony.
Toughens penalties for throwing bodily fluids at prison guards.
Prohibits sex between prison guards and inmates.
 
AWAITING GOVERNOR'S SIGNATURE
Would toughen penalties for violating orders of protection.
Would allow fingerprinting of juveniles age 13 to 15 charged with certain felonies.
Would authorize judges to require use of electronic monitoring devices.
Would increase prison sentences for assault in the first degree, gang assaults and attacks on police officers, peace officers, firefighters, paramedics and emergency medical technicians.
Would authorize the State Crime Victims Board to provide benefits to victims of international acts of terrorism committed outside the United States.
Would allow members of a victim's family to make a statement before sentencing on behalf of a victim when victims are unable to express themselves.
Would authorize the possession and use of self-defense sprays like Mace.
 
LEGISLATION FAILED
Would have eliminated parole for all violent felons and required the state to build new maximum security prisons to house them.
Would have given prosecutors the right to appeal sentences and bail amounts.
Would have loosened the rules that the authorities must follow in searching for and seizing evidence from criminal suspects.
Would have stiffened penalties for juvenile offenders and required that juvenile offenders older than 16 be transferred to adult prisons from Division for Youth detention centers.
Would have given judges the authority to order H.I.V. tests for people charged with a range of offenses.
Would have banned assault weapons and increased penalties for those who commit crimes with them.
Would have increased penalties for obstructing abortion clinics and other types of health clinics.
Would have enacted new penalties against people who commit acts of violence or intimidation based on an individual's race, color, religion, gender, age or sexual orientation.
Would have made convicted criminals bear all or part of the costs of their crimes to victims and government.
 
LEGISLATION VETOED
Would have changed the way that the police and district attorneys make crime victims aware of their rights and services available to them.
 
Agriculture
 
LEGISLATION SIGNED
Gives farmers who earn less than $150,000 a year an annual property-tax credit that would total $3,000 to $4,000 by the year 2000.
Requires people who buy or sell cattle, horses, swine, deer or camels to obtain state health permits that verify that the animals have no diseases.
Authorizes the Commissioner of Agriculture and Markets to create and enforce regulations to control the spread of infectious diseases among farm animals.
 
AWAITING GOVERNOR'S SIGNATURE
Would toughen licensing requirements for dealers of farm products to protect farmers from fraud.
 
LEGISLATION FAILED
Would have made it illegal to force-feed an animal for nonmedical reasons.
Would have made it a misdemeanor for failing to provide animals food, water, shelter and veterinary care.
 
 Education
 
LEGISLATION SIGNED
Gives residents in cities with fewer than 125,000 people the right to approve or reject their local school district's budget during annual referendums.
Allows the New York City Schools Chancellor the authority to suspend local school boards or board member for mismanagement, corruption or other wrongdoing.
 
AWAITING GOVERNOR'S SIGNATURE
Would toughen eligibility requirements for preschool children entering summer special education programs and limit the role of service providers in referring preschoolers into special education programs.
 
LEGISLATION FAILED
Would have turned the New York City Board of Education into a city agency, giving the mayor control over the system's $8 billion budget and the selection of the chancellor.
Would have broken up the Board of Education into five separate school boards, one for each borough.
Would have abolished the city's 32 community school boards and expanded the number of seats on the Board of Education.
Would have allowed teachers to expel misbehaving students from their classrooms.
Would have barred New York City from shifting state financial aid for schools to other city agencies.
Would have required that at least one third of the Governor's appointees to the board of the State University of New York be former employees of the system or graduates of one of its schools.
 
 Business and Labor
 
 LEGISLATION VETOED BUT OVERRIDDEN
Changes the way New York City resolves contract disputes with its police and fire unions.
 
LEGISLATION SIGNED
Prohibits retailers from selling goods that were produced in illegal sweatshops and collects back wages from sweatshop operators who do not pay their employees.
Extends a state law for one yearprohibiting any decrease in health benefits for retired employees of public school districts and boards.
 
AWAITING GOVERNOR'S SIGNATURE
Would require the state to establish a bureau to detect fraud in the state's $5 billion workers' compensation system, lower insurance premiums for businesses with good safety records and shield businesses from certain kinds of lawsuits.
 
LEGISLATION FAILED
Would require employers to keep records on toxic substances that their employees come into contact with in the workplace.
Would have provided unemployment insurance benefits to employees who decide to leave their jobs because of sexual harassment.
Would have raised the state's minimum wage to $5.05 from $4.25 in what would have been the first increase since 1991.
Would have allowed victims of sexual assault to collect disability benefits if they are attacked while on the job.
Would have imposed sanctions against employers who discriminate against employees for taking time off from work to serve on juries.
Would have allowed employees to take up to 16 weeks of unpaid leave for serious medical or family reasons.
 
Transportation
 
LEGISLATION SIGNED
Increases the fines for parking illegally in a space reserved for handicapped people.
Stiffens penalties against people under the age of 21 who are found to have any alcohol in their system while driving.
Toughens the requirements for obtaining a handicapped parking permit.
 
AWAITING GOVERNOR'S SIGNATURE
Would allow for the construction of a rest stop on the Long Island Expressway.
Would increase penalties against drivers who pass a school bus that is discharging or picking up passengers.
 
LEGISLATION VETOED
Would have removed Spring Valley tolls on the New York State Thruway.

HEADLINE: POLITICS: THE REPUBLICAN;
Dole Finds Environment A Problem And an Issue

BYLINE: By ADAM NAGOURNEY 

DATELINE: ANDERSON, Calif., July 29

 BODY:
For the second successive day, Bob Dole used a Western setting today to assail President Clinton's environmental policies, asserting that local economies had suffered from the Administration's allegiance to protecting wildlife and open land.
The venue today was a timber mill in this remote pocket of Northern California, and the topic was restrictions on logging that Mr. Dole said had devastated timber communities between here and the Canadian border. But, in an indication of the difficulties Mr. Dole faces in turning the environmental issue to his advantage, today he scaled back the harsh attack on Mr. Clinton's environmental record that he had offered on Sunday in Montana, in deference, aides said, to California's sensitivity to environmental concerns. 
 In Billings on Sunday night, with a farmhouse at his back and logs stacked on flatbed trucks to his side, Mr. Dole mocked "Bill Clinton's war on the West," which he said was dictated by "extremists who don't have the slightest regard for the Western economy, for jobs or for the rights of property owners."
Today, standing outside a lumber mill with the smell of sawdust in the air, he made no mention of the "war on the West." Instead, he offered a narrower attack on Mr. Clinton's opposition to a Republican-sponsored Congressional measure that would allow the logging of diseased and dead trees on public lands.
"The men and women who work in this industry have been abandoned -- and let me underscore -- abandoned, by this Administration," Mr. Dole said, under a scorching sun on a day on which the temperature reached 87 degrees by 7:15 A.M. "That's a fact. You've been abandoned by this Administration. You and your community have suffered too much.
"I don't believe we need to choose between a strong economy and a safe environment," he said. "It's a false choice. We can have both in America." He continued: "We can have both right here in California. We don't need to pit one against another.
"We can have both," Mr. Dole said. "We can have both -- an Administration that wants to have both. If you don't want to have both, you're not going to have both. In a Dole administration, we will make it work."
Mr. Dole's remarks today were less pointed than either what he said in Montana, or what his senior California adviser, Ken Khachigian, had wanted him to say in what will be Mr. Dole's last visit to this state before the convention in San Diego begins in two weeks. "There are a lot of voters in this state who feel there are a lot of environmental extremists," Mr. Khachigian said to reporters before Mr. Dole spoke. "They have indulged in greenery run amok."
But after Mr. Dole finished his considerably milder remarks on the topic, Mr. Khachigian said: "The meat wasn't as red as some people might have liked. But the point was made."
Mr. Dole's aides say that winning the West is critical to Mr. Dole's hope of victory in November and that tapping into the resentment among many property owners to environmental policies that have blocked land development is critical to winning the West. But in California, where polls show Mr. Dole trailing far behind Mr. Clinton, the environment has historically been an issue that helps determine elections. And that accounted for the different tone Mr. Dole struck here, his aides said.
"It's a tough issue to make too much ground on because this state is so strongly in favor of the environment," said Mark DiCamillo, director of the California Field Poll. "You can't mine it too much. It's one of the quote-unquote motherhood issues in California."
That view was reflected in Mr. Dole's audience here today. James White, 33, owner of a small plumbing business, said he was reconsidering his support for President Clinton out of concern that he had not done enough to deal with terrorism in the United States -- but Mr. White stopped short when he was asked if he shared Mr. Dole's concern about excessive environmental regulation.
"This is a resort community," Mr. White said. "We need to protect it while we can. A lot of us live here."
Mr. Clinton's campaign spokesman, Joe Lockhart, said that Mr. Dole was "practicing the politics of the past -- pitting business interests against the protectors of the environment." Mr. Lockhart also challenged Mr. Dole's statistics about job loss in the timber industry, by adducing statistics from the Bureau of Labor Statistics showing that 3,100 logging and milling jobs had in fact been gained in Washington, Oregon and California during Mr. Clinton's tenure. There were 41,400 jobs lost in the same sector in the eight years President Bush was in office.
Mr. Dole's aides said their figures had come from a paid consultant to the forest industry.
 
In His Own Words
 
BOB DOLE
 
At a timber mill yesterday in Anderson, Calif.:
"This election presents some very important choices for people who work in America's timber industry. After four years in office, President Clinton now has a record on timber issues. It's not a record to be proud of, and it's certainly not a record that deserves to be continued for four more years.
The fact is we still have a system of court-managed gridlock. Timber supplies from public lands have all but disappeared, replaced by imports from other countries. And today the U.S. imports 35 percent of our lumber from Canada. That shouldn't be happening in America. We have the timber. We have the resources. We have the work force. And we have people who want jobs. And that figure has grown. More American mills have closed. More breadwinners have lost their jobs. And more communities have been devastated. These were good, family-wage jobs that kept a family together, gave them opportunities for education, gave them opportunities to spend more time with their family and gave them opportunities where only one person a family had to work. And when they disappeared, entire towns and villages were cast into depression."

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

 BODY:

Judges Rule Against Law To Ban Internet Smut
Three Federal judges in New York ruled that the Government's attempts to halt indecent material on the Internet were unconstitutionally broad and voted to grant a temporary injunction against enforcing the Communications Decency Act, which President Clinton signed on Feb. 8.
The ruling added emphasis to an earlier decision in Philadelphia that affirmed First Amendment rights in cyberspace. The Justice Department plans to appeal it. [Page A11.]
 
Stocks Down in Slow Trading
Stocks fell in the slowest session of the year, 281.6 million shares, as investors awaited a raft of economic reports that could determine whether the Fed raises interest rates. The Dow industrials lost 38.47 points, and the Nasdaq composite 12.97 points. [D8.]
 
Prices of Treasury securities dropped sharply, sending the yield on the 30-year bond up to 7.09 percent, from 7.01 percent on Friday. [D15.]
 
California Bank Merger Set
First Nationwide Bank FSB has agreed to buy California Federal Bancorp for $1.2 billion in cash, or $23.50 a share. [D2.]
 
Shareholders of Carver Federal Savings Bank voted to approve a restructuring plan. [B3.]
 
Humana Executive Resigns
Humana Inc. said that its chief financial officer, W. Roger Drury, had resigned, and that it was reorganizing to improve cost controls and customer service. [D15.]
 
Broadcasters in Education Accord
The broadcast industry, acting before President Clinton convened a conference on children's television, agreed to require stations to show three hours of children's educational programming a week. [A8.]
 
Novel Rocket for Shuttle Successor
Engineers at Lockheed Martin are building a new type of engine called the aerospike that turns rocketry inside out. [C1.]
 
New Prodigy Executives
Prodigy took its first steps as an independent company under its new owner, International Wireless, and received new executives. [D2.]
 
J.& J. Sues, Opposing Arbitrator
Johnson & Johnson filed suit, seeking to overturn an arbitrator's ruling that it must hand control of its H.I.V. home-testing business back to a dismissed former executive who developed the test. [D4.]
 
Argentine Markets Hold Steady
Argentina received a much-needed vote of confidence as its financial markets remained steady, despite the ouster of Finance Minister Domingo Cavallo last week. The Merval index of the 28 most-traded stocks rose 2.8 percent, or 14.36 points, to 519.22, in heavy trading. [D7.]
 
A Firm's Rush to Go Public
GKN Securities, a New York brokerage that brings small companies to market, plans to go public itself this week, despite S.E.C. investigations hanging over its head. Floyd Norris: Market Place. [D8.]
 
Selling Computers to Women
More women than ever are buying and using personal computers, but advertising for high-technology products continues to be aimed at men, a new study by Intelliquest for Conde Nast shows. Stuart Elliott: Advertising. [D5.]

HEADLINE: AGREEMENT STRUCK ON MOST ELEMENTS FOR WELFARE BILL

BYLINE: By ROBERT PEAR 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, July 29

 BODY:
House and Senate negotiators said today that they had reached agreement on most provisions of a welfare bill they will send to President Clinton. On several issues, it appears, they have disregarded his appeals for changes in the legislation.
The bill, scheduled for floor votes in both chambers this week, would end the Federal guarantee of cash assistance for the nation's poorest children and would instead give each state a lump sum of Federal money to run its own welfare and work programs. 
 After meeting with Mr. Clinton at the White House today, Speaker Newt Gingrich said, "We have every reason to believe the President will sign this."
But Mr. Gingrich did not say why he had reached that conclusion, and White House officials said little. One Democrat on Capitol Hill said, "They don't seem to want to tell anybody" what the President will do.
As the Congressional negotiators moved to resolve differences between the two chambers, they moderated the most stringent provisions of the House bill. But contrary to what Mr. Clinton had said he hoped for, they did not grant benefits beyond those offered in the Senate bill.
For example, the conferees agreed that legal immigrants who have not become citizens should generally be denied benefits and that families dropped from welfare should not be eligible for vouchers to buy supplies for their children.
In a joint news conference today, religious, civil rights and antipoverty groups joined representatives of labor unions in saying the bill was beyond repair and should be vetoed. Among the groups were the National Urban League, the Children's Defense Fund, the National Conference of Catholic Bishops and the Union of American Hebrew Congregations.
Their news conference kicked off a weeklong lobbying effort by critics of the bill and its supporters alike, among them Republican governors, who urged Mr. Clinton today to sign it.
For Mr. Clinton, a major question now is whether to speak out before Congress votes on the revised measure, so as to influence the outcome. If he is silent or equivocal, many Democrats will most likely cross over and join Republicans in supporting the bill. Democrats on both sides of the Capitol said tonight that they were pleading with the White House for a clear signal of the President's intentions.
A White House official said, "From what we have heard, it's encouraging that the conferees are staying close to the Senate bill," which the President prefers over the House version. But "we have not seen the final product yet," said the official, speaking on the condition of anonymity.
Another Administration official said, "The President's Cabinet is at odds with his political advisers on the question of whether he should sign or veto the welfare bill." Cabinet officers express scorn for the bill, while the political advisers want Mr. Clinton to sign it to show that he is in step with public opinion, this official said.
Welfare has emerged as a major issue in this year's Presidential race, and Mr. Clinton, who promised during his 1992 campaign to "end welfare as we know it," has repeatedly said he wants to sign a bill overhauling the welfare system.
But if he signs the bill that he will receive in the next few days, he will alienate many of his most loyal constituents within the liberal wing of the Democratic Party.
Democrats have been involved in meetings of the conference committee but say they have played only a limited role in major decisions. A top Democratic Congressional aide, who had fragmentary information on the agreement now emerging, said the Republicans had moderated the bill and "sanded down the rough edges except in the area of restricting benefits for legal immigrants, where they perceive no political disadvantage in being barbaric."
On the other hand, Representative Lamar Smith, Republican of Texas, defended the Republicans' decision to deny most forms of public assistance to legal immigrants who have not become citizens. "There must be a distinction between citizens and noncitizens," Mr. Smith said in an interview. "Shouldn't we give priority to needy citizens rather than people who have voluntarily chosen to retain their allegiance to another country?"
The conference committee decided to retain a broad prohibition that would make noncitizen immigrants ineligible for food stamps, Supplemental Security Income and most other types of public assistance financed by the Federal Government. The conferees agreed to make an exception for Head Start, job training and some education programs that would, in effect, help immigrants help themselves.
And House and Senate negotiators said they had decided to give states the option of providing or denying Medicaid to legal immigrants already in the United States.
But a Republican Congressional aide involved in the negotiations said, "Under no circumstances will we allow handouts -- cash welfare -- to go to noncitizens."
The conferees also decided to buttress a five-year lifetime limit on cash welfare by prohibiting states from using Federal block grants to provide vouchers or other noncash benefits to families who reach the limit. Mr. Clinton had sought such vouchers to help families buy diapers, clothing, medicine and school supplies for their children.
While the conferees will make clear that states cannot provide vouchers with money they receive under welfare block grants, however, the states could finance them under a separate law that authorizes social services for the poor.
Negotiators said they had also agreed on tough new work requirements for food-stamp recipients. Adults who are unemployed and have no dependents could get food stamps for a maximum of six months in any three-year period.
This is less harsh than the provision in the House bill, which would have allowed a person who was not working and had no dependents to receive food stamps for only three months out of the entire 32-year period from age 18 to age 50.
The Senate Democratic leader, Tom Daschle of South Dakota, denounced the House provision on food stamps today, saying it was "one of the most egregious examples of the extremist approach the House took on welfare reform."
Robert Greenstein, director of the Center on Budget and Policy Priorities, said that even the compromise provision was draconian, because it would deny food stamps to people who were willing to work but unable to find jobs. He estimated that 900,000 people could lose benefits.
Republicans said the public supported this type of work requirement. Mr. Lott said it was unthinkable that people should "be able to get food stamps indefinitely, every year forever."
House and Senate negotiators said they had tentatively decided to drop the idea of a food-stamp block grant, which would have been an option for the states under the House bill but not under the Senate version. The final bill thus keeps the Federal guarantee of food stamps for the poor, with uniform eligibility standards and benefit amounts, as Mr. Clinton wanted.
But the compromise bill would still cut projected spending on food stamps by roughly $24 billion over six years, with at least $3 billion more saved by denying food stamps to legal immigrants. Thus, it appears, the conferees did not scale back these cuts, as Mr. Clinton had wanted.
The conferees have tentatively agreed to bar illegal immigrant children from the national school lunch program, even though such children are legally allowed to attend public schools. But they have not decided what penalties, if any, should be imposed on schools that serve subsidized lunches to those children.
