HEADLINE: President Finds Benefits In Defeat on Health Care

SERIES: THE CLINTON RECORD: Health Care -- Third of seven articles.

BYLINE: By MICHAEL WINES and ROBERT PEAR 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, July 29

 BODY:
It has been three years since President Clinton and his wife, Hillary, set out to transform American medicine with a plan for universal, affordable health care. And today, signs of revolution are everywhere.
Health maintenance organizations are booming: more than 58 million Americans are enrolled in prepaid health plans. Three in four doctors have converted at least part of their private practice into work for the H.M.O.'s and health-care companies that increasingly control the supply of patients and the cost of treatments. Patrician medical establishments -- like Massachusetts General Hospital in Boston and New York Hospital-Cornell Medical Center in Manhattan -- that once seemed impervious to commercial pressures are racing into mergers to guarantee their survival. 
 In some respects, it is what the Clintons' army of health-care cognoscenti envisioned: medicine made more affordable by a long-overdue dose of competition.
But it is not the President's revolution. Mr. and Mrs. Clinton's proposed overhaul foundered in Congress two years ago, a spectacular defeat that overshadowed other Administration goals and left the President nearly mute on the subject. Indeed, the President has passed on opportunities to attack Republicans in Congress for blocking what was, by any measure, a revolutionary change in domestic policy.
Looking back, it is difficult to believe that Mr. Clinton's health plan was the very centerpiece of his Presidency -- "our most urgent priority," as he told Congress in a grand unveiling of the plan in September 1993. Then, the health-care system was not just in crisis, but was an imminent threat to the financial security of American families.
"Millions of Americans are just a pink slip away from losing their health insurance, and one serious illness away from losing all their savings," Mr. Clinton told Congress then. "And on any given day, 37 million Americans, most of them working people and their little children, have no health insurance at all."
Some three years later, 40 million Americans are without health insurance. And the Administration's principal legacy in health-care policy has been to get out of the way while states and big corporations swiftly remake the system.
They are transforming it with stunning speed. It is a process that some experts in health policy credit the White House for accelerating, but that others say has kept health care prohibitively expensive for the uninsured and subordinated the quality of care to the drive for profits.
"By putting the health-care reform issue on the table, President Clinton catalyzed a lot of action in the private sector, the private marketplace," said Ronald M. Hollander, president of the Massachusetts Hospital Association. "It drove everybody to develop strategies for dealing with that change."
Other experts say Mr. Clinton's failure and subsequent retreat permanently set back efforts to expand the guarantee of basic medical care to everyone, not just the poor and the elderly already covered by Medicaid and Medicare.
"He destroyed the chances for a very significant and productive reform of the health-care system," said Prof. Alain C. Enthoven, a Stanford University economist and 1992 Clinton supporter who devised the concept at the core of the President's plan, called managed competition. "He blew a historic opportunity."
The President agreed in an interview this month that his policy reach had exceeded his political grasp.
"I overestimated the extent to which a person elected with a minority of the votes, in an environment that was complex, to say the least, could make -- could achieve in a sweeping overhaul of the health-care system," Mr. Clinton said.
His ambitious plan was stonewalled by Republicans, whom the White House had largely ignored during its long gestation, and pummeled by insurance and business lobbies who saw it as a threat. In the end, it was rejected by a public that came to see it as a bid to replace their family doctor with the Bureau of Motor Vehicles writ large.
Now, asked in an interview to describe his priorities for a second term, the President addresses health care in this way: "I think we need to look at what we can do to try to find ways for people to hold on to health insurance if they have sustained periods of unemployment, if they outrun their ability to maintain their old health insurance policy because their employer is not obligated to pay anymore."
Prominent by its absence was any hint that Mr. Clinton would again try any thorough reshaping of the system. Since the collapse of his 1993 proposal, the words "universal health care" have vanished from the President's vocabulary and dropped off his legislative agenda.
It is now the Republican Congress that is pressing for overhaul, at least of the Federal Medicaid and Medicare programs. And it is Mr. Clinton who has become, in some eyes, the chief defender of the status quo.
Bruce C. Vladeck, administrator of the Federal Health Care Financing Administration, which supervises the two programs, said the President was acting out of necessity. "To the extent that playing defense is an accomplishment," Mr. Vladeck said, "I think we've prevented destruction of the Medicaid program and significant damage to the Medicare program, with the President's veto of the Balanced Budget Act. That's a pretty significant accomplishment."
In presenting himself as the savior of those popular benefit programs, Mr. Clinton has done much to save his own political career as well. Opposing the Republicans' health-care proposals was one cause on which the White House and Democrats in Congress could agree. And it has become an important element in the revival of the party's popularity in the last year.
 
The Initiative
Bold Move to Assure Universal Coverage
What Mr. Clinton envisioned in his 1993 health-care plan was what Democratic leaders from Harry S. Truman had failed to produce: guaranteed, affordable health care for everyone, with benefits that would meet every basic medical need.
Mr. Clinton's thesis was simple: everyone should be required to join a health-care plan, and business should have to provide coverage for all workers. Health-care providers should be required to accept all comers. No patient would escape paying a share of the nation's medical bill. No doctor or insurer would be able to pass the health-care buck by leaving the sickest and poorest patients for someone else to treat. Competition among the newly equalized health-care providers would improve quality and hold down costs.
But what was simple in principle proved devilishly complex in the details. The flywheel of the Clinton plan was a scheme called managed competition, in which networks of doctors, hospitals and other health-care providers competed for patients. Among other concerns, critics worried that too few would serve rural states like Idaho or Montana to generate the competition needed to hold costs down.
So the plan proposed a National Health Board to oversee the operation of the health-care system and monitor the quality of care, as well as to set a national health budget to make sure costs stayed in line. To keep insurance both universal and affordable, it proposed to require employers to pick up 80 percent of the cost of any health-care plan their workers chose, an "employer mandate" that later became a significant issue in the debate.
And to keep everyone in the same system -- the key to controlling buck-passing and containing inflation -- the President proposed partially folding Medicaid and Medicare into the system while retaining their Federal subsidies.
Supporters called the proposal visionary. But some critics accused the White House of trying to nationalize health care and impose price controls on medical services.
As one, small businesses rebelled against being forced to buy health plans for workers, arguing that the cost would drive many into bankruptcy. The White House said it was open to other ways of paying for the plan, but it neither proposed nor endorsed any.
The insurance industry saw the plan as a competitive threat and set out to dispatch it quickly with a national advertising campaign designed to unsettle voters.
But it was the Republicans in Congress, denied any say in writing the plan and not seriously drawn into the process by the White House after its release, who may have dealt the heaviest blows. They dubbed the proposal "socialized medicine." They wielded Mr. Clinton's proposed employer mandate against him like a club, calling it proof of the President's liberal, big-Government agenda.
Congressional Democrats proved hapless. In the House, they fell to bickering over the final shape of universal health care and never managed to unite behind a single plan that could be passed. In the Senate, Republicans and Democrats could not even move a bill out of the Finance Committee.
By the time Congress went on its annual August recess in the summer of 1994, universal health care was effectively dead, except as an election issue that autumn.
The White House did not help its own cause. In truth, the President's actual proposal did confer much more power on the Government than the original fathers of managed care, like Mr. Enthoven, ever expected.
"Managed competition was meant to be a moderate, centrist approach, using market forces, minimal government controls, certainly no price controls," Mr. Enthoven said. "Clinton completely violated what he had told the American people. His plan amounted to a Government takeover of the health-care system. He talked centrist, and he tried to govern left."
Looking back, critics and admirers alike say, Mr. Clinton made a tactical error by placing his wife in charge of the task force that prepared his plan. That gave opponents the opportunity to charge that the nation's medical system was being upended by someone who was not even elected. Mrs. Clinton erred further by drafting much of the 1,700-page proposal in secret.
And her principal adviser, the private consultant and policy analyst Ira C. Magaziner, tried so hard to anticipate every possible consequence of change that the finished plan became a Rube Goldberg system beyond the comprehension of most people. Republicans created a flow chart of the proposal that resembled a street map of Istanbul, and the image of a hopelessly complicated bureaucracy designed by woolly headed academics stuck.
The President said he wanted a bipartisan bill. But by the time he was ready to strike a deal, in the summer of 1994, his plan had been so thoroughly savaged that he had little political leverage. In November, the midterm elections saw the Republican sweep of Congress, and the President's grand plan faded into history.
 
The Counterthrust
Republicans Try To Limit Federal Role
The rout of his own proposal left Mr. Clinton without a role in health policy. But as Republicans took control, he adopted a new stance: defender of the system he had vowed to change.
"I got the message of the 1994 election," Mr. Clinton told a White House audience the next summer. "And I won't let the Government mess with your Medicare."
The President left the field to the Republicans, whose idea of health-care overhaul was to reduce the Government's role as much as possible. They began by trying to wring billions of dollars out of the Medicare and Medicaid programs and promptly ran into the same political chain saw that had mowed down Mr. Clinton's plan. For the last two years, Mr. Clinton has positioned himself as a bulwark against a Congress that he says is bent on destroying medical care for poor people and the elderly.
Republicans in Congress wanted to transform Medicare gradually from a Government-run program into a voucher system controlled largely by its patients. Elderly people could remain in the program or enroll in private health plans for which the Government would pay a fixed monthly stipend. Those who wanted additional coverage would pay from their own pockets.
Republicans argued that this would inject competition into the health-care business, entice patients into cheaper, more efficient managed-care plans and drive down Medicare's cost by $168 billion over seven years, at last estimate. They also proposed to cap total annual Medicare spending for the first time.
The Republicans would have made even bigger changes for Medicaid, intending to reduce its expected growth by $85 billion over seven years. They proposed to do away with the Federal entitlement and give each state a lump sum to run its own health-care program for the poor.
Mr. Clinton's response was to denounce both proposals and then offer more cautious alternatives. His first budget for the 1996 fiscal year, issued in February 1995, did nothing to curb the cost of Medicare or Medicaid, both of which have been growing about 9 percent a year. His second budget, issued four months later, trimmed $124 billion from Medicare and some $54 billion from Medicaid, both over seven years.
Those figures were within shouting distance of the Republican proposals. But unlike the Republicans, the President would have achieved most of his savings the old-fashioned way, by trimming payments to health-care providers. His most daring proposal was to limit the annual growth in the average amount spent on a Medicaid recipient, a "per capita cap" rather than an overall limit on Medicaid spending.
Anything more, he said time and again, was radical Republican extremism.
"The status quo is not an option," Mr. Clinton told the White House Conference on Aging in May 1995, but "I will not support proposals to slash these programs."
Paradoxically, while the White House and Congress have radically different ideas about the Government's role, their notions about how to improve Medicare and Medicaid have often turned out to be quite similar.
Mr. Clinton and the Republican majority both wanted to create a more competitive market by moving beneficiaries into H.M.O.'s. Both envisioned substantial cuts in the growth of Medicare spending. Both wanted to give the states more freedom to run Medicaid programs -- and, Mr. Clinton, in fact, has given 13 states waivers to do so.
But there was a defining difference. Mr. Clinton would have made his changes within strict Federal guidelines, with ironclad guarantees of benefits for anyone who qualified. The Republicans would have left far more of the choice to states and to individuals.
"The ultimate question is whether health care is a social good or a market commodity," said John C. Rother, chief lobbyist at the American Association of Retired Persons. "If you believe health care is a commodity, like food or automobiles or clothes, then that leads you down one road. If it's a social good, like public education or clean air, you go down another."
While the deadlock between Republicans and Democrats has blocked any meaningful change in Government health programs, both parties have been rewarded with surges of public support -- the Republicans in 1994 and the Democrats this year.
 
The Future
Looking for Solutions For Troubles Now
The President gives little indication that he wants to grapple with health-care overhaul in a second term. To the contrary, he now seems dedicated to smoothing the ragged edges of the system the nation already has.
In the interview, Mr. Clinton said he hoped to find a way to keep Medicare's vast hospital insurance trust fund solvent for the next 10 years while a bipartisan commission devised a long-term solution for the program. The trust fund is projected to go bankrupt in 2001, leading most experts to agree that whoever is elected President this year will have to prop it up, and soon.
In the short run, Mr. Clinton said, he just wants to keep Republicans from making basic changes, like allowing beneficiaries to leave the program, that he believes would threaten the coverage of the elderly poor. In the early days, Mr. Clinton had declared it imperative that everyone have health insurance. Today, he speaks of the plight of the uninsured primarily as a warning of what the elderly stand to lose.
"My idea of changing Medicare is not to leave lower-middle-class seniors in the same position that lower-middle-class non-seniors are in in America," he said. "You know, the people that don't have health insurance in America are the non-senior, nonpoor, not-quite good middle-class people, by and large. These are the people without any health insurance. And what I didn't want to do was to see us make structural changes in the Medicare program which would recreate in the senior population the same problems we've got in the non-senior population."
The President said he hoped to sign some version of Senate legislation, sponsored by Senators Nancy Landon Kassebaum of Kansas and Edward M. Kennedy of Massachusetts, which would enable workers to take their health insurance with them when they change jobs. In a second term, he said, his next step would be to find some way to insure people who remain jobless for long stretches.
Whatever the President could do to give more people access to health care, the future of the system seems certain to be charted not by a National Health Board or a global health-care budget, but by the market.
Who will benefit and what the costs for individuals and the nation will be are still being sorted out.
With the rise of for-profit medicine, "many exciting innovations are occurring in medical practice, including more emphasis on preventive care," said Robert H. Miller, a health economist at the University of California in San Francisco. "But the current competition is substantially different from what was envisioned by the architects of managed competition."
Mr. Rother, of the American Association of Retired Persons, has a sarcastic name for what has emerged: "unmanaged competition."
"In a way," Mr. Rother said, "both parties sort of stubbed their toes on this. H.M.O.'s are taking over at a very rapid rate. It's slowed the growth of health-care costs for people under 65, but it's also caused a lot of dislocation and confusion."
On the one hand, health-care spending grew more slowly in 1994 than in any other year since 1960, and many employers say insurance premiums have leveled off or even declined. On the other hand, the President's campaign pledge to make health care "a right, not a privilege" has gone unmet, while the ranks of the uninsured have grown.
Small businesses and the self-employed have few protections that Mr. Clinton proposed in 1993, leaving them to wrestle unassisted with the cost of medical coverage.
Mr. Miller, who has studied changes in 15 communities across the country, said that "while the creation of health-care networks helps develop the potential for improving the health-care system, it does not necessarily lead to improvements" in the quality of care. So far, he said, health plans and health-care providers are competing on price more often than on quality.
Dr. Samuel O. Thier, chief executive of the Partners Health Care System in Boston, said the absence of a Federal policy had created a vacuum that had been filled by managed-care companies driven by profit.
That system, Dr. Thier said, "is not driven by any commitment to broadening access to care. There is little incentive for managed-care companies to serve the uninsured."
The public also appears apprehensive. In a New York Times poll conducted from June 20 to 23, nearly one-third of the 1,121 people surveyed -- 32 percent -- said the health-care system had gotten worse since Mr. Clinton became President. Only 12 percent said it had gotten better.
That may sound ominous for an incumbent seeking re-election. But two-thirds of respondents blamed the Republican Congress more than the President for the failure to enact universal health insurance.
In fact, Mr. Clinton may have emerged with a political windfall of sorts. Public criticism of the unsettling changes in health care -- changes very much akin to what his plan might have engendered -- is being borne by big insurance companies and big H.M.O.'s, not big Government.
"All the criticism being leveled against the market would have been leveled even more ferociously against the Government," Stuart H. Altman, a professor of national health policy at Brandeis University, said in a recent interview. "As it turned out, the best thing that ever happened to the Government, for this purpose, was not to have passed the Clinton bill."
Next: The environment

CORRECTION-DATE: August 1, 1996, Thursday

 CORRECTION:
An article on Tuesday about President Clinton's record on health care misstated the Senate's legislative record on the matter. The Senate Finance Committee did approve a bill in July 1994 that would have extended health insurance to 95 percent of the population by 2002, but the bill stalled in debate on the Senate floor and never came to a vote.

HEADLINE: CURRENCY MARKETS;
Dollar Falls as Traders See Less Prospect for a Rate Rise

BYLINE: By The Associated Press 

 BODY:
The dollar fell against most leading currencies yesterday after economic data dampened hopes for higher United States interest rates.
"We have been expecting a rate rise for three months," said Tom Benfer, director of foreign exchange at the Bank of Montreal branch in New York. "The prospects for no change in Fed policy is responsible for the dollar having a soft time today." 
 The dollar also came under pressure as Sweden cut a key interest rate, aiding the German mark.
In trading in New York late yesterday, the dollar stood at 107.91 Japanese yen, down from 108.14 yen on Monday. The dollar traded at 1.4766 marks, down from 1.4802. The British pound fell to $1.5565 from $1.5585.
The dollar gained in early New York trading, following the United States bond market higher, after the Labor Department reported that wages and salaries rose nine-tenths of a percent for the March to June quarter, seasonally adjusted. The report indicated that wage pressures were not quite as strong as expected.
But the dollar soon reversed course as the report, as well as data showing a drop of 5.3 percent in sales of new homes in June, suggested that the Federal Reserve would have less reason to raise short-term interest rates.
Many currency traders had been betting on an increase in rates, which typically bolsters the value of the dollar by making dollar-denominated deposits more attractive to foreign investors.
Still, traders will be looking for further clues to the direction of United States interest rates this week, in particular Friday's report on July employment.
The dollar also weakened as the mark gained against the Italian lira,
French franc and Swedish krona.
The mark gained after the Swedish bank reduced its repurchase rate by 15 basis points, or hundredths of a percentage point.
"Mark gains in Europe are helping to underscore the weak dollar tone," said Marc Chandler, a senior analyst at Deutsche Morgan Grenfell in New York. "It is unclear what it will take to turn around dollar psychology."
In other trading, the dollar fell to 1.2023 Swiss francs, from 1.2063 Swiss francs on Monday, and to 5.0080 French francs from 5.0210 francs. The dollar was unchanged at 1,526 Italian lire.

HEADLINE: CREDIT MARKETS;
U.S. Reports Push Bonds Up Sharply

BYLINE: By ROBERT HURTADO 

 BODY:
Treasury securities prices rose sharply yesterday, as Government reports on wages and housing eased investor concern that the current rate of growth would accelerate inflation and cause fixed-income investments to drop in value. In fact, bonds turned in their best performance in more than a week and nearly recouped all the Monday losses.
The 30-year benchmark issue rose 21/32 , to 878/32 ; the yield, which moves in the opposite direction of the price, fell to 7.03 percent, from 7.09 percent on Monday. 
 But traders know that with a large amount of economic data still to be released this week, the market is far from out of the woods.
Today's refunding announcement by the Treasury will give market participants an idea on the size of the Government's quarterly refunding next week; also set for release today is the July Chicago Purchasing Managers survey.
Those reports are to be followed tomorrow by the July National Association of Purchasing Management survey; initial weekly unemployment claims; auto sales; preliminary second-quarter gross domestic product, and June construction orders. And on Friday, the Government is set to release its employment report on the number of jobs added to the economy in July.
Yesterday, the Labor Department released its quarterly employment cost index, the measure of total compensation for employment, including pensions and health benefits. The index showed a gain of eight-tenths of 1 percent in the second quarter, at the low end of expectations.
Another Government report showed that sales of new homes slid 5.3 percent in June, reversing a 1.2 percent rise in May, which had been revised sharply downward itself from an original estimated gain of 7.5 percent. Weekly retail sales figures also showed enough softness to keep bond prices higher, despite a rise in the Conference Board's consumer confidence index to 107.2 in July from 100.1 in June.
Donald Fine, chief market analyst at Chase Asset Management, said, "A lower-than-expected reading on the employment cost index, due in part to revisions to back data, buoyed the bond market despite a jump in the consumer confidence index and a pace for new-home sales that is still strong by historical standards."
Concerning the employment cost index, Mr. Fine said that led by the wages and salaries component, the index "certainly has had less inflationary undertones than had been feared, but at the same time, its rising trend may prove to be a problem in the future."
The surge in consumer confidence in July, Mr. Fine said, was due to large gains in both the respondents' appraisals of their present situations as well as their expectations for the future. In both, he said, the employment picture was seen as bright.
As far as the June new-home sales, they dropped to an annual rate of 734,000 from a downwardly revised May level of 775,000. But these monthly levels are still rather healthy, Mr. Fine said, and had yet to conclusively demonstrate any impact from higher interest rates.
Larry M. Berman, senior fixed-income analyst with Technical Data in Boston, said that the employment cost index did not relieve any of the mystery surrounding what the Federal Reserve's Open Market Committee, which sets interest rate policy, might do at its Aug. 20th meeting.
"With the market now focused on key data releases later this week, we expect the 30-year bond to trade between a yield of 7.01 percent and 7 percent until we see nonfarm payroll data on Friday," he said.
While Alan Greenspan, the Fed chairman, has laid out in testimony before Congress the Fed's forecast of slowing economic growth and moderating inflation for the rest of 1996 and into 1997, there is a risk that the growth is stronger than the central bank anticipates. Peter J. Canelo, chief investment strategist at Dean Witter Reynolds, thinks Mr. Greenspan is right. "Even if the anticipated slowdown does not proceed as smoothly as the Fed might prefer," Mr. Canelo said, "we expect some moderation in real economic growth and inflation, which should enable bond yields to settle down in the coming months and help the stock market return to its highs."

HEADLINE: Anne Simon, 82, Who Wrote of Threats to the Sea

BYLINE: By WOLFGANG SAXON 

 BODY:
Anne W. Simon, an environmentalist whose books explored the perils to the world's seas and shorelines, particularly on her beloved Martha's Vineyard, died on Monday at her Manhattan home. She was 82 and also had a home in Chilmark, Mass.
At her death she was working on a book on the changing nature of Martha's Vineyard and what might be done to save the island where she had summered for many years. An article based on its first chapter was published in the July issue of the monthly Martha's Vineyard Magazine. 
 The article echoed her earlier book, "No Island Is an Island: The Ordeal of Martha's Vineyard" (1973). There she told the tale of how the Vineyard, after hundreds of years of slumber, surrendered to tract subdivisions, an explosion of second homes, sewage spillovers and traffic jams.
Another volume, "The Thin Edge: Coast and Man in Crisis" (1978), discussed the precarious state of dunes and beaches, wetlands and estuaries everywhere that economic development reaches them. "Neptune's Revenge: The Ocean of Tomorrow" (1984) was an unsparing critique of the environmental damage to oceans from oil spills, overfishing, the greenhouse effect and the dumping of sewage, toxins and radioactive waste.
Anne Wertheim was born in Cos Cob, Conn., and in 1935 graduated from Smith College, where she edited the campus newspaper. She earned a master's degree at the School of Social Work of Columbia University.
She was a writer at radio station WNYC, became a television critic for The Nation and subsequently wrote for McCall's, Good Housekeeping and other periodicals. Her first book was "Stepchild in the Family: A View of Children in Remarriage" (1964), a primer based on her own experience as a stepchild and as a stepparent.
She was married three times and had taken the surname of her last husband, Prof. Walter Werner, while keeping her pen name. He died in 1986. Her previous marriages, to Dr. Louis Langman and Robert Simon, ended in divorce.
She is survived by a son and three daughters from her first marriage: Thomas Langman of Washington, Betsy L. Schulberg of Westhampton Beach, L.I., Lynn L. Lilienthal of Reston, Va., and Deborah L. Lesser of Berkeley, Calif., and 11 grandchildren.

HEADLINE: Public Endorses Uplifting Movies, Dole Tells Hollywood

BYLINE: By ADAM NAGOURNEY 

DATELINE: LOS ANGELES, July 30

 BODY:
Returning to the place and theme that helped jump-start his Presidential campaign 14 months ago, Bob Dole today offered a revised view of Hollywood, asserting that the public rewards films that celebrate American values and rejects those that depict excessive sex and violence.
Mr. Dole, who a year ago scolded film makers for producing a "nightmare of depravity," said today he was returning to Hollywood to "praise what is best in your industry." He offered a list of commercially successful films that he asserted proved the public will patronize movies that "raise our vision of life instead of dragging us down." 
 "The American people are rejecting entertainment that insults our intelligence and offends our sense of decency," Mr. Dole said, speaking on the padded sound stage at 20th Century Fox, where "Alien 4" will begin shooting next month, and blocks away from the faux New York backdrop of grafitti-marked buildings that is a set for the television series "N.Y.P.D. Blue."
"We are rejecting films that deride our country and our values," Mr. Dole said in his 20-minute speech, which his campaign had previewed as a major address. "As consumers with choices and as people with standards, the great majority of us are rejecting the very idea that art must offend and degrade, instead of uplift and inspire."
By way of evidence, Mr. Dole pointed to the commercial failure of the movie "Striptease," and the successes of "Forrest Gump," "Apollo 13," "Independence Day," "Braveheart" and "Babe." He was holding them up "in the hope that others will follow your example."
"Some in the industry may wish to produce movies that are degrading, debasing, tawdry, second-rate, antiheroic," he said. "I would ask them to spare us the market-made-me-do-it excuse. This is a free country. No one is forcing you to do anything."
In tone and substance, Mr. Dole's speech was markedly different from the one of a year ago. At that time, bowing to the strong urging of aides worried about his then-struggling nomination campaign, Mr. Dole upbraided studio executives and asserted that they shared responsibility for undermining American culture. Today, he spoke of what he said were the accomplishments of the film industry, and suggested that market forces should reinforce that trend.
Mr. Dole's aides said his remarks today were not intended to telegraph a new view about movies, or to suggest that the tenor of filmmaking had changed appreciably as a result of his original criticism. William J. Bennett, a former Education Secretary who is traveling with Mr. Dole and had urged him to deliver the speech, described it as a "nuanced" effort to make the same point that Mr. Dole had made a year ago, but approaching it from a different perspective.
Still, Mr. Dole's speech today reflects the fact that his political needs are markedly different now from what they were a year ago, when he was in a heated contest for the Republican Presidential nomination, and needed to court conservatives suspicious of his credentials on social issues. His position today is more complicated, as he tries to position himself in the political center in preparation for the fall campaign, while attempting to draw cultural distinctions between himself and President Clinton.
Mr. Dole is also being counseled by advisers like Mr. Bennett that the way to revive his anemic campaign is to stress cultural and social issues, rather than solely pursue the economic course that his other advisers have pushed. Mr. Bennett, riding from Hollywood to an Orange County enclave of Republicans where Mr. Dole gave a second, shorter address, compared the influence of the film industry today to the Roman Catholic Church in the Middle Ages. "I believe what is done here has more impact on the country overall then what is done in Washington: the country's sense of itself, its morale, and its direction," Mr. Bennett said.
If nothing else, today's speeches were intended to do for Mr. Dole what the one a year ago did: draw attention to his campaign. That speech, as Mr. Dole noted with some satisfaction today, "continued a national debate," as it caught the attention of columnists, editorialists and talk show hosts across the country.
Today, Mr. Dole, obviously eager to reignite that spark, spoke from Stage 15 on the 20th Century Fox lot, standing in front of a mural painting of the Hollywood Hills, with red and blue spotlights framing the stage on each side.
Mr. Dole went out of his way to say he was not urging film makers to produce only "feel-good movies," adding, "None of wants to live in a Muzak culture without variety, tension or depth."
He offered the movie "Schindler's List" as an example of a movie that was socially redeeming. Mr. Dole also said he thought violence was acceptable in some cases. The violence in "Braveheart," he said, was "true to the story -- some have been there -- a story of men fighting for freedom, not just brutality thrown in there for the shock and thrill of it."
But it did appear, as the day went on, that the candidate was having some second-thoughts on "Independence Day," a movie about an attempted takeover of the Earth by alien invaders that he saw with Mr. Bennett in preparation for his speech and that he has plugged as an example of a film that celebrated American values. Mr. Dole said that as much as he enjoyed the movie, which depicts the violent destruction of major American cities by the alien spaceships, he might not suggest it for a child under 13 or 14.
The only movie Mr. Dole singled out for criticism was "Striptease," which Mr. Dole's aides said he did not see, saying its commercial failure proved his point. Patronage outside movie theaters, Mr. Dole said, showed that "by a landslide margin, Americans are choosing the good over the grotesque; excellence over exploitation; quiet virtue over gratuitous violence, and character over pointless cruelty."
Mr. Dole's point about the success of such movies was correct, though not complete. Some of the most successful films in recent years have been action and violence films like "Pulp Fiction," and the "Lethal Weapon" and "Die Hard" series.
Mr. Dole's remarks received restrained applause from his audience. Jack Valenti, a onetime aide to President Lyndon B. Johnson who as president of the Motion Picture Association of America helped devise the movie rating system 22 years ago, said he had no quarrels with Mr. Dole's remarks, but added:
"To many Congressmen and Senators right now, there's a ceaseless antagonism toward Hollywood because politically, it is high-reward and low-risk," Mr. Valenti said after hearing Mr. Dole speak. "So when you can't do anything about poverty or the budget deficit, and you can't deal with Bosnia or the possibility of nuclear explosions in Russia, what do you do? You bash Hollywood and get on the front pages."
 
In His Own Words
 
BOB DOLE
 
Speaking yesterday at 20th Century Fox studios in Los Angeles:
 
"Your freedom is yours to use as you wish, so let's dispense with a familiar myth. It's the usual response offered in defense of gratuitous violence, casual sex and generally cheap behavior in some movies. I'm referring to that familiar line, 'We're only giving the public what it wants.'
 We've all heard that line.
 There are other lines I find more convincing, the lines outside America's theaters. It's clear that by far most of the public wants something better, and we know that Hollywood is capable of producing better. Now some in the industry may wish to produce movies that are degrading, debasing, tawdry, second rate, anti-heroic, but I would ask them to spare us the 'market made me do it' excuse. This is a free country. No one is forcing you to do anything.
 Besides, in Hollywood today, the big story is that respectability is good business. You can watch your ratings rise and your box-office receipts go up, and still look yourself in the mirror. You can put your money at risk, while still keeping you artistic integrity. If our ticket windows are a kind of cultural ballot box, then the results are in and we can call a winner. By a landslide, Americans are choosing the good over the grotesque, excellence over exploitation, quiet virtue over gratuitous violence, and character over pointless cruelty. . . .
 The values I have spoken about in this campaign, and which I will continue to speak about in confident and unapologetic ways, are not intolerant values. They're what make us kind and civil toward one another. They're what make tolerance possible in a country of many faiths, and unity possible in a country with one future. Far from being divisive, those principles are what keep this country together.
 So I would just leave you with a reminder that cynicism is contagious, but so, too, is decency. Anyone with influence over our culture has to choose one or the other. And maybe no one expressed that choice better than Clark Gable's character, Rhett Butler, in 'Gone With the Wind.' He says: 'What most people don't seem to realize is that there is just as much money to be made out of the wreckage of a civilization as from the upbuilding of one. I'm making my fortune out of the wreckage.' "

HEADLINE: Sales of New Homes Plunge, Suggesting Slower Economy

BYLINE: By Bloomberg Business News 

 BODY:
Sales of new homes fell in June, while consumer confidence surged in July, two reports showed yesterday.
The Commerce Department said that sales of new single-family homes fell 5.3 percent in June as higher mortgage rates hurt demand. 
 The report suggested that the economy might be slowing. Sales declined in two of the nation's four regions in June. Besides the 29.3 percent drop in the Midwest, they declined 4.8 percent in the South. Sales rose 26.6 percent in the Northeast and 1.4 percent in the West.
Sales of new homes in May were also revised downward. Sales rose just 1.2 percent that month, rather than the huge 7.5 percent jump reported previously.
The average home sales price in June rose to $163,300 from $162,700 in May, while the median sales price was up to $140,000 from $136,400.
 "The economy should slow down," said Brian Wesbury, chief economist at Griffin, Kubik, Stephens & Thompson, a Chicago-based investment firm. "The second quarter was a catch-up for the weak winter growth."
Still, consumer attitudes about the economy remain upbeat. The Conference Board said its index of consumer confidence unexpectedly advanced to 107.2 in July -- the highest level in more than six years.
"Consumers are clearly confident about current economic conditions and anticipate further economic growth in the months ahead," said Edgar Fiedler, a Conference Board vice president.
July's reading of 107.2, which follows a revised June reading of 100.1, was the highest since May 1990. About 5,000 households are surveyed on their attitudes toward business conditions, jobs and their paychecks. Consumer spending accounts for about two-thirds of economic activity.
Yesterday's survey mirrors a similar index issued July 19 by the University of Michigan. That survey, which some analysts consider less volatile than the Conference Board report, registered a gain in consumer sentiment during July. The University of Michigan survey is released only to subscribers.

HEADLINE: COMPANY REPORTS;
Digital Posts $433 Million Deficit in Quarter

BYLINE: Reuters 

DATELINE: BOSTON, July 30

 BODY:
The Digital Equipment Corporation posted a $433 million deficit in its fiscal fourth quarter today after taking a $492 million charge to pay for 7,000 job cuts and plant closings.
Digital, the nation's third-largest computer maker, warned analysts earlier this month that it would take a restructuring charge to cover an 11 percent cut in its 60,900-member work force and close 3.5 million square feet of space at manufacturing plants and distribution sites. 
 Without the charge, income at the struggling computer maker, which is based in Maynard, Mass., fell to $59 million, or 33 cents a share, from $160 million, or $1.01 a share.
Digital's chief executive, Robert Palmer, said that the fourth-quarter loss was only a temporary setback to the company's year-and-a-half recovery after years of multibillion-dollar losses.
"In many respects, our turnaround suffered a slight setback in the quarter," Mr. Palmer said. "I'm confident that Digital will overcome today's challenges and achieve a level of growth that is comparable with industry leaders. I expect a more stronger and profitable year in fiscal '97."
Seventy-five percent of the $492 million charge went to cover the costs of cutting 7,000 jobs across across all of Digital's businesses, Mr. Palmer said. The company cut 1,800 jobs in the fourth quarter to end the year with 59,100, its smallest work force in 15 years, he said.
Mr. Palmer said that Digital had seen strong growth in its Alpha systems work station business, with Alpha revenue up 34 percent from a year ago, driven by demand for its new midrange server, called the 4100 Alpha Server, and the high-end 8000 Alpha Server. Servers are computers that link PC's in a network.
But the personal computer business continued to struggle, with revenue at the unit falling 3 percent in the fourth quarter from the comparable period a year earlier.
Mr. Palmer squelched speculation that the company would quit the PC business, saying that it was important for Digital to provide a wide range of products to support its growing work station business.
Mr. Palmer, who took over the PC business earlier this month, backed away from an earlier suggestion that he would soon name a new chief to the division.
Enrico Pesatori, who joined Digital to head the PC division and became Mr. Palmer's apparent successor, resigned July 1 after the division's earnings were hurt by rising inventories.
"I'm not actively looking for a replacement for Enrico at this point," Mr. Palmer said.
Digital said that as it had previously indicated, revenue in some European markets was down in part as a result of an economic slowdown and currency fluctuations. The company said it did well in other markets like Japan, Britain, Italy and Latin America.
Digital has moved to strengthen its management and expand its direct sales force in Europe.
Digital also announced today that it would buy back 10 million of its more than 155.5 million outstanding shares.
"The buyback is obviously going to impact the stock on a positive note," a trader said. "But if the buyback wasn't announced, you wouldn't be doing cart wheels over this number."
Digital shares were up 62.5 cents, to $36.125, on the New York Stock Exchange yesterday after rising as high as $37.

HEADLINE: Will a Tax Cut Help Dole?

BYLINE: By Herbert Stein; Herbert Stein, a senior fellow at the American Enterprise Institute, was chairman of the President's Council of Economic Advisers under Richard Nixon and Gerald Ford.

DATELINE: WASHINGTON

 BODY:
So here's Bob Dole, trying to decide what to do about taxes. All of his associates, many of whom want him to win so they can be big shots in a Dole administration, want him to come out for a big tax cut. They say that he's so far behind that it is the only thing that will save him.
To show him that's not only good politics but also good economics, his advisers trot out a bunch of economists, some of them Nobel Prize winners. They say that if we cut taxes, growth will speed up, we won't lose as much revenue as you might think, and everyone will be happy. 
 But there's another bunch of economists who say that cutting taxes will increase the deficit so much that the growth rate will slow down and everyone will be worse off. Probably some of them won Nobel Prizes, too. And there are other economists who say: Nobody knows, it could go either way, it all depends on the nature of the tax cut, among other things.
So what is Bob Dole to do? He can say to himself that even if cutting taxes is a bad idea, it's better for the country that he be elected than that Bill Clinton be re-elected. This is typical Presidential logic -- the need to do something not quite right in order to keep the opposition from getting elected and doing something worse. Anyway, Mr. Dole can think that if he gets elected and finds out a tax cut is a bad idea, he won't have to do it. That has happened to Presidents before.
But will it help him to get elected if he comes out for the big tax cut? I keep thinking about Harry Truman. In 1948, after the Republican Congress passed a tax cut over Truman's third veto, he ran against it and won an election he wasn't supposed to win.
Of course, Truman ran against the Republicans for passing what he called a "rich man's tax cut." Can Mr. Dole propose a tax cut that Democrats won't be able to call a rich man's tax cut? If he makes the 15 percent across-the-board tax cut that some of his advisers are talking about, that would be a much bigger cut for Steve Forbes's than for the average American. Steve Forbes and most of his friends will probably vote for Mr. Dole anyway.
And what about the capital-gains tax cut that all Mr. Dole's advisers want. Jack Kemp says that would mainly help low- and middle-income people. But does anyone other than Jack Kemp believe that?
Or Mr. Dole could propose a tax cut that would give every man, woman and child $200. That wouldn't be a rich man's tax cut. But it's not a pro-growth cut, and thus Mr. Dole's friends wouldn't like it. Is there any way to propose a tax cut that Mr. Clinton can't trump with a cut of his own that is more for the working man and less for the Republican fat cats?
Anyway, the public has probably gotten pretty cynical about campaign promises on taxes. George Bush said "Read my lips, no new taxes," and then he raised taxes anyway. Bill Clinton promised to cut taxes for middle America, and then he raised taxes. Ronald Reagan did fulfill his 1980 campaign promise to cut taxes, but he then raised taxes almost every year after that.
Of course, Mr. Dole could say that he would balance the tax cut with spending cuts. But he would have to say something about defense, Medicare and Social Security. His belief in a strong defense is a main point in his campaign. And can you imagine him selling a program that would cut Steve Forbes' taxes and slash Medicare and Social Security benefits?
The voters know that his advisers are urging Mr. Dole to recommend a big tax cut because they are desperate about the election, not because it represents his policy and principles. Can Mr. Dole afford to be seen doing that? The biggest thing he has going for him against Mr. Clinton is that voters think he sticks by his beliefs, that he tries to do the right thing even though it's hard. Should he sacrifice that public estimation of him now?
Mr. Dole's political mentor, and mine, Richard Nixon, used to say: "Honesty may not be the best policy, but it is worth trying once in a while." Maybe this is the time to try it. Mr. Dole could say something like this:
"America is not suffering from too much taxation. The greatest problem is the huge deficits that are going to appear in the next generation if we don't do something about them now. We must slow down the growth of spending for Medicare and Social Security. And we cannot afford tax cuts, not even those proposed by this Congress. I know that some economists have said that a tax cut will not increase the deficit very much. But our long-range economic and fiscal situation is too precarious to permit us to rely on such comforting estimates. It isn't good enough just to balance the budget by 2002. The next generation of workers is going to bear the costs of Medicare and Social Security for the baby boomers. We should be running a surplus now to reduce the debt we leave them to carry."
Maybe that won't win the election for Bob Dole. But it might help to awaken America to the economic dangers facing it and make it easier for a future President, whoever it may be, to act responsibly. And if Mr. Dole is not that President, it would let him retire as a man who always did what he thought was best for the country.
It's a tough choice.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

TYPE: Op-Ed

HEADLINE: Battles on Conservation Are Reaping Dividends

SERIES: THE CLINTON RECORD -- Fourth of seven articles: The Environment

BYLINE: By JOHN H. CUSHMAN Jr. and TIMOTHY EGAN 

DATELINE: KELLOGG, Idaho

 BODY:
A rust-colored river runs through the valley where Barbara Miller is trying to rear her four children, in a town of brittle-boned homes holding off age and gravity. Neighborly howdies blend with nervous talk about the poison that keeps showing up in the blood of local children.
The Silver Valley, a basin in the Idaho mountains that once produced most of the nation's silver and much of its lead, has long been one of the most polluted places in the United States. Things are gradually getting better, Mrs. Miller said, in part because the Clinton Administration has speeded the cleanup at Superfund toxic waste sites like the one in her neighborhood where mine refuse had smothered the land in toxic filth. 
 But Mrs. Miller and her neighbors are also struggling to live with another result of President Clinton's environmental policy. Liberated from environmental restrictions thanks to a bill Mr. Clinton reluctantly signed last summer, loggers have been clearing the national forest just upstream. Early this year when the rains came, the naked hillsides could not restrain the runoff, sending a flood coursing through the valley and shifting more than one million pounds of lead downstream where the toxic sediment settled in people's yards.
"We've seen a lot of progress in this valley," said Mrs. Miller, whose yard was dug up to remove lead endangering her children. "But now, with this new logging, a lot of people are upset."
If Mrs. Miller and her neighbors feel whipsawed by Federal environmental policies, their experience may also be emblematic of the Clinton Administration's entire record on the environment, capturing in one spot some of the most complex issues of natural resources and public health that define environmentalism.
Torn between economic forces and ecological imperatives, the White House has sought to satisfy entrenched advocates on both sides of divisive policy struggles, only to find itself drawn into confrontation almost every time it sought consensus.
Measured by a legislative yardstick, the Administration's environmental record would rise but a few inches. There were hardly any landmark environmental laws signed in the Rose Garden, and certainly nothing to rival Theodore Roosevelt's preservation of wild spaces, or Richard M. Nixon's endorsement of the modern environmental legal structure or Jimmy Carter's protection of big parts of Alaska from development.
Rather, the Administration's record is best defined by how it performed in a colossal collision with Congress midway through its first four years. When Republicans won control in 1994, they aggressively pressed forward with an antiregulatory agenda that environmentalists saw as threatening to dismantle a whole generation of rules that not only preserved open spaces and wilderness areas, but also put protection of the public's health and safety above other considerations, including economic ones.
An intense battle over how to reshape environmental regulations ensued, with the Administration presenting its own, moderate ideas but essentially playing a defensive game and labeling the Republicans as tools of industrial special interests. The fight made every issue, including cleaning up toxic wastes or chopping down ancient forests, a matter of pitched partisan politics.
And now, it appears that the payoff is coming, not only in strong support in polls but also in legislative accomplishments. Later this week, President Clinton will sign a new pesticides bill that strongly resembles what he proposed three years ago, imposing strict new health protections in place of limited, outmoded and scientifically flawed ones and paying special attention to protection for infants and children. The legislation was passed with stunning swiftness by unanimous voice votes in a House and Senate that seemed to have learned the political power of mainstream environmentalism.
Polling by The New York Times in June showed that by a margin of nearly 2 to 1, people trusted Mr. Clinton more than the Congress to protect the environment. But even as Mr. Clinton won praise for acting as a shield against Republican efforts to roll back environmental protections, he is often criticized as someone who has fallen short on the expectations.
 
The First Steps
Economy and Ecology Breed an Ambivalence
The newly formed Clinton Administration was a study in environmental ambivalence. As a candidate, Mr. Clinton was a self-styled new Democrat with an innate knack for compromise and a commitment to the economy. As Governor of Arkansas, that had sometimes meant bowing to the needs of industry when it came to environmental protection.
But in Al Gore, Bill Clinton had chosen a Vice-Presidential nominee who had written a best seller that called "rescuing the environment the central organizing principle for civilization."
The same ambivalence permeated the Cabinet and the White House staff, where there was a constant tension between people who figuratively wore green armbands and those who preferred green eyeshades. There were strong environmentalists and Gore loyalists, but also noncommittal number-crunchers and a cadre of political movers and shifters.
"We spent '93 and '94 working on how to make the environment an integral part of our long-term economic strategy, but still keep high standards," the President recalled in an interview. "I do not believe the public supports a Government walking away from the environment. I just don't believe they do."
The President started his term with some daring environmental initiatives, even in his economic program. Taking his advice from Interior Secretary Bruce Babbitt, an ardent environmentalist from Arizona, he promised to break a century-old habit of subsidized logging, mining and grazing on public lands.
But there were immediate signs that when it came to a fight with Congress -- even one still controlled by Democrats -- the White House was not as determined as the environmentalists had imagined. The Administration, in its first budget battle with Congress, quickly retreated from initiatives to increase grazing fees on public lands and impose an energy tax. Especially in the Senate, lawmakers from energy and ranching states controlled the show, and Mr. Babbitt became something of a pariah to them.
Another sign came when Congress roughed up the Administration on a proposal to elevate the Environmental Protection Agency, headed by a determined Gore loyalist, Carol M. Browner, to Cabinet status. It was mainly a symbolic move that would have done little beyond putting new brass plaques on some bureaucrats' doors. But it got nowhere, largely because conservatives -- including Democrats -- insisted on tacking on requirements that environmental rules be subject to economic tests to see if they were worth the cost.
When asked to defend the Administration's record, Vice President Gore cited a long list of administrative actions, many of which received relatively little public attention, including policies on ocean dumping, recycling paper, managing fisheries and the like.
Faced with a Congress that proved unable to agree on laws that govern such major issues as wetlands, endangered species, and cleaning up toxic dumps, the Administration took major steps to smooth the process of getting permits for development and completed cleanups at hundreds of Superfund sites. But that is hardly the stuff of history books.
The 103d Congress, still controlled by Mr. Clinton's own party, finally approved, after a decade of debate, a huge expansion of the National Park system in California's deserts. But Congress was generally hostile to green causes, and as the 1994 election approached environmental activists glumly joked that the Mojave Desert preserve was set aside just to give them a bigger wilderness to wander in.
 
The Confrontation
Republican Triumph Spurs the Defense
In the end, the Administration would make environmentalism a pillar of its program, but that was only after Congress slipped into the hands of Republicans who mounted a broad legislative offensive to roll back environmental regulations and slash the budgets of the agencies enforcing them. The President responded with vetoes and threats of vetoes, and his popularity surged.
Playing defense, supporters and critics alike agreed, the President found his footing.
"What's the old principle from physics?" Mr. Gore mused when asked how the midterm election had shaped the Administration's own environmental stance. "A force creates a counterforce, an equal and opposite reaction."
But for that reaction to take hold in the Clinton White House first required that the President himself realize that wrapping himself in green would pay off politically.
It happened at about the time that Dick Morris, the pollster and political confidant, moved into the White House, bringing fresh surveys showing that even among antiregulatory Republican conservatives in Virginia -- the heart of opposition territory -- there was a strongly held belief that environmental regulations should be stronger, not weaker.
It appeared that the Republicans in Congress were making a huge political mistake.
Under its new Republican majority, the House in 1995 quickly passed a bill to overhaul the making of regulatory decisions on cleaning air, water and waste sites and protecting species and the habitats where they live. Part of the Republicans' Contract With America, the bill required elaborate cost-benefit studies of any new rules and opened significant new legal avenues for industry to appeal rules they thought were too burdensome.
Still shocked by an election that had created the first Republican Congress in a generation, the Administration reacted cautiously, adopting a series of administrative changes and pilot programs that it promised would give businesses more flexibility while keeping pollution control standards in place.
The House also moved swiftly with revisions to the Clean Water Act to eliminate Federal jurisdiction over most wetlands and ease on controls over industrial pollutants. Republican leaders also pushed a bill that would have greatly weakened the Endangered Species Act, and another to repeal many retroactive liability provisions of the Superfund law, which force polluters to pay for environmental damage from dumping toxic wastes.
Even though environmentalists were howling, both the regulatory bill and the water bill passed the House without anyone at the White House even murmuring the word "veto."
But the mood grew increasingly combative. Sensing the political advantage of confrontation, the President finally dived into the fight.
Ms. Browner said she knew things had changed in May 1995, when George Stephanopoulos, a major political adviser to Mr. Clinton, telephoned the President as they drove to a news conference where Mr. Clinton was about to denounce the water bill and, for the first time, pledge to veto legislation rolling back an environmental protection.
"Be sure he calls it the Dirty Water Act," Mr. Stephanopoulos said to Ms. Browner, signaling that the environmental issue had grabbed the attention of top political advisers. The President did so, and the bill stalled in the Senate.
The Administration also deftly exploited the close involvement of business lobbyists, who were tilting their campaign contributions heavily toward Republicans, in drafting some of the new legislation. That raised the hackles of many voters, polls showed.
"We've obviously stopped the wave of repealers and attacks on environmental laws," Mr. Babbitt, the Interior Secretary, said. "But the turn to a positive agenda is just barely in the making. The adversarial approach to legislation is what this town is all about. It is a town full of lobbyists and lawyers on all sides, who are obsessively inward-looking, who do not have a feel for how these issues work on the ground. The question for post-'96 is, are we fated to repeat this endless cycle, or do we have an opportunity to find, and build and cement a consensus that will hold?"
 
The Strategy
Search for Consensus Is the Main Goal
Indeed, a hallmark of the Administration's environmental policy is the premise that consensus can work, letting everybody win.
That premise has been tested in one setting after another, with varying results. It was the basis for the Administration's approach to the Endangered Species Act, a law which has often led to court battles between antagonists but which the Administration used instead to negotiate broad compromises on preserving habitat. Similarly, the Administration tried again and again to broker what it termed "common sense" agreements on controlling industrial pollution.
Confronting a groundswell of opposition to the species law among property owners trapped in litigation and delay, the Administration set out to win over the logging companies, developers, and ranchers who were its biggest critics. In Georgia came a deal to protect the red-cockaded woodpecker while allowing logging to continue in parts of the bird's habitat. In Southern California, a similar deal was struck with housing developers to protect the gnatcatcher, a songbird.
In both cases, not just a single species but an entire ecosystem could come under protection, potentially helping hundreds of plants and animals avoid eventual demise. And landowners who agreed to sign contracts for proper stewardship were promised what Mr. Babbitt called "no surprises." Once a habitat was protected, there would not be subsequent demands for further restrictions on how the land was used.
Administration officials believe that the changes are permanent.
"We have reinvented the way habitat protection is done in this country, and I don't think we are ever going to go back," said George Frampton, the Assistant Secretary of Interior for Fish and Wildlife who was the head of the Wilderness Society before joining the Administration.
Ms. Browner said her agency had brought about permanent change.
"I honestly believe that in 10 years, when you look at the regulatory portion of the agency's work that how those standards are set will be place by place, sector by sector," Ms. Browner said. "That is fundamentally different than doing it the pollutant-by-pollutant, crisis-by-crisis way of the past."
It has not been easy, especially amid the anti-bureaucracy hammering from Capitol Hill, to turn her slogan of "common sense" into the agency's standard operating procedure.
The difficulty was captured at a meeting of Government officials, environmentalists and industry representatives in Washington last October. The advisory committee had convened at the request of auto makers to endorse a new, flexible approach that the Administration was proposing to meet the air pollution permit requirements of the Clean Air Act, which had passed with bipartisan support in 1990 and were signed by President George Bush.
While few citizens were even aware of the new controls put on industry by Title V of that law, everybody in the room knew that billions of dollars were potentially at stake in how the regulations to carry out the law were written and that industry lobbyists were working hard on Capitol Hill to have that part of the law rewritten..
So Ms. Browner made clear to the group that she wanted a quid pro quo before the committee endorsed her new policy and put the stamp of consensus on it. She wanted corporate lobbyists to back off from their efforts to roll back the regulations on Capitol Hill.
"I think I asked a perfectly legitimate question," Ms. Browner said later. "The business people were sitting in the room, but man, they were getting a better deal somewhere else."
In the end, the question was left hanging that day like a thin summer smog. And the essential issue -- how hard the the industry will lobby for fundamental change in environmental regulation -- will help determine what lies ahead.
But especially after Senate Democrats successfully filibustered Bob Dole's regulatory overhaul, a major part of the Contract With America, industry appeared to be once again joining the search for a middle road on environmental regulation.
This year, when the President's Council on Sustainable Development issued recommendations on a new framework for environmental regulation, it was endorsed by companies like Dow Chemical as well as by environmental groups like the Sierra Club.
"I truly believe," said the head of the White House Council on Environmental Quality, Katie McGinty, "that the President will go down in history for having put in place a new generation of environmentalism, based on cooperation not confrontation: defining and securing the common ground, defining the common interest, not the special interest."
Even Republican environmental leaders acknowledge that this might occur. William Ruckleshaus, who headed the E.P.A. when it was first created under Mr. Nixon and again after it was shaken by scandal in the Reagan Administration, said he hoped the political opportunity to overhaul the system would arise "sometime after the next Presidential election."
"Windows of rationality, where we experience a calmer atmosphere more conducive to constructive change, open infrequently, and usually only for a short period of time," Mr. Ruckleshaus said.
 
The Continuing War
Logging Compromise Kindles Criticism
In the woods, consensus has been as elusive as Sasquatch.
Just ask Tom Vuyovich, as he watches the logging trucks rumble day and night past the little motel he owns in the Oregon Cascades. All around him, big trees are falling in the Willamette National Forest. Detroit Lake is silted in by the inevitable erosion from heavy logging. Protesters trying to block further cutting are arrested daily.
"We're right in the middle of a giant war, and they're logging everything," Mr. Vuyovich said. "Clinton said he was going to end this. So why are they taking 500-year-old trees out and scaring away all the tourists?"
As a candidate, Mr. Clinton promised to end the epic conflict over the last of the nation's big trees. Barely three months into his Presidency, he tried to fulfill that promise by convening what he called a forest summit, an extraordinary session in Portland, Ore., attended by environmentalists, timber industry executives, workers and Government officials, including the President, nearly a third of his Cabinet and low-ranking Forest Service rangers.
What came out of the summit was a plan largely to end logging on the last six million or so acres of ancient forests on public land, most of it in the Pacific Northwest. And there was a pledge to spend $1.2 billion to retrain workers and restore land torn up by excessive logging.
It seemed, at first, to be a major triumph. Protests disappeared. Lawsuits were settled. Ex-loggers went to school, and unemployment in the timber communities actually fell to record low levels.
But the industry continued to complain about how much timber they were allowed to have, and in mid-1995, Congress responded with what came to be called the salvage logging rider. This provision was tacked onto an unrelated spending bill. Described by its sponsors as a measure directed at culling dead and dying trees from ailing forests, it also opened the way for cutting many healthy stands as well, bypassing the protection of environmental laws.
Mr. Clinton vetoed it. Then, stunning some top environmental aides, he reversed course and signed the measure. A Federal court then gave the broadest possible interpretation to the bill, and throughout the West, even in the very areas that Mr. Clinton had vowed to protect, logging picked up speed.
"That was, indeed, our biggest mistake," Mr. Gore said, "and it never would have happened except for a miscalculation on our part about the magnitude of what was wrong with the provision. We were genuinely surprised, indeed shocked, by the court decision, which vastly expanded the scope of something we thought could be muted by administrative action. We knew it was not good policy. But it was one of those examples of one thing imbedded in a huge, overall measure that had to pass."
Critics call that a lame excuse.
"Because of that rider, I give this President a 'D' on forests," said Jim Jontz, a former Democratic member of Congress now working on environmental issues.
The Sierra Club protested in a park across the street from the White House with a sarcastic 21-chain-saw salute. David Werntz of the Northwest Ecosystem Alliance summed up the feeling recently in a single word: "Betrayal!"
But sound bites and photo opportunities are not the real stuff of environmental pollution. Natural resources and the public's health are, and they are harder to measure. Unlike an economic record, which can be measured in the quarterly rate of employment, gross national product, and inflation, an environmental record is written over long years and in one ordinary place at a time.
What ordinary people want in the Silver Valley, Mrs. Miller said, is a river that is no longer dead, a river that does not pose a health risk to children, a river without the word "Superfund" attached to it.
"It's in our back yard now," Mrs. Miller said. "But nobody is immune when you're talking about things that affect how we all live."

HEADLINE: Workers' Pay Rises Again, But Benefits Lag Behind

BYLINE: By LOUIS UCHITELLE 

 BODY:
In the clearest signal yet that wages and salaries are moving up, the Labor Department announced yesterday that its broadest measure of what American workers receive in their paychecks rose by nine-tenths of 1 percent in the second quarter. That was the fourth consecutive quarterly increase greater than the inflation rate.
The employment cost index, which has drawn little attention until recently, is caught up in both the election campaign and monetary policy. The Clinton Administration cited it as fresh evidence that 20 years of wage stagnation was finally ending -- a contention many economists dispute. And Alan Greenspan, the Federal Reserve chairman, singled out the index in recent testimony as a key statistic in the Fed's deliberations over interest rates. 
 Actually, the index sent out two messages pleasing to both Washington and the markets. While paychecks are finally allowing workers to gain some ground in living standards, the overall increase in what employers pay for labor has remained rather mild. As a result, the stock and bond markets concluded yesterday that employment costs alone were not likely to spur the Fed to raise interest rates at its August meeting to head off inflation.
The reason is that while wages and salaries have risen quite noticeably, going up by 3.2 percent for all workers for the 12-month period that ended on June 30, benefit payments have lagged behind. They rose by seven-tenths of 1 percent for the quarter and only 1.8 percent for the 12 months, mostly because company-paid health insurance premiums are barely increasing.
When wages and benefits are combined, representing the total labor cost for employers, the rise in the overall employment cost index was eight-tenths of 1 percent for the second quarter and 2.9 percent for the year. That was just a hair above the inflation rate of 2.8 percent for those 12 months.
"The overall index came in at slightly less than the markets had expected, and traders are betting, for that reason, that the Fed won't raise rates next month," said Stephen S. Roach, chief economist at Morgan Stanley.
Long-term interest rates fell in trading yesterday, dampened also by a Commerce Department report that sales of new single-family homes fell in June, for the second consecutive month. On the New York Stock Exchange, the Dow Jones industrial average, which often responds well to reports of moderate or even weakening economic growth, rose by 47.34 points yesterday, to 5,481.93. The only strongly positive statistic was a report from the Conference Board, a business organization, that consumer confidence had risen in July.
The Clinton Administration, meanwhile, cited the wage component of the employment cost index as fresh evidence that most Americans were finally getting decent raises after years in which wages and bonuses rose by less than the inflation rate.
"We have turned the corner," said Joseph E. Stiglitz, chairman of President Clinton's Council of Economic Advisers, offering a Democratic election campaign theme that is challenged by many other economists. Mr. Stiglitz credits Administration policies, particularly a reduction in the budget deficit, for generating enough economic growth to make wages rise steadily since the fall of 1993. But other economists say the growth is simply a natural part of the recovery from the 1990-91 recession. "The Administration could not ask for more from this economy than it is already getting," said Edward Yardeni, chief economist at Deutsche Morgan Grenfell/C. J. Lawrence Inc.
A similar, even stronger spurt in wages in the 1980's lasted three years and then disappeared as the economy weakened and the unemployment rate, which had fallen below 6 percent -- as it has today -- rose again. "This spurt in wages may turn out to be similarly brief," said Lawrence Mishel, chief economist at the Economic Policy Institute. Some economists, including Mr. Greenspan, say the economy might begin to weaken by the election.
Even without a slowdown, the Administration and many economists argue that American workers are improving their productivity sufficiently to pay for the wage increases they have received so far. By producing more goods and services to sell, they have taken the pressure off their employers to raise prices. Labor costs, both wages and benefits, would have to rise at an annual rate of at least 3.5 percent before pressure develops to increase prices by more than they are now rising, Mr. Stiglitz said. And Government data as well as anecdotal evidence suggest that while wages are rising, the increases are relatively mild.
The International Association of Machinists, for example, said that wage increases for its 500,000 members are rising by 3 percent a year in contracts negotiated over the last 18 months with airlines and aircraft manufacturers. Before that, the rise had been only 2.5 percent. But much of this increase, union officials say, has come in the form of bonuses and other one-time payments that could disappear in weaker economic times.
The Labor Department's Bureau of Labor Statistics reports that the biggest increase over the last year, 4.3 percent on average, has gone to salespeople: car salesmen, real estate and stock brokers, insurance agents, retail clerks, restaurant workers and the like. But part of the improvement has come in higher commissions and tips that would disappear if the economy weakened.
Shortages of skilled workers are also pushing up wages. That is happening at the Principal Financial Group in Des Moines, which employs 14,000 people in the marketing of insurance and retirement funds. "We have run into significant shortages of computer analysts and programmers," said David J. Drury, Principal's chairman, "and we are adjusting salary scales for this small group, but not by a huge amount, maybe 3 to 5 percent."
