HEADLINE: Buchanan to Push for Trade and Tax Planks

BYLINE: By JAMES BENNET 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, July 30

 BODY:
Trying to reclaim the initiative on issues central to his faded Presidential campaign, Patrick J. Buchanan plans to make an announcement on Wednesday revealing planks on taxes and trade that he wants included in the Republican platform.
Mr. Buchanan has been expected to play a leading role in any fight over abortion rights at the party's convention, which begins on Aug. 12 in San Diego. But his proposed economic planks show that he hopes to have far broader influence and push Bob Dole, the party's expected Presidential nominee, to embrace a flat tax on income, seek to end inheritance taxes on "family farms" and use "every trade weapon at our command to defend the economic interests of the United States." 
 Mr. Dole has said he plans to introduce his own economic plan, which will include tax cuts.
Judging from an advance copy of Mr. Buchanan's proposed planks, which was provided by a senior aide to his campaign, the ideas and language may prove a bit too provocative for the Republicans.
"The amoral transnational corporations that dominate foreign trade have adopted a profit-uber-alles mind-set," it declares in part. "Like the denizens of Jurassic Park, they exhibit only appetite. They have no allegiance to any country; they exhibit no loyalty even to their own workers."
The senior aide to Mr. Buchanan, speaking on the condition of not being named, said the campaign was working with sympathetic delegates on the Republican platform committee and hoped to see the language added to the platform when the committee met in San Diego.
Failing that, Mr. Buchanan would have to win the support of several state delegations to propose the planks on the convention floor.
"Theoretically, we could do something on the floor," the aide said. But "that's a major, major enterprise."
So far, the Dole campaign has shown little interest in accommodating Mr. Buchanan, who amassed the second-largest number of delegates in the primaries but has withheld an endorsement of Mr. Dole. Mr. Buchanan has been denied the opportunity to make a speech before the convention, and on Monday, he rejected a chance to appear for 15 seconds in a videotaped presentation.
While he regularly declared his opposition to abortion in his campaign speeches, Mr. Buchanan tended to dwell more on his economic ideas. Some of Mr. Buchanan's proposals, including one that Republicans seek the repeal of the 1990 and 1993 tax increases, are similar to those Mr. Dole's advisers say he is already considering.
But Mr. Buchanan also calls for a flat tax with "generous exemptions for children and deductions only for charitable giving and mortgage interest on the family home." He would also eliminate corporate taxes on "small businesses."
He says he would pay for his tax cuts partly with taxes on goods from "nations such as China and Japan that run chronic trade surpluses at our expense, through unfair trade practices."
Mr. Buchanan's proposals on trade are at odds with longstanding Republican policy. The 1992 Republican platform endorsed international trade agreements, praising "the greatest expansion of international trade in history."

HEADLINE: One Mother's Ordeal With Life on Welfare

BYLINE: By DIRK JOHNSON 

DATELINE: COAL VALLEY, Ill., July 26

 BODY:
In her rusting house trailer on a patch of thistles and stones in the Mississippi River valley, Manda Seefield clearly feels embarrassment about her plight: a welfare recipient for most of the last six years. But as her words tumble out, they are tinged with anger. She knows that many Americans, including some in Congress, believe that all a welfare beneficiary needs is a stronger backbone, and maybe a swift kick in the pants.
"They think we have this easy life, eating candies and watching TV all day, without a care in the world," said Ms. Seefield, 26, who collects $365 a month in aid. "They ought to come down to my level once. See what it's like to take care of kids on this kind of money. See what it's like to run into obstacles everywhere you turn." 
 When Congress considers historic changes in welfare, condemning a culture of dependency, it is aiming squarely at people like Manda Seefield. Ms. Seefield was a child of welfare; her mother held jobs only sporadically. Like many long-term welfare recipients, Ms. Seefield became pregnant as a teen-ager.
Her story provides fodder for both sides of the welfare debate: on the one hand she is healthy and cannot seem to find work despite a fairly robust economy. On the other hand she has applied in vain for many jobs and has trouble finding child care.
If the welfare bill and its five-year limit on receiving aid becomes law, women like Ms. Seefield could face the prospect of an abrupt halt in welfare payments.
A closer look at Ms. Seefield's life reveals a welter of complications that make an escape from the rolls far more difficult than it might seem. "It's not something to be proud of," she said quietly, after shooing her two small children out of the room. "I try my best. And then something happens. It's really hard to get off welfare."
With no diploma, no special skill and no support from the father of her two small children, Ms. Seefield has scant options in the job market.
She is unwilling to work the night shift, because child care during the late hours is extremely hard to find, or very expensive. The minimum-wage jobs like flipping burgers and making beds, which she had done as a teen-ager, usually do not pay health insurance, a necessity for the mother of small children who have health problems.
Besides all that, even lower-paying jobs are beginning to demand more sophisticated levels of skills, especially when it comes to computers. Filling out a job application at an office supply store recently, Ms. Seefield was overwhelmed by the number of technical questions.
"Half of the computer stuff," she said, with a note of defeat, "I never even heard of."
About 70 percent of aid recipients leave the rolls within two years, but about half come back, according to Michael Kharfen, a spokesman for the United States Department of Health and Human Services. Nationally, the average cumulative time spent on welfare is about six years.
Like many welfare recipients, Ms. Seefield herself grew up in poverty. Her mother was an alcoholic who sometimes worked as the manager of a topless bar. As a child of 10 or 11, Ms. Seefield said, she would be left alone to fend for herself for a week or more at a time.
She dropped out of Rock Island High School during her senior year, and worked at a couple of fast food restaurants. She also worked minimum-wage jobs as a hotel maid, discount store clerk and gas station cashier.
At 19, she became pregnant. At 22, she had her second child. She has never married.
She was going to school last year to get her high school equivalency diploma, but stopped around Christmas as the bills mounted. She took a job at Mother Hubbard's Cupboard, a convenience store at a gas station, where she earned $4.90 an hour.
But earlier this month, she was dismissed. She said a supervisor became unhappy with her for "covering for this other girl."
After losing the job, she said she has applied for work at an office supply store, a hotel, a department store and a gas station, among other places. But she is not optimistic about her chances.
It cannot help, Ms. Seefield acknowledged, to have an application form that shows a spotty work history.
"They don't want you unless you have experience," she said. "But how do you get experience, unless somebody is going to give you a job?"
In some cases, she said, it is financially better to take welfare than to work, considering the cost of child care and insurance. "I don't know what I'd do without the medical card," she said.
Her 6-year-old son, Christopher, takes medication for being hyperactive. He was held back in kindergarten. Her 4-year-old daughter, Ashlie, has severe asthma.
If she had things to do over again, Ms. Seefield said, she would never have dropped out of high school. And she would have realized she was too young to have babies.
But she had become smitten with a teen-age boy, also a high school dropout, that she had met while "cruising the avenue" in nearby Moline.
"I was madly, passionately, head-over-heels in love," she said.
They had talked about marrying, but he always had second thoughts. He also had plenty of other girlfriends. He left when she became pregnant with Ashlie.
Ms. Seefield said the man has "six or seven children by different women." While she was working at the gas station, he happened to stop there one day. They talked for about 15 minutes, and she showed him pictures of Christopher and Ashlie.
She now has a folder with statements from the Illinois Department of Public Aid, which keeps track of the child support he owes. As of May, the bill had climbed past $22,000.
"We have chased him and chased him and chased him," Ms. Seefield said. "And every time we catch him, he quits his job and moves to another state."
She said Christopher used to ask why his father did not love him.
"That's a very hard question to answer to a little boy," she said. "I tell him that he's just been very busy, and that maybe he'll have time for him later."
Always living on the lip of financial catastrophe, Ms. Seefield said, she must practice a sort of triage, focusing on whatever is urgent and knowing which bills can be put off.
With school starting in a month, Ms. Seefield is worried about money she will need for books, supplies and clothes. Despite the financial straits, she said she never talks about money with the children. "I want them to think everything is hunky-dory," she said. "Worrying is my job."
These days, she has been seeing a man, and he bought a washer-dryer and a television for the family. They do not have a telephone or cable. With the help of a friend's father, she bought the 1978 trailer last year for $1,000. She still must pay $140 a month rent for the trailer lot.
"I'll admit, it's very hard to get off aid," she said. "It's very hard. You have to find a pretty good job. And I'd like to know, where are they?"
She sometimes thinks about what she would do if the welfare checks ended for good. "All I know, is that I'm going to keep a roof over my children's head, and I am going to get them clothes and food," she said.
"I'll find a way," she added. "I just don't know what it is right now."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Manda Seefield, 26, collects $365 a month in welfare benefits to support herself and her daughter, Ashlie, 4, and son, Christopher, 6. Speaking of the difficulties of getting off public assistance, she said: "I try my best. And then something happens. It's really hard to get off welfare." (Warren Paris for The New York Times)

LENGTH: 221 words

HEADLINE: Cross-Border Deals Are Down This Year

BYLINE: By Reuters 

 BODY:
Cross-border merger and acquisition activity slipped in the first half of 1996 from last year's record levels, according to a study released yesterday by KPMG Peat Marwick.
But investors still put plenty of money into mature economies like the United States and Britain, while retreating somewhat from emerging markets like China. 
 "We're seeing a flight to safety," said Steve Blum, corporate finance partner at KPMG, which defines cross-border deals as those in which the buyer and seller are based in different countries.
KPMG said 2,707 cross-border acquisitions, joint ventures and minority investments, with a total value of $110.5 billion, were announced in the first six months of 1996. That marked a 12 percent decline in the number of cross-border deals and a 6 percent spending drop from record levels of 1995.
The United States still attracted the most investment, with 397 deals, though activity was down 9 percent from levels a year earlier. The total value of the United States deals slipped 11 percent, to $35.4 billion.
KPMG said American activity abroad also fell, especially in China and France in the entertainment and communications industries. The number of cross-border purchases by United States companies dipped 2 percent, to 722, and the value fell 18 percent, to $22.3 billion.
